==PAGE:7==
Abstract
It is estimated that 4% of men and 2% of women in Denmark, above the age of
45, suffer from obstructive sleep apnea (OSA) syndrome. OSA is a sleep disorder
with short- and long-term consequences, which include sleep-related issues, so-
cial implications, and cardiovascular diseases. OSA is diagnosed in an overnight
sleep laboratory with a polysomnogram (PSG). Monitoring can be expensive and
time-consuming. Treatment for OSA is usually performed with Continuous Pos-
itive Airway Pressure (CPAP), which helps the subjects with breathing during
the night. At least 50% of all OSA patients are undiagnosed. In this pilot study,
a novel OSA classification and an in-ear multimodal prototype system is intro-
duced, which allows patients to be taken from the hospitals to the private-sphere
to decrease the burden on the healthcare system. Sixty features were derived
from photoplethysmography (PPG), arterial blood oxygenation (SpO2), abdomi-
nal accelerometer and heart rate (HR) from 186 patients, used as input for three
different machine learning (ML) methods.
Random Forest (RF) achieved the
highest results with an accuracy, sensitivity, and specificity of 100%, 97%, and
100% respectively. The results prove that new, easy-to-use, low-cost wearable
sensors could provide valuable OSA screenings.
==PAGE:8==
==PAGE:9==
Resume
Det anslås, at 4% af mænd og 2% af kvinder i Danmark over 45 år, lider af
obstruktiv søvnapnø (OSA) syndrom.
OSA er en søvnforstyrrelse med både
kort- og langsigtede konsekvenser, som omfatter søvnrelaterede problemer, so-
ciale konsekvenser og hjerte-kar-sygdomme. Mindst 50% af alle OSA-patienter
er udiagnosticerede. OSA diagnosticeres i et overnatningslaboratorium med et
polysomnogram (PSG). Overvågning kan være dyr og tidskrævende.
Behan-
dling for OSA udføres normalt med kontinuerlig positiv luftvejstryk (CPAP),
som hjælper patienterne med at trække vejret om natten. I denne pilotunder-
søgelse introduceres en ny OSA-klassifikation og et in-ear multimodalt proto-
typesystem, som gør det muligt for patienter at blive behandlet i eget hjem frem
for på hospitalet, hvilket vil nedsætte byrden på sundhedssystemet. Tres signal
karakteristiske udregninger fra photoplethysmografi (PPG), arteriel iltmætning,
abdominal acceleration og hjertefrekvens fra 186 patienter, som bruges som in-
put til tre forskellige machine learning (ML) metoder.
Random Forest (RF)
opnåede de højeste resultater med en nøjagtighed, sensitivitet og specificitet på
henholdsvis 100%, 97% og 100%. Resultaterne viser, at nye, let-anvendelige og
billige bærbare sensorer kan bidrage til værdifulde screeninger af OSA.

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
1.1
Problem Statement
Continuous vital signs monitoring is of vital importance because the general pub-
lic is getting older and with increasing prevalence of developing chronic diseases.
The World Health Organization predicts the importance of chronic cardiovascu-
lar diseases as a primary cause of death in the coming 20 years to be increasing
[1], which cause a more significant burden for the taxpayers. Therefore, having
a readily available and inexpensive device, for use in the comfort of a home envi-
ronment, is desirable for the broader public.
Suffering from obstructive sleep apnea have in recent years shown to increase the
likelihood of developing several cardiovascular diseases. Moreover, general sleep
deprivation can cause several unwanted consequences, such as accidents and re-
duced quality of life. Studies also suggest, that long-term oxygen deprivation
during sleep might lead to mental health issues, hence early detection and treat-
ment is crucial.
The diagnosis based on the current golden standard for detection of obstruc-
tive sleep apnea is cumbersome and expensive because it relies on extensive
polysomnography readings, which has to be reviewed by a trained professional.
The diagnostic methods are based on guidelines given by the American Academy
of Sleep Medicine (AASM). This imposes several practical and financial limita-
tions when screening the general public. Therefore, most patients are undiag-
nosed.
Developing an easy to use wear-and-forget disease monitoring system, using in-
ear sensors, can provide a comfortable and high-quality signal acquisition as an
inexpensive screening tool for obstructive sleep apnea.
==PAGE:16==
2
1 Introduction
1.2
Problem Aims
This thesis aims to develop a continuous wearable smart monitoring system for
detection of obstructive sleep apnea. The system consists of an optical in-ear
sensor combined with a chest accelerometer, which strives to take patients from
the hospitals to the private-sphere, allowing for a significant reduction in costs.
Data acquisition, biomedical signal processing, and signal validation are per-
formed using the In-Ear Sleep Recorder prototype system. In order to detect
and classify sleep apnea, patient data must be gathered. Due to regulatory bu-
reaucratic processes and time limitation, obstructive sleep apnea data is acquired
through an online database, instead of the prototype created for this study. How-
ever, data and features extracted from the database are directly comparable to
those obtained by the In-Ear Sleep Recorder.
The software is developed and
processed in MATLAB™, except the firmware code, which is developed in C++.
In the list below, the general objectives of the thesis are presented.
• Design prototype for testing
• Acquire knowledge of signals and processing methods
• Develop vital signs estimation algorithms
– Statistical comparison with hospital equipment
• Investigate shutdown algorithm and signal quality index design
• Develop machine learning algorithms for classification of OSA
• Evaluate system performance in terms of:
– Optimal classification algorithm
– Accuracy, sensitivity, specificity and more
– Number and duration of OSA events
==PAGE:17==
CHAPTER 2
Preliminaries
This chapter provides the background knowledge required to design a system
based on photoplethysmography, the principles of pulse oximetry and which fac-
tors to include when building a system to detect obstructive sleep apnea.
2.1
Oxygen Transport
Oxygen is vital to the functioning of each cell in the human body. In the ab-
sence of oxygen for a prolonged amount of time, cells will die. Thus, oxygen
delivery to cells is an essential indicator of a patient’s health. Pulse oximetry is
a conventional, noninvasive method used in clinical environments developed to
analyze oxygen delivery. Oxygen delivery to cells requires the use of the respi-
ratory system as well as the circulatory system. Ventilation is the initial step,
moving air into the lungs. The oxygenated blood circulates the body until it
reaches oxygen-depleted areas, where oxygen diffuses to cells, and carbon dioxide
is transferred to the blood returning to the lungs. Neurons in the brain stem
control the ventilatory process. The circulatory system can modulate cardiac
output to affect the oxygen delivery.
The process of ventilation provides a continuous supply of oxygen to the lungs.
After oxygenated blood has circulated through the body, it is brought back to the
lungs through arterial capillaries to exchange gases, receive oxygen and ridding
itself of carbon dioxide. Blood is re-oxygenated and recirculated through the
body. Gas exchange occurs through the process of diffusion. Diffusion is the
net movement of particles from an area of high partial pressure to a region of
relatively lower partial pressure through a process of random motion. The actual
gas exchange of the blood takes place in the alveoli, seen in figure 2.1 [2].
==PAGE:18==
4
2 Preliminaries
Figure 2.1: Respiratory tract including a zoomed section of the alveoli in the
lower respiratory tract [3].
Large pulmonary capillary beds surround the alveoli. Air in the alveoli has a
higher partial pressure of oxygen and a lower partial pressure of carbon dioxide
than the aortic blood. The pressure gradient causes diffusion to occur. The net
movement of carbon dioxide will be towards the alveoli and the net movement
of oxygen towards the blood, seen in figure 2.2. The blood then returns to the
heart via a pulmonary venule to be pumped out to the rest of the body [2].
Figure 2.2: Oxygen transport, showing the exchange of CO2 and O2 between
the alveoli and pulmonary capillaries [4].
Gases are not particularly soluble in blood, which is composed mostly of water.
Thus, for effective oxygen transport, a secondary method of transportation is
required. The compound hemoglobin provides a binding mechanism that allows
oxygen to be transported through the blood. Hemoglobin plays an essential role
in supplying the necessary amount of oxygen to the body.
Hemoglobin is a respiratory pigment contained within red blood cells. One red
blood cell contains approximately 265 million molecules of hemoglobin. Hemoglobin
==PAGE:19==
2.1 Oxygen Transport
5
is composed of heme and globin units, connected by polypeptide chains. One
hemoglobin molecule contains four heme and four globin units, each heme and
globin unit can carry one molecule of oxygen; hence, one hemoglobin molecule
can carry four oxygen molecules.
Hemoglobin changes color when oxygenated. When hemoglobin is oxygenated, it
is bright red, while dark red when deoxygenated. This color change is used in
the application of pulse oximetry to measure hemoglobin oxygen saturation.
Figure 2.3: Oxyhemoglobin dissociation curve, shows the relationship between
the partial pressure of oxygen in the blood and the percentage of oxygen bound
to hemoglobin. Left-shift caused by lower CO2, higher pH and lower temperature,
and vice versa [5].
The oxyhemoglobin dissociation curve is the relationship between the partial pres-
sure of oxygen in the blood and the percentage of oxygen bound to hemoglobin
compared to the maximum, seen in figure 2.3. Factors such as decreasing carbon
dioxide concentration, increasing pH, and decreasing temperature will shift the
curve toward the left. A left-shifted curve implies that the hemoglobin molecules
will be more saturated at a lower partial pressure of oxygen. When a completely
deoxygenated hemoglobin molecule combines with one oxygen molecule, it has
a higher affinity for the second oxygen molecule.
This is true for each addi-
tional oxygen molecule. The change in affinity also applies to the reverse process.
When the first oxygen molecule is released from the hemoglobin molecule, it is
more likely to release the second oxygen molecule, hence, the dissociation curve
is a sigmoid [2].
==PAGE:20==
6
2 Preliminaries
2.2
Ear Anatomy
To perform vital signs estimations using in-ear sensors, understanding the anatomy
and blood supply of the ear canal is significant. By the use of photoplethysmog-
raphy, which measures the amplitude of reflected light at different frequencies, it
is possible to estimate oxygen saturation and heart rate from the reflection of the
ear canal and thereby the underlying blood vessels. In-depth understanding will
furthermore assist in determining the placement of the sensor inside the ear canal.
The ear can be divided into the external, middle and inner ear. The external ear
consists of the auricle, or the pinna and the external auditory canal. The pinna is
the fleshy part of the external ear, primarily composed of elastic cartilage covered
with skin, seen on figure 2.4a. The external ear canal is lined with hairs and
ceruminous glands which produce cerumen, more commonly known as earwax.
The tympanic membrane is a thin, semitransparent, nearly oval membrane that
separates the external and middle ear. Medial to the tympanic membrane is the
air-filled cavity of the middle ear. The two covered openings, the round, and
oval windows separate the middle ear from the inner ear. The auditory tube
opens into the pharynx and equalizes air pressure between the outside air and
the middle ear cavity. The inner ear consists of tunnels and chambers within
the temporal bone, called the bony labyrinth. The bony labyrinth is divided into
three regions: cochlea, vestibule, and semicircular canals [6]. The ear has an
abundant blood supply from the posterior auricular artery and small auricular
branches of the superficial temporal artery, seen in figure 2.4b [7].
(a) Anatomy of the human ear, displaying
the external, middle and inner ear [6].
(b) Arteries for blood supply of the ear [7].
Figure 2.4: Anatomy and blood supply of the ear.
==PAGE:21==
2.3 Photoplethysmography
7
The skin lining and substructure is different in the various parts of the ear canal.
Closest to the tympanic membrane is the bony ear canal, which has a very thin
and fragile layer of skin. Especially the inner part of the bony ear canal is very
exposed to rupture, inflammation, and thermal irritation. Furthermore, the layer
has limited substructure and therefore small blood supply. There is little move-
ment in this part of the canal [7].
As illustrated in figure 2.5a, the cartilaginous part of the ear canal has a thicker
layer of skin for protection, hair, as well as different subcutaneous anatomy than
the bony part. The cartilaginous section offers a thick layer of soft tissue which
both provides many blood vessels as well as flexibility. This tissue allows for com-
fortable displacement within the canal when placing and wearing in-ear devices.
Additionally, the thicker layer of skin provides much better protection against
injuries, inflammation, and general irritation. Illustration of the skin and sub-
cutaneous layers of the tissue can be seen in figure 2.5b. Due to the soft tissue,
placement within this part of the ear canal is much more susceptible to movement
and artifacts caused by motion, especially jaw and facial movements.
(a) Skin thickness and substructure of the
cartilaginous and bony parts of the ear [7].
(b) Substructure of body tissue [7].
Figure 2.5: Skin and substructure of the different body tissue.
Based on this, optimal placement of in-ear devices is generally deemed to be the
cartilaginous part, being superior regarding wearer comfortability and underlying
structure. However, one has to keep in mind the movement artifacts this might
produce.
2.3
Photoplethysmography
Photoplethysmography is a low-cost optical technique used to detect blood vol-
ume changes in a microvascular bed of tissue. Because PPG can be measured
==PAGE:22==
8
2 Preliminaries
non-invasively on the skin surface, readings are easily accessible, making it widely
used in hospital practices. The technique has made a resurgence of interest es-
pecially in the realm of portable technologies such as medical equipment and
training equipment, i.e., pulse watches. The PPG waveform consists of a pul-
satile (AC) physiological waveform attributed to synchronous cardiac changes in
the blood volume with each heartbeat. This AC component is superimposed on
a slowly varying (DC) baseline, containing various lower frequency components
associated with respiration, sympathetic nervous system activity, and thermoreg-
ulation. The AC component typically has a frequency of 1 Hz, depending on the
heart rate, seen in figure 2.6. The contribution of each component in the PPG
signal is visualized in figure 2.9 [8].
Figure 2.6: The pulsatile AC component of the PPG signal and corresponding
ECG. The AC component is actually superimposed on a much larger quasi-DC
component that relates to the tissues and the average blood volume within the
sample [8].
Most pulse oximeters are based on the method of PPG. However, the accuracy of
the PPG suffers from motion artifacts, and the patient must have adequate blood
perfusion near the placement of the pulse oximeter probe. Signal processing can
derive heart rate from the PPG waveform. Temporal lowpass filtering reduces
the effect of motion artifacts on heart rate estimation [2].
2.4
Pulse Oximetry
A pulse oximeter estimates oxygen saturation of arterial blood transcutaneously
and provides vital information about the cardiorespiratory function. Pulse oxime-
try gives an empirical measure of arterial saturation. However, with state of the
art equipment and proper initial calibration, the correlation between the pulse
oximeter measurement, SpO2, and the actual arterial blood oxygen saturation,
==PAGE:23==
2.4 Pulse Oximetry
9
SaO2 is adequate, with a 3% discrepancy of less than generally. Research of pulse
oximetry has shown that any reasonably small loss in accuracy is outweighed by
the advantages of non-invasiveness and continuous, immediate availability of data
[2].
The principle of a pulse oximeter is based on the light emission through a tissue
bed such as the finger or earlobe. The light absorbance of oxygenated and de-
oxygenated hemoglobin at the two wavelengths are different, seen in figure 2.7.
Oxygenated hemoglobin absorbs more infrared light and allows more red light
to pass through, whereas, deoxygenated hemoglobin absorbs more red light and
allows more infrared light to pass through. The wavelength light band of red and
infrared light is 620-750 nm and 700-1000 nm, respectively [9].
Figure 2.7:
Optical exticintion spectra of oxygenated and deoxygenated
hemoglobin. The y-axis is the extinction coefficient on a log-scale [9].
By comparing the optical extinction at the indicated wavelengths, red and in-
frared, a pulse oximeter can distinguish oxygenated from deoxygenated hemoglobin.
Hemoglobin is the primary light absorber in human blood at wavelengths used
in pulse oximetry. The absorption characteristics of hemoglobin change with its
chemical binding and the wavelength of the incident light. Although oxygenated
and reduced hemoglobin absorbs most of the light passing through blood, they do
not represent the only hemoglobin species present in human blood. Hemoglobin
may combine with other substances such as carbon monoxide or hydrogen sulfide.
Binding oxygen in the pulmonary capillaries and releasing it in the systemic cir-
culation is the primary purpose of hemoglobin. Hemoglobin is fully saturated
when four oxygen molecules are bound, called oxyhemoglobin (HbO2). If not
fully saturated with oxygen, it is called reduced hemoglobin (Hb). Oxygen satu-
ration is measured in percentage and determined by the amount of oxygenated
==PAGE:24==
10
2 Preliminaries
hemoglobin compared to the sum of oxygenated and reduced hemoglobin, written
as
SpO2 =
HbO2
Hb + HbO2
× 100
(2.1)
The extinction coefficients of both hemoglobin species are equal at the isosbestic
point (805 nm). The Hb is more transparent to light from the infrared region
than HbO2, hence the drop in intensity [2].
There are two different techniques for measuring the light emission of the LED’s,
reflective and transmissive mode. In the transmission mode, the LED’s and the
photodetector is at opposite sites of each other, with a given measuring site in-
between, such as a finger. In contrast, reflective mode works by having the LED’s
and the photodetector next to each other, in this case, the sensor detects the light
that is reflected. This can be seen in figure 2.8.
Figure 2.8: Light-emitting diode (LED) and photodetector (PD) placement for
transmission- and reflectrance-mode PPG, respectively [10].
Since most conventional systems work in transmission mode, they can only be
applied to thin, peripheral parts of the body (e.g., fingertip or earlobe). These
systems are strongly affected by reduced peripheral perfusion. For example, due
to cardiovascular disease, the peripheral microcirculation (e.g., fingers, ear lobes,
and toes) can become disturbed. In case of life-threatening situations (e.g., sepsis,
hypothermia, and shock), blood is usually centralized away from the limbs to
maintain the perfusion of vital organs such as the heart, lungs, or brain. Hence,
in situations when monitoring of vital signs is crucial, reduced perfusion may
inhibit pulse oximetric measurement at the limbs. For these reasons, it is useful
to apply pulse oximetry at places which are not affected by centralization. The
inner ear canal seems to be an ideal measurement site due to its proximity to the
brain. The inner ear canal is also less disrupted by motion artifact and provides
high wearing comfort [11][12].
==PAGE:25==
2.4 Pulse Oximetry
11
2.4.1
Light absorbance in pulse oximetry
Light traveling through biological tissue, such as the finger or ear, is absorbed by
different absorbing substances, seen in figure 2.9. The primary absorbers of light
are skin pigmentation, bones and the arterial and venous blood. Pulse oximeters
take advantage of arterial pulsation in order to transform the PPG signal to a
meaningful measurement such as heart rate or oxygen saturation.
Figure 2.9:
Variation in light attenuation in different tissues and the corre-
sponding PPG waveform [10].
The arteries contain more blood during systole than during diastole, this increases
the pressure and thereby also the diameter of the arteries. This effect only occurs
in the arteries and arterioles, not in the more flexible veins. The absorbance of
light in tissues with arteries increases during systole because of the larger amount
of absorbing tissue (hemoglobin), since the optical path length increases. This
alternating part of the total absorbance makes it possible to differentiate between
the absorbance due to venous blood, a constant amount of arterial blood, and
other non-pulsatile components such as skin pigmentation (DC component) and
the absorbance due to the pulsatile components of the arterial blood (AC compo-
nent), seen in figure 2.9. The alternating part of the light absorbed by the living
tissue usually does not exceed 1% to 2% of the constant absorbance of the DC
components. The time varying signal of transmitted light is referred to as the
PPG signal [2].
The pulse oximeter reading, SpO2, is an estimation of the arterial oxygen satu-
==PAGE:26==
12
2 Preliminaries
ration SaO2. When measuring at two wavelengths, it is only possible to distin-
guish the concentrations of two different absorbers (Hb and HbO2). In humans,
many species of hemoglobin are present, such as carboxyhemoglobin and methe-
moglobin. This means that the number of wavelengths should equal the number
of species of hemoglobin to determine the concentrations of Hb and HbO2 cor-
rectly. Because Hb and HbO2 are the main absorbers, the error may be small.
Nevertheless, the estimation of oxygen saturation from the arterial blood is not
exact.
2.5
Beer-Lambert Law
Beer’s law is used to accurately determine the oxygen saturation of hemoglobin
solution, which does not apply for whole blood because of the scattering effects.
Nevertheless, this model helps to develop an understanding of the absorbance of
light as it passes through living tissue.
Beer’s law describes the attenuation of light traveling through a uniform medium
containing an absorbing substance, in this case, hemoglobin.
When incident
light I0 enters a given medium, a part of the light is transmitted through the
medium while another part is absorbed. The light intensity I traveling through
the medium decreases exponentially with distance
I = I0e−ϵ(λ)cd
(2.2)
where ϵ(λ) is the absorptivity of the absorbing substance at a specific wavelength,
c is the concentration of the absorbing substance which is constant in the given
medium, and d is the optical path length through the medium. Beer’s law is
based on the principle that the sum of transmitted and absorbed light equals
the incident light. It does not account for physical processes which include the
reflection of the light at the surface of the medium nor scattering of light in the
medium.
The transmittance T of light traveling through a medium with an absorbing
substance is defined as the ratio of transmitted light I to the incident light I0
T = I
I0
= e−ϵ(λ)cd
(2.3)
The properties of Beer’s law are valid even if more than one substance absorbs
light in the medium. Each absorber contributes its part to the total absorbance.
Mathematically this system is represented merely by superposition of the indi-
vidual absorbing process [2].
==PAGE:27==
2.5 Beer-Lambert Law
13
The arterial oxygen saturation can be derived based on Beer’s law as a function of
the ratio of absorbances at two wavelengths. Due to nonlinearities in the LEDs,
the photodetector, and light absorbance in the tissue, the absorbances have to
be normalized in the ratio. This model results in a theoretical calibration curve,
which does not apply in practice.
The light intensities measured at the red and infrared wavelength have to be
normalized for them to be comparable since the LED’s might emit light with
different intensities. Furthermore, the absorbing characteristics of the DC com-
ponents at the two different wavelength and the sensitivity of the photodetector
differ from each other, as well as the tissue absorption and path length varies
widely from patient to patient and the path site. The normalized signal is calcu-
lated by dividing the raw signal by the maximum peaks in each point, for the red
and infrared wavelength respectively. This results in normalized signals which
have the same intensities during diastole, which achieves a normalized signal at
the two different wavelengths which are independent of the incident light (raw
signal) caused by the pulsation of blood in the arteries and can be compared with
each other, as shown in figure 2.10 [2].
Figure 2.10: Raw and normalized PPG signal from infrared and red LEDs,
respectively [2].
The result of the normalized signal can be formulated as
In = I
IH
= e−[ϵHb(λ)cHb+ϵHbO2(λ)cHbO2]∆d
(2.4)
where IH is the minimum light intensity (during diastole) and IL is the maxi-
mum light intensity, seen as valleys (IH) and peaks (IL) in figure 2.9 and 2.10
respectively.
==PAGE:28==
14
2 Preliminaries
The absorbance of the light is derived by calculating the natural logarithm of the
ratio between the pulsatile and non-pulsatile components in the light signal for
each of the wavelengths. The R-ratio between the components of each wavelength
is given as
R =
ln(IL,R/IH,R)
ln(IL,IR/IH,IR)
(2.5)
Combining the above equation with equation (2.4) the ratio can be derived as
R =
[ϵHb(λR)cHb + ϵHbO2(λR)cHbO2]∆dR
[ϵHb(λIR)cHb + ϵHbO2(λIR)cHbO2]∆dIR
(2.6)
Because each extinction coefficient (ϵ) is a constant at a specific wavelength of
light, the relation can be used to calculate SpO2 directly. However, in practice,
the R-values are mapped using empirical calibration. This is due to ϵ values not
taking light scattering into account, shown in figure 2.11.
Figure 2.11: Calibration curves for pulse oximeters. The solid line is the the-
oretical curve by Beer’s law and the dashed line is the empirical curve.
The
difference between these curves is mainly due to light scattering effects [2].
An alternative and very useful formula to equation (2.5) is the peak valley method
also known as the ratio of ratios
R =
ln( RL
RH )
ln( IRL
IRH ) =
ACR/DCR
ACIR/DCIR
(2.7)
where RH, RL, IRL, IRH is the minimum and maximum emergent light intensi-
ties of red and infrared light respectively, which is equal to the ratio expressed
in terms of the AC and DC components.

2.6 Heart Rate Estimation from PPG Signal

seen in the figure above, the standard formula used in most literature is given as
SpO2 = 110 −25 × R
(2.8)
2.6
Heart Rate Estimation from PPG Signal
Heart rate can easily be detected from PPG signals because it provides accurate
interpulse intervals. The literature contains a lot of different approaches on how
to make a robust HR estimation under different conditions.
Motion artifacts
are a significant problem when estimating heart rate from PPG signals.
Das
et al.
propose a straightforward signal processing scheme which accounts for
noisy PPG signals due to motion artifacts while being robust [13].
The HR
detection algorithm performs by applying the autocorrelation to a small data
frame (window) of 2 seconds. The autocorrelation function (ACF) is defined as
y(m) =
N
∑
n=1
X(n)X(n + m)
(2.9)
where N is the total number of samples in the window and m varies from zero to
(N-1). The prominent peak at lag zero corresponds to the signal compared with
itself and should be disregarded in peak detection. The distance (lags/samples)
to the second peak is used to calculate the heart rate, given by
HR = 60 × Fs
Ns
(2.10)
where Fs is the sampling frequency, and Ns is the index (lag) point corresponding
to the peak location. The minimum and maximum heart rate to be detected is set
to be between 50 bpm and 140 bpm since regular human heart rate is expected
to be within this range during overnight recordings. The maximum (Nmax) and
minimum (Nmin) sampling index are calculated from the equation of heartbeat
rate as, Nmin = 60×F s
120
and Nmin = 60×F s
50
[13]. This will be explored later and
can be seen in figure 7.6 in practice.
2.7
Accelerometer
Accelerometers have become a popular sensor unit which is inexpensive, prac-
tical and have many functions. Newer technology such as smartphones, sports
equipment, virtual reality and medical devices integrate this as a sensor unit. Ac-
celerometers in medical devices are primarily used to detect motion, posture or
==PAGE:30==
16
2 Preliminaries
other physical measures. As the name suggests, accelerometers measure accelera-
tion which is caused by motion. In physics, acceleration is defined as the rate of
change of velocity over time. In a single dimension, this would mean the rate at
which an object speeds up or slows down. If the acceleration is zero, this implies
that the object has a constant velocity. However, even if the object is standing
still, accelerometers still sense external forces which are applied, such as gravity.
This implies the following:
• Positive values indicate an increase in velocity
• Negative values indicate a decrease in velocity
• Positive values indicate a constant velocity (which can be different from
zero)
In practice, accelerometers are built as Micro-Electro-Mechanical Systems (MEMS).
These units have both stationary and movable capacitance plates in multiple di-
rections. When movement occurs, the movable capacitance plate will move in
either direction, causing a change in voltage as a result of the shift in distance
between the capacitance plates. The architecture of a MEMS chip can be seen
in figure 2.12.
Figure 2.12: Archicture of a Micro Electro-Mechanical System, converting ac-
celerations into voltage by stationary and moveable capacitance plates [14].
The voltage and its fluctuations are representative of the movement and accel-
eration happening outside the sensor, and the voltage readouts from the sensor
can be used to estimate accelerations and movements reversely. In a tri-axial
accelerometer, accelerations can be calculated by the three principal axes given
as X, Y and Z, seen in figure 2.13.
==PAGE:31==
2.7 Accelerometer
17
Figure 2.13: Axes and orientations sensed by a tri-axial accelerometer [15].
Many MEMS are capable of detecting angular speed around the principal axes,
which is known as a gyroscope. The concept of the gyroscope is illustrated below
in figure 2.14.
Figure 2.14: Angular speed sensed by a tri-axial accelerometer [15].
In this project, an accelerometer is incorporated into the system. By using a single
tri-axial accelerometer, it is possible to determine many physiological parameters
and estimations. Several computations, such as respiratory rate, movement sens-
ing, noise estimation, adaptive noise filtering, position tracking and more can be
estimated.
2.7.1
Accelerometer estimations
Precise estimation of respiratory rate can prove to be hard to perform because
of the limitation posed by many modern respiratory measurements. In a clinical
setting, estimates are performed using flow measurements, such as a nasal can-
nula, or pneumographic measurements, such as respiratory chest bands, which
can cause discomfort to the patients. However, less obtrusive sensors and system
are often more prone to noise [16].
In recent years, many different methods have been developed to estimate res-
piratory rate from sampled signals such as ECG, PPG or wearable MEMS ac-
celerometers. However, even though respiration rate using PPG signal is feasible,
==PAGE:32==
18
2 Preliminaries
it requires a large window size to decrease respiration rate (RR) estimation errors
adequately. Moreover, signals that contain information about various physiologi-
cal measures might be hard to separate without causing artifacts, as they overlap
in time and frequency space.
In this study, both accelerometer and PPG data are available to perform respi-
ration rate estimations. Based on current research, the best results in term of
algorithm complexity and estimation error rely on MEMS accelerometers, espe-
cially if placed in the abdominal or torso region where the physiological changes
(chest and abdominal expansion) during breathing have the highest amplitude.
The study by Guul et al. showed that a chest-based accelerometer (smartphone),
can be used to estimate respiratory rate as well as sleep position reliably [17].
Due to the time restraints posed on this project, accelerometer data will primarily
be used to estimate respiration frequency. Given more time, more estimations
algorithms such as sleep position and breath detection could be employed based
on the tri-axial accelerometer. Additionally, data can be beneficial regarding mo-
tion detection and noise cancellation.
==PAGE:33==
CHAPTER 3
Sleep Apnea
American Academy of Sleep Medicine provides guidelines for scoring sleep-related
events, and for classification of sleep disorders. The term sleep-disordered breath-
ing encompasses a range of disorders, of which most fall into the categories ob-
structive sleep apnea, central sleep apnea, or sleep-related hypoventilation. Due
to the high prevalence of OSA, evaluation of the affected population, with PSG,
will have a significant cost. Moreover, the availability of testing equipment and
sleep laboratories in some regions might be limited. Undiagnosed and untreated
OSA is a significant burden on the healthcare system. Innovation and optimiza-
tion can prove to have a vital impact on early and accurate diagnosis of this
common disorder [18].
In this section, the sleep disorder OSA will be investigated. A preliminary study
to this thesis was made. To discover the etiology, pathophysiology, classification,
prevalence, signs, symptoms, and polysomnography, refer to Appendix G: Perva-
sive Technology Study, Chapter 3.
Obstructive sleep apnea is identified if the amplitude (peak to trough) of the
airflow signal is flat or nearly flat.
This is noted when amplitude of airflow
decreases below at least 90% of the amplitude of “baseline” breathing, i.e., to
≤10% of the baseline for 90% of the event duration, (identified during a period
of regular breathing with stable oxygen levels), and if this change lasts for ≥10
seconds. When thermistry (airflow signal) is missing or uninterpretable, the nasal
pressure signal (cannula flow) can be used as an alternative signal for scoring
obstructive apnea [19]. This definition will be used in later chapters for machine
learning.
3.1
Discussion
As explained in Pervasive Technology Study, diagnosis of OSA is evaluated with
PSG. The suspected patients sleep in a clinical sleep laboratory in order to be
diagnosed. During the overnight sleep, patients are equipped with several sensors,
==PAGE:34==
20
3 Sleep Apnea
wires, and electrodes, several hours ahead of the actual sleep examination. The
typical sensors and recording setup can be seen in figure 3.1.
Figure 3.1: Typical sensor setup for overnight PSG recordings and diagnosis
performed on patients suspected of having OSA [20].
As a consequence, the quality of sleep is reduced, which might affect the signif-
icance of the examination. The recorded signal requires a trained physician to
analyze the data for a final diagnosis. Following this sleep analysis, a report is
created for the possible diagnosis of OSA.
Considering the disadvantages of the PSG setup, it is useful to develop unob-
trusive sensor systems which are suitable for homecare screening, taking the
particular requirements of home care into account. A small and wearable sleep-
monitoring system could potentially allow diagnostics and eventually even treat-
ment of OSA in a familiar environment. This would achieve high acceptance
among patients and would increase the reliability of the diagnosis since an im-
provement in the patients sleep quality is expected. By means of a pre-diagnosis
by nocturnal pulse oximetry, the amount of unnecessary PSG screenings can be
minimized, and hence, could significantly reduce costs of the healthcare system
[12]. The In-Ear Sleep Recorder is a prototype created with the aforementioned is-
sues in mind, trying to make an easy, cheap and comfortable system for diagnosis.
In figure 3.2 it is visible how PPG derived estimates such as oxygen saturation
and heart rate can be expected to behave during apnea events.
Cessation of
respiration can be observed in impedance pneumography (IP) at t = 10 s, followed
by a steady decrease in heart rate. As a result of the cessation of breathing, a
short time window will elapse, and subsequent drop in oxygen saturation can be
==PAGE:35==
3.1 Discussion
21
seen, eventually resulting in mild or severe hypoxemia, based on the duration of
the apnea. The decrease in oxygen saturation will trigger the sympathetic nervous
system leading to an increase in heart rate above the baseline level. PPG and IP
are given in arbitrary units (a.u.) [21].
Figure 3.2: Excerpt of synchronous SpO2, HR, PPG and IP tracings during an
apnoeic event from a neonatal subject. Cessation of respiration can be observed
in IP at t = 10 s, followed by a steady decrease in heart rate. As a result of the
cessation of breathing, a short time window will elapse, and subsequent drop in
oxygen saturation can be seen, eventually resulting in mild or severe hypoxemia,
based on the duration of the apnea. The drop in oxygen saturation will trigger
the sympathetic nervous system leading to an increase in heart rate above the
baseline level. Adapted from [21].
In addition to PPG signals, other signals also indicate changes associated with
cessation of breathing during OSA events. In figure 3.3, it is clear how changes
in airflow are reflected in thoracic and abdominal effort signals, measured with
respiratory belts as shown in figure 3.1. It is evident that movement by the chest
and abdominal regions almost cease to exist during apneas, and is, therefore, an
excellent indicator of how the patient is breathing.
==PAGE:36==
22
3 Sleep Apnea
Figure 3.3: Abdominal and thorax signals during upper airway collapse in a
patient with OSA recorded by PSG [22].
Implementing a system incorporating both a PPG sensor and the derived heart
rate and oxygen saturation, along with an accelerometer, placed at the abdominal
region, will, therefore, be able to provide a lot of information about sleep-related
and respiratory events.
OSA has become an area of focus within research during the last decade. This is
due to the short- and long-term consequences of OSA. In figure 3.4, short-term
consequences pertaining to apneas during sleep can be seen.
Figure 3.4: Flowchart indicating the short-term consequences of obstructive
sleep apnea [22].
During the recent years, there have been many studies investigating possible links
between OSA and chronic- or inflammatory diseases. Moreover, subjects with
untreated OSA have an increased risk of developing cardiovascular diseases, and
stroke. Long-term consequences of OSA might also include mental impairment
and decline due to long-standing nocturnal hypoxemia. Studies have shown that
OSA might lead to deficits in executive function, attention and memory [23]. In
==PAGE:37==
3.1 Discussion
23
a study performed by the authors Owens et al. the risk of developing cancer,
based on the diagnosis of OSA, was investigated. However, the conclusions on
the links are still vague, as a lot of the risk factors associated with developing
OSA is also associated with developing cancer, such as obesity [22].

CHAPTER 4
State of the Art
In this chapter, an overview of the leading research within the fields of hearables
and in-ear modalities (primarily PPG), accelerometer and sleep apnea studies are
presented. This review assesses the general level of development within each field,
to determine which methods and classification schemes have proven to yield the
best results. In order to find the relevant studies, many different keywords and
search engines have been used, seen in figure 4.1.
Search sites used: DTU Findit, Google, Google Scholar, ScienceDirect, Web
of Science, Journal of Neural Engineering, IEEE, Elsevier, and PubMed.
Figure 4.1: Search related keywords used in the state of the art study of relevant
research articles.
==PAGE:40==
26
4 State of the Art
4.1
Discussion
In this section, a discussion within each field of research is presented. Relevant
articles for hearables, accelerometer and apnea can be found in table 4.1, 4.2 and
4.3 respectively. In Appendix F is a thorough summary of the reviewed state of
the art.
4.1.1
Hearables and Photoplethysmography
There is a broad consensus that the human ear canal is a viable location for
monitoring vital signs using continuous biosensors. This location provides high
comfortability and access for multimodal sensors for robust recordings when com-
pared to PSG recordings. As concluded by Poh et al. 24/7 monitoring might
also have a social aspect to it, standing out using wearable health sensors is
not desired. The auditory canal provides a key location for inconspicuous sen-
sor placement which can even look like regular ear-buds. Furthermore, the ear
canal is not affected by peripheral vasoconstriction rendering PPG signals unus-
able. Enabling users to move their arms or legs without causing motion artifacts.
Moreover, frequent and noninvasive monitoring is critical for early detection of
diseases.
An overview of the reviewed articles can be found below in table 4.1. A summary
of each article can be found in Appendix F.1.

4.1.2
Accelerometer
The papers reviewed in this section, display the various features and estimations
which can be performed using a system which is entirely or partly based on ac-
celerometer data sensors.
Using an accelerometer, Sánchez Morillo et al. showed that it was possible to
extract respiratory information relating to apnea events with a 98% accuracy.
Several studies performed using accelerometer at the thorax or abdominal region
showed that respiratory rate and flow could be estimated using this single modal-
ity. Using the same technique, Dehkordi et al. showed the possibility to detect
abdominal effort with a mean error of 11%. Using an additional audio signal,
Guul et al. showed great potential in screening of OSA with an accuracy of 98%.
Furthermore, Gjoreski et al. and Sun et al. showed that accelerometer data for
assessment of activity recognition is possible.
Incorporating an accelerometer sensor will, therefore, provide additional valuable
information, which can be utilized in the diagnosis and assessment of OSA.

4.1.3
Sleep Apnea
The papers reviewed in this section, shows a great promise for detection of ob-
structive sleep apnea, using features completely or partly from modalities which
are identical to those proposed in this study.
Using SpO2 signals Almazaydeh et al. achieved an accuracy of 93% when per-
forming detection of apnea. Behar et al. and Uçar et al. achieved accuracies
of 92% and 98% respectively, when performing monitoring and classification of
OSA, using PPG signals. Furthermore, the remaining studies show great poten-
tial combining the proposed modalities with additional, such as ECG or audio
signals. PPG derived features such as heart rate or heart rate variability are
valuable for detection of OSA.
Developing a system including the proposed modalities, methods and algorithms
for detecting and classifying OSA, promises good results. An overview of the
reviewed articles can be found below in table 4.3. A summary of each article can

==PAGE:46==
32
4 State of the Art
4.2
Conclusion
In this study, the majority of articles reviewed focus on monitoring and detection
of obstructive sleep apnea and in-ear PPG measurements. Providing cost-efficient
and readily available screening systems for monitoring and classification of OSA
is essential for the diagnosis of suspected patients. Due to the practical and finan-
cial limitations of modern PSG recordings, many of the suspected OSA cases are
still currently undiagnosed. The combination of in-ear sensors and signal features
with high predictability power for OSA could further improve screening accuracy.
Ideally, OSA screening is performed with minimal displeasure to the suspected
patients, in the comfort of their own home, which could also provide better record-
ings. Developing such a system would, therefore, allow for cheap and accessible
screening for OSA worldwide.
==PAGE:47==
CHAPTER 5
In-Ear Sleep Recorder
The prototype created for this study was made in collaboration with Oticon A/S,
named In-Ear Sleep Recorder. Oticon produced the hardware and physical con-
struction of the device; however, the authors of this study participated in all the
decision making. Moreover, the software implementation and programming were
performed by the authors. The creation of the prototype has been an ongoing
process which started in September 2017 and finished at the start of April 2018.
Generally speaking, the hardware consists of two sensors, a microcontroller unit
(MCU), a hearing aid plus an empty shell and finally a battery, which will be
explored in the following section.
5.1
Hardware and Design
The prototype can be divided into two subsystems, the in-ear piece, and the 3D
printed box. The in-ear piece consists of the Oticon SmartRITE™hearing aid
device as an empty placeholder-shell. The hearing aid can be seen in figure 5.1.
In a regular hearing aid this contains a battery, however, for continuous overnight
monitoring, a larger battery is required. The shell functions only as an anchor,
to keep the piece safely in place inside the ear. At the end of the wire connected
to the shell is the Maxim MAX30101 PPG sensor, which is a reflective optical
sensor, with red, infrared and green LED’s. The sensor is glued onto the side of
a regular hearing aid-speaker, again, functioning as a placeholder.
==PAGE:48==
34
5 In-Ear Sleep Recorder
Figure 5.1: Oticon A/S SMARTRite™hearing aid device consisting of the main
unit placed behind the ear and the speaker placed inside the ear canal, silicone
dome for placement and comfort. Adapted from [47].
The speaker and sensor are placed inside an excavated earplug. The earplug was
a decent temporary solution in order to avoid too much noise contamination by
movement and generally lousy placement. The in-ear piece can be seen in figure
5.2.
Figure 5.2: Shell, sensor and in-ear piece made from memory foam substrate
used for placement in the external ear.
Goverdovsky et al. found that choosing the correct material for the earpiece was
critical for proper signals, the viscoelastic nature of the material in an earplug
exhibits good conformance to the shape of the ear canal, while also having a de-
sirable stress-strain curvature; however, the density of the viscoelastic material
also has a significant impact [28]. For this study, simple trial and error tests were
performed with varies earpieces to find the best performing earplug. The Maxim
MAX30101 sensor also includes a temperature gauge for monitoring, which is a
crucial signal for vital signs generally. However, it is not used in this project for
detection of OSA.
The 3D printed box, seen in figure 5.3, is approximately of 100 x 70 x 20 mm.
The exterior consists of a power switch and a switch for logging data onto the
SD card. It also has two programmable bottoms, which has no functions at the
moment but could be programmed to other needs required further on.

==PAGE:52==
38
5 In-Ear Sleep Recorder
5.2.1
PPG Sensor
The MAX30101 is a integrated pulse oximetry and heart-rate monitor module.
It includes internal LED’s, photodetectors, optical elements, and low-noise elec-
tronics with ambient light rejection. The MAX30101 provides a complete system
solution to ease the design process for mobile and wearable devices [48]. The
sensor is 5.6 x 3.3 x 1.55 mm in size, making it small enough to fit inside the ear
canal, and it has a high signal to noise ratio making it robust to motion artifacts.
The chip contains three respective LED’s, having the peak wavelengths 660 ± 10
nm for the red, 880 ± 10 nm for the infrared, and 530 ± 7 nm for the green LED.
Figure E.2 is a schematic of the system diagram and can be seen in Appendix E.
The MAX30101 integrates red, green, and IR LED drivers to modulate LED
pulses for SpO2 and HR measurements. The LED current can be programmed
from 0 to 50mA with proper supply voltage.
The LED pulse width can be
programmed from 69 µs to 411 µs to allow the algorithm to optimize SpO2 and
HR accuracy and power consumption based on use cases.
It is also possible
to control the sample rate from 50 Hz to 3200 Hz, and the number of samples
averaged, from 1 to 32 samples. Finally, the analog-to-digital converter (ADC)
range can vary from 2048 to 16384 nA. The sensor has two premade modes of
operation: SpO2 mode and heart rate mode. SpO2 mode is a multi-LED mode,
making the sensor able to process red and IR LED’s in sequence. The time slot
between active sequential channels is seen in figure 5.7 below. HR mode only
reads the IR LED. The MCU has a bit length of 18 bits.
Figure 5.7: Maxim MAX30101. Channel slot timing for the SpO2 mode with
a 1 kHz sample rate [50].
The MAX30101 also has an on-chip temperature sensor which can be used for
calibrating the temperature dependence of the LED measurements, since ambi-
==PAGE:53==
5.2 Sensors
39
ent temperature changes might cause small changes to the red LED which can
be compensated for. Furthermore, temperature data can be logged in order to
provide additional information about the subject.
Since this study aims at both providing heart rate and oxygen saturation reliably,
a custom set of optimal settings were determined in order to find which param-
eters for the MAX30101 sensor, to provide the highest quality PPG signals for
analysis. The optimal settings for this study were found by trial and error. Table
5.2 shows the settings used for data acquisition. Note that only the red and IR
LED’s were used in this study.
Table 5.2: Optimal setup of the Maxim MAX30101 PPG sensor.
Pulse Width

Averaging

IR Current

5.2.2
Accelerometer Sensor
The InvenSense MPU-6050 accelerometer sensor is a low cost, high-performance
motion tracking sensor, often used in top-end smartphones, tablets, and other
wearable systems. The sensor combines a tri-axial gyroscope and a tri-axial ac-
celerometer, with an onboard Digital Motion Processor, which processes complex
six-axis MotionFusion algorithms [49]. The device is 4 x 4 x 0.9 mm. In this study
only the accelerometer is used which has a programmable range of ± 2g,4g,8g,
and 16g. To track body position, the smaller and more precise ±2g range is used,
which has a high sensitivity of 16384 LSB/g (least significant bit per g) and a
sampling rate of 1 kHz. A block diagram of the hardware components in the
MPU-6050 sensor can be seen in Appendix E.1.
The sensor is incorporated in a breakout board which contains level conversion as
well as an I2C-bus for data transfer. The GY-521 breakout board is used in this
study. A block diagram of the sensor components and connection can be seen in
figure 5.8
==PAGE:54==
40
5 In-Ear Sleep Recorder
Figure 5.8:
Block diagram of the components of the MPU-6050 sensor and
connections [49].
5.3
Firmware
The PPG and accelerometer sensors are connected to the Adafruit MCU which
process the data flow for interpretation which works similarly to a central process-
ing unit (CPU) on a computer, but less sophisticated. Schematics and a block
diagram of the MCU can be found in Appendix E.1.
In order to control the transfer of data to an SD card or a PC, to configure
the sensor modules, and to control the behavior of the MCU, firmware for the
system needs to be developed. The firmware implemented in the Sleep Recorder
system has been developed by Oticon, along with the authors of the project. The
focus of the authors have been primarily on the development of firmware for the
configuration of sensor modules, with the settings as described in the previous
sections 5.2.1 and 5.2.2. The firmware implementation for the MAX30101 sensor
and MPU-6050 sensor is primarily made up of writing to small memory slots,
known as registers, which are used to store information about sensor settings.
Datasheets for the sensors, containing all of the available registers and settings,
can be found at [50][49]. The values of the sensors are acquired and streamed
using a first in, first out (FIFO) method for organization of streaming data from
a buffer.
The firmware has been developed in the Arduino IDE software program, which
can be downloaded for free online [51]. The IDE contains several libraries and
board drivers which are needed to program and run the M0 Feather MCU. More-
over, the IDE provides plotting and logging functions which have been used for
programming and for visualizing the optimal settings for the sensors. All of the
code is written in C++ and can be easily read and compiled by various software.
The firmware code developed in this project can be seen in Appendix I.
A flowchart illustrating the overall firmware implementation can be seen in figure
5.9.
==PAGE:55==
5.4 In-Ear Placement and Signal Quality
41
Figure 5.9: Simple flowchart of the firmware implementation developed for the
In-Ear Sleep Recorder system.
5.4
In-Ear Placement and Signal Quality
A previous study done in collaboration with Oticon found that signals received
from behind the ear and inside the auditory canal both provide good signals,
however, finding a proper position for the pulse oximeter behind the ear was dif-
ficult. Moreover, the effects of wax within the auditory canal had no significant
impact on the signals received [52]. In this study, even further investigation of
the in-ear signals was explored. The orientation of the sensor (LED’s), affected
the signal quality significantly; however, this is subject specific, but generally
orienting the sensor upwards or inwards (towards the other ear), provides proper
signals. Orienting the LED’s outwards causes the light emission to penetrate the
tragus, thus, less light is reflected which affects the signal quality. Lastly, having
the LED’s pointing downward does not provide a useful signal. The shape of the
ear causes the sensor not to have skin contact, with the current earpiece design,
which is essential in order to acquire proper signals. This is why Oticon is in
progress of designing a new earpiece that ensures good signal quality. However,
the patent has not yet been filed, and the design is highly classified, so no further
explanation can be given in the time of writing.
Motion artifacts are the most significant problem with pulse oximetry, as ex-
plained previously.
The new earpiece tries to ensure better skin contact and
might provide a decent solution to motion artifacts caused by jaw movement and
talk.
Another study experienced poor in-ear signals [53].
To the knowledge of the
authors, this was primarily caused by poor sensor and skin contact. The earpiece
used in that study was a standard silicone dome from a hearing aid seen in figure
==PAGE:56==
42
5 In-Ear Sleep Recorder
5.1. This provides little to no stabilization, therefore, motion artifacts are prone
to occur. Because of these aforementioned limitations, an earplug is used in this
study instead.

the data logged on the SD card proves to have inaccuracies regarding the timing
of the samples. The sampling frequency during a 1600 second recording, and a
60-second snippet of this recording, can be seen in figure 5.10.

This led to an investigation into possible causes of this inconsistency. Through
talks with firmware and hardware developers at Oticon, it was determined that
the inconsistencies were due to overflow and flushing of the buffer when reading
and transmitting the data while logging to the SD card at the same time. This
can be solved by the implementation of a Real-Time Operating System, which
involves running specific tasks on different threads. Since the development of the
original firmware, such a system has been implemented, which eliminates this

problem.
However, due to the time constriction on this project, the authors were not able
to perform additional recordings using the new firmware setup. The consequences
of the sampling inconsistencies will be explored in later chapters.

CHAPTER 6
Regulatory
For the In-Ear Sleep Recorder system to become applicable in the real-world, and
to record data on patients, the prototype must be approved for clinical trials. Ap-
plication to several regulatory entities must be filed, and the device must abide
by several international standards which must be documented.
In Denmark, the Danish Medicines Agency (Lægemiddelstyrelsen) must be noti-
fied in order to perform clinical investigations of non CE marked medical devices.
The investigation can commence upon receiving authorization from the regional
research ethics committee (Videnskabsetisk Komite), which primarily assesses the
ethical aspects of the investigation, along with the Danish Medicines Agency [54].
The application to perform clinical investigations should contain data that demon-
strate compliance with several international standards, purpose, and classification
of the device along with a risk assessment. Specification of regulatory require-
ments for the applications can be found in Danish Executive Order No. 292 of
19 March 2010.
The most essential requirements for the application is shown below. To view all
requirements, refer to [54].
1. Clinical Investigation Plan
2. Investigator’s Brochure
3. Statement certifying that the device in question complies with the Essential
Requirements, apart from those aspects covered by the investigation
In order to conform to the regulatory procedures, a draft for a regulatory inves-
tigative brochure was made by the authors of this study, in collaboration with
Ph.D. M.D. Poul Jenum and Oticon. Additionally, after inquiring biocompatibil-
ity procedures from the regulatory department of Oticon, a biological evaluation
report was conducted for the future prospects of the developed system.

to the international standard ISO 10993-1, Biocompatibility Testing. This ISO
analyze the system in terms of contact with body tissue and evaluates cytotoxic-
ity, sensitization, irritation, haemocompatibility, and much more.

CHAPTER 7
Signal Processing
In previous chapters, it was described how the In-Ear Sleep Recorder gathered
data.
This chapter will investigate the raw signals gathered from the device.
Signal processing was performed in MATLAB. The code can be seen in Appendix
H. The data recorded is stored on a micro SD card.
PPG signals contain a
vast amount of information about the user, including respiration, HR, heart rate
variability, oxygen saturation and more. Acceleration signals contain information
about body position, movement, and respiration. However, due to the limited
time of this project, only HR, SpO2, and respiration rate (from the accelerometer)
will be explored.
Intensive research has been done in the field of portable vital signs, portable
healthcare, fitness and health monitoring. Estimations of these specified features
can be calculated in several different ways. Various methods have been explored,
only the best-performing algorithms and methods will be analyzed and used for
this thesis.
Figure 7.1 gives an overview of the signals recorded by the Sleep Recorder, and
how different signal processing schemes are used to estimate the different features.
The signal processing and results of each algorithm will be explained in detail in
the following sections.
==PAGE:62==
48
7 Signal Processing
Figure 7.1:
Flowchart of prototype signals, signal processing and vital sign
estimations from PPG and accelerometer data.
7.1
Motion Artifacts in PPG
In order to proceed, motion artifacts embedded in the PPG signal has to be
addressed. One of the issues with medical equipment becoming smaller, wearable
and generally more comfortable is the fact that the signals will become much more
prone to noise. Even more so because the frequency band of motion artifacts
overlaps that of the PPG signal, making it impractical to separate the noise from
the signal using classic filtering. For example, the frequency band of respiration
is 0.04-1.6 Hz, and the frequency band of motion artifacts caused by the patient’s
movement is 0.1 Hz and higher. The frequency band of the PPG signal pulse
wave is in the range of 0.5-4.0 Hz [55].
7.2
Heart Rate
The concept of estimating HR from a PPG signal is straightforward in theory;
find each peak in the signal, and count the amount of time between the current
and the previous peak, after which it is transformed into beats per minute. That
being said, one has to take artifacts into account when implementing an HR
algorithm in practice. Making the appropriate algorithm is essential for reliable
results despite motion artifacts. Figure 7.2 is a flowchart of the HR estimation
algorithm.
==PAGE:63==
7.2 Heart Rate
49
Figure 7.2: Flowchart of the PPG signal processing and the method used to
estimate heart rate.
When estimating HR, the focus is peaks and inter-peak intervals in the PPG
signal. The consensus in the literature indicates that the IR LED light is better
for HR estimation, because the red LED fluctuates more, as seen in figure 7.10.
However, estimating HR from the red LED should give a similar result. Further-
more, Lee et al. concluded that when estimating HR during exercise, green or
blue LED’s are better [56]. These LED’s are less affected by artifacts but also
less accurate (due to lower intensity of the light, hence less penetration of the
arteries). This is why most smartwatches capable of estimating HR use green
LED’s.
The raw infrared PPG signal is filtered by an 8th order Butterworth filter with a
passband between [0.5, 5.0] Hz, for removal of noise outside the frequency band
of interest. The resulting magnitude response and phase response is shown figure
C.1 in Appendix C.
It is evident that frequencies between 0.5 and 5 Hz are not attenuated and that
the filter is an infinite impulse response (IIR) filter, hence a non-linear phase.
The output of the filter is shown in figure 7.3, note that since the passband does
not include 0 Hz, the DC offset is removed which is why the signal is centered
around zero.
==PAGE:64==
50

figure 7.4. Despite the inconsistent sampling rate discussed in section 5.5, it is

a heart rate of 60 beats per minute, which is consistent with the literature and
makes sense for a healthy person at rest [57].

7.2 Heart Rate
51
The HR estimation algorithm proposed in section 2.6, is now possible to execute
after having filtered the IR PPG signal. The entire PPG signal is partitioned into
sliding windows of two seconds with a one-second overlap. Two-second windows
are large enough for temporal information to be sufficient, meaning, at least two
PPG waves are contained within the window, as seen in figure 7.5. Making the
windows overlap with one second is simply to have a higher HR update-rate,
meaning the user would get HR information more frequently.
Having larger
windows would not capture sudden changes in HR.

Figure 7.5: Two-second window of bandpass filtered infrared PPG signal.
After filtering and windowing, the algorithm performs autocorrelation. Figure
7.6 shows the two-sided autocorrelation of an IR PPG window.
==PAGE:66==

Autocorrelation Function
Figure 7.6: Output from the autocorrelation function from a two-second IR
PPG window.
The distance between the dominant peak to the second peak
(either way), is consistent with the cardiac frequency.
The autocorrelation is the same as the cross-correlation of two identical signals.
The ACF works by taking the two second PPG window and ’comparing’ it with
a copy itself as a function of delay. The prominent peak is seen in figure 7.6 at
lag zero, corresponds to the signal compared with itself without delay, and since
these two signals are identical, the autocorrelation becomes one. Because of this,
the ACF is symmetrical around lag zero. The second largest peak of the autocor-
relation is seen at lag -100 and 100, which corresponds to the inter-peak duration
in the IR signal. This is why the window should contain at least two peaks in the
PPG waveform, otherwise, there would be no second peak in the ACF, making
HR estimation impossible. If the window contains three peaks, the ACF would
also have three peaks, with decreased similarity for each peak. The advantage of
this algorithm is that it provides a simple yet excellent HR estimation, even in
the presence of motion artifacts.
Figure 7.7 shows the single-sided ACF of the same window as the figure above.
The peak-detection algorithm searches for a peak within the two vertical lines,
based on the theory of physiological heart rate range explained previously. Inside
this range, the algorithm searches for the maximum peak indicated by a red
circle. The lag, or sampling index, Ns, at this location is used in equation (2.10)
to estimate the heart rate. In this example the subject’s estimated HR is
HR = 60s × Fs
Ns
= 60s × 100Hz
100 lag
= 60 bpm
which seems quite reasonable for a young person, in decent shape at rest during
the recording.

7.3
SpO2
Arterial blood oxygen saturation is the most common method used nowadays
in hospitals to estimate SaO2.
The following section is PPG derivations for
calculating SpO2 using the In-Ear Sleep Recorder. Figure 7.9 shows a simplified
flowchart of the SpO2 algorithm approach. In section 2.5 it was shown how to
calculate the SpO2 in theory, however, the Beer-Lambert law does not take into
account the wavelength-dependent effect of scattering.
Figure 7.9: Simplified flowchart of the signal processing used to estimate SpO2
values.
In order to compare the optical extinctions for deoxygenated- and oxygenated
hemoglobin both the red and IR light spectrum has to be used, from which SpO2
can be derived. Figure 7.10 shows a 1600 second raw signal. The signal consists
of several different activities, and this is especially visible in the red signal which
is more prone to changes. Initially, the signal contains roughly four minutes of
resting(sitting), followed by three desaturations, clearly seen as dips in the red
signal.
The desaturations were achieved by complete exhalation, followed by forced
breathing stop for as long as possible.
Following the breathing stops, was a
short period of resting. Afterward, around three minutes of high motion (stand-
ing squads) was performed, as seen in the figure at around second 1000 to 1200,
ending with the removal of the earpiece.

==PAGE:70==
56
7 Signal Processing
The SpO2 algorithm works by initially lowpass filtering the two signals to remove
out of band noise, such as power line interference, quantization noise, and high-
frequency motion artifacts. Stuban et al. found that the PPG signal could be
lowpass filtered without affecting the SpO2 measurement, as long as the cutoff
frequency was above the cardiac cycle frequency [58]. The AC and DC component
is defined as the amplitude above and below 0.5 Hz respectively, extracting these
are achieved by high and lowpass filtering the two signals. The signal processing
steps are visualized in figure 7.12.
Figure 7.12: Flowchart of the PPG signal and the signal processing algorithm
used for SpO2 estimations.
The first lowpass filter is a 6th order Butterworth filter with a cutoff frequency
of 5 Hz. The cutoff frequency is tuned to be as low as possible without ruining
the SpO2 measurements. The subsequent highpass and lowpass filters are both
5th order Butterworth filters, meaning they are IIR filters with non-linear phase,
seen in figure C.2 and C.3 in Appendix C.
As stated previously, the AC component contains the waveform of the arterial
pulse, whereas the DC component contains the venous blood and surrounding
tissue, seen in figure 7.13.
==PAGE:71==
7.3 SpO2
57
Figure 7.13: AC and DC components of red and IR PPG signals respectively,
computed by highpass and lowpass filtering.
These four different signals are then segmented into sliding windows of two sec-
onds with one-second overlap, exactly like the HR algorithm. The windows of
the red AC and DC components are shown in figure 7.14.
Figure 7.14: AC and DC components of a 2 second red ppg window used to
estimate SpO2.
Two similar equations were proposed in equation (2.5), however, the practical
difference between the two is very significant. The peak-valley method has dif-
==PAGE:72==
58
7 Signal Processing
ficulty with noise induced by motion artifacts, and subsequently, the algorithm
can not distinguish PPG peaks from noise peaks. Therefore, unless the data is
clean, the peak-valley method has its limitations. This is why the more robust
root mean square (RMS) AC/DC method is applied in this study.
Each window of the red and IR AC- DC component is divided, as shown in the
flowchart above. From which the RMS is then calculated. Dividing the resulting
values grants the estimated R-value of that specific two-second window, consis-
tent with equation (2.5).
In theory the relationship between the R-ratio and SpO2 was given in equation
(2.8), however, as explained earlier, calibrating the curve empirically gives a more
reliable result. This is because the manufacturing tolerances in the illumination
cause the oxygenation value to vary. Additionally, Beer-Lambert law does not
take into account the wavelength-dependent effect of scattering [59]. Using the
equipment from Rigshospitalet - Glostrup as a reference, a new calibration line
could be fitted to the data; ideally, this data should be gathered from a lot of
different subjects, but not feasible due to time constrictions. Furthermore, it
should be noted that medical devices are calibrated using data from patients in
a controlled desaturation study, where the volunteers breathe a sequence of gas
mixtures of decreasing oxygen content. Arterial blood samples are taken from
the volunteers to be investigated in a clinical laboratory. The resulting R-values
are then compared to state of the art equipment as shown in figure 7.15.

Each axis contains motion information in respect to its specific direction. When
==PAGE:75==
7.4 Respiration Rate
61
estimating respiratory rate, the acceleration vector perpendicular (Z-axis) to the
chest is of interest, as this direction contains most of the relevant information.
Placement of the sensor and the direction of the three axes can be seen in figure
7.20.
Figure 7.20: Orientation of the wearable tri-axial accelerometer sensor located
within the chest box, in respect to the user.
Motion consists of both high and low frequencies, during physical activity the
signal contains a lot of high-frequency noise. However, this is not a problem,
as with PPG signals, since respiration rate is comprised of very low frequencies
relative to regular motion. Therefore, the signal of interest is easily separated
from the noise by proper filtering.
The typical respiration rate of an adult at rest is in the range 12-18 breaths per
minute [60]. The lower limit of the respiration rate is set by the respiratory center
in the medulla oblongata. During physical exercise, however, respiration can go
much higher. The upper limit of respiration rate is estimated to be around 30-35
breaths per minute. This means that the respiration frequency is estimated to lie
between 0.2 to 0.55 Hz (12-33 bpm). In order to extract the respiratory signal,
an 8th order Butterworth filter with a passband between [0.2, 0.55] Hz is applied.
The magnitude and phase of the filter can be seen in figure C.4 in Appendix C.
The filtered Z-axis acceleration data can be seen in figure 7.21. The bandpass
filtering is performed on a randomly selected 60-second window of raw Z-axis
data. In order to remove the phase response, filtering is performed by filtfilt in
MATLAB™.
==PAGE:76==
62

In order to perform estimation of respiration rate, the accelerometer signal is
divided into 60-second windows with one-second overlap. This means that res-
piration estimates can be performed after one minute of recorded data, followed
by updates every second. The size of the window is determined by choosing the
minimal size which gives a sufficient frequency resolution to perform accurate
detection of respiration rate.

7.4 Respiration Rate
63
Estimating the respiratory rate is done by calculating the dominant frequency
of a given window. The spectral density is calculated by power spectral density
(PSD). This method provides power spectral density estimates performed with
averaging to reduce variability within the limits of the Nyquist Theorem. The
resulting PSD of the above figure is seen in figure 7.23.

PSD (dB/Hz)
PSD of window
Figure 7.23: PSD of a 60-second accelerometer window used to estimate the
respiration frequency.
A dominant frequency of 0.23 Hz corresponding to 14
breaths per minute.
The dominating frequency is found at the maximum PSD
RRfreq = freq(max(PSD)) = 0.23Hz
(7.2)
Since respiration rate is usually given as breaths per minute, the respiration
frequency is converted into conventional units given by equation (7.3).
RR = RRfreq × 60 = 14 bpm
(7.3)
This method can be applied to the entirety of the signal, estimating respiration
rate of the subject for each second. In figure 7.24, 180 seconds of respiration rate
values can be seen, calculated based on acceleration data from the In-Ear Sleep
Recorder. This will be validated in a later chapter.

==PAGE:79==
CHAPTER 8
Signal Validation
In this chapter, signals and estimation algorithms used for the Sleep Recorder will
be investigated. The proposed heart rate, oxygen saturation, and respiratory rate
algorithms will be validated against equipment from Rigshospitalet - Glostrup.
Furthermore, performance statistics of the methods developed by the authors will
be discussed.
Data was collected over three recording sessions using different test subjects.
A thorough study using several test subjects is still required to fully validate
the results. However, the validation performed in this pilot study suggests the
real potential of the device.
In the following, acquisition of reference signals,
recordings using the In-Ear Sleep Recorder and statistical evaluation of similarity
between the devices will be explored.
8.1
Reference Signal
To properly evaluate the signals acquired during recordings, estimations have to
be compared to values obtained using an approved medical device. At Rigshospi-
talet - Glostrup, the medical equipment used to assess OSA is made by Som-
noMedics™.
In this project, the SOMNOtouch™RESP, a small polygraphy
screening device, is used as the reference, seen in figure 8.1.
Figure 8.1: Main unit of the SOMNOtouch™RESP - Cardiorespiratory screen-
ing device [61].
==PAGE:80==
66
8 Signal Validation
To perform polygraphy readings, different sensor equipment can be attached to
the main unit. Sensors and modalities which can be used for recordings include:
• Pressure / Flow, Snore
• Effort Thorax & Abdomen
• SpO2, Pulse rate
• Body position
• Movement (Sleep Wake Determination)
• CPAP- /BiPAP-Pressure
Figure 8.2: Illustration of the use of SOMNOtouch™RESP - Cardiorespiratory
screening device with sensor connection [61].
An illustration of the recordings performed by the SomnoMedics™RESP can be
seen in figure 8.3 below.
Figure 8.3: Recordings of different modalities using the SOMNOtouch™RESP
- Cardiorespiratory screening device [61].
==PAGE:81==
8.2 Recordings
67
For this project, only a subset of the available signal modalities is needed to
perform validation. To validate oxygen saturation and heart rate estimates, SpO2
and pulse rate readings were recorded. To validate respiratory rate, thorax and
abdominal effort were recorded. To obtain additional information, pressure/flow
signal using a nasal cannula was recorded. The recordings from the reference
equipment are the basis for the statistical validation of the acquired signals.

8.3
International Validation Standards
Based on international ISO standards, estimations by pulse oximetry must be
performed with a certain accuracy to be approved clinically as a medical device.
This is done using the standard ISO 80601-2-61:2017, which states that overall
accuracy of estimates must be within ±2% of SaO2, and is estimated as
Arms =
v
u
u
t 1
N
N
∑
i=1
(SpO2i −Ri)2
(8.1)
where Ri refers to the reference values. Since the reference device is an approved
medical device, it conforms to this standard. This implies that values provided
by the reference equipment are not the ground truth, and are also subject to im-
precision and artifacts. However, for the purpose of this study, recordings from
the reference device are accepted as the ground-truth. Proving a high similarity
between the obtained and reference signals will then indirectly indicate a high
similarity between estimations and the actual physiological ground truth. This
also implies, that when encountering small inconsistencies between the reference
and estimated signals, the ground-truth assumption of the reference device will
become highly ambiguous.
To perform proper validation of the device, a clinical study is required, using
a large number of test subjects as well as actual blood-gas readings. However,
==PAGE:83==
8.4 SpO2
69
for the scope of this pilot study, validation using clinically approved reference
equipment is deemed to be sufficient before commencing a sizeable investigative
study.

As illustrated, overall estimate and reference values come further apart during
noisy conditions and lead to uncertain and more unstable SpO2 values computed
by the algorithm. The red LED signal is prone to artifacts during noisy condi-
tions, where ear displacement will cause the amplitude of the reflected signal to
vary a lot. This is a general problem regarding SpO2 methods, especially using
reflective PPG. However, as explained in a previous chapter, the SpO2 algorithm
utilized is the method least prone to noise. Note that the SpO2 values are similar
although less precise than for the other conditions.
To evaluate the estimation vs. reference values statistically, the method of Bland-
Altman plots will be employed. A study from Bland and Altman showed that
frequently used statistical measures such as correlation coefficients could be mis-
leading in the interpretation of results, and proposed a simple method for com-
parison of new measurement methods to established ones [62]. This method is
the most recognized for measuring statistical agreement and show the mean dif-
ference with 95% confidence interval between obtained values. Such a plot can
be seen in figure 8.10 for the different activities.
