Estimation and reference values vary based on the activity performed. During
clean conditions, the mean difference in SpO2 values is 0.07 with a 95% confidence
interval at around ±1%. These results indicate that there is a high statistical sim-
ilarity and that the in-ear method can be confidently used and possibly replace
existing methods for clinical purposes. The limits of agreement are very reason-
able for desaturation events, where the mean difference is acceptable and higher
confidence intervals are mostly due to variations in the slope of SpO2 values
around desaturation events. Inspecting the desaturations, minimum SpO2 values
and time of desaturation show excellent agreement. During noisy conditions, the
statistical similarity decreases, especially with regards to mean difference, when
compared to clean signals. The overall agreement between measurement methods
seems to be within reasonable limits, which looks very promising for the prospect
of in-ear measurement of oxygen saturation using PPG sensors. The statistics of
the Bland-Altman method can be seen in table 8.2.

fit to be based mostly on intermediate values. In table 8.3, additional statistics are

more useful than R2 since RMSE measures the spread of the residuals which
indicates how concentrated the data is around the around the line of best fit.

and the sensitivity of the algorithms differ, meaning that during periods of desat-

heart rate, estimations vs. reference values become less precise, despite showing
the same overall trends. Secondly, PPG from the reference device is recorded
with a finger clip instead of in-ear measurements of the Sleep Recorder. This
means that same amount of noise will not be present in both signals simultane-
ously, as the location and therefore movements causing these artifacts are not
identical.

within acceptable limits. Proper alignment of signals, elimination of drifting and

Although there is high disagreement between estimation and reference measures,
computations on the reference signal are reliable. This has been controlled, by
counting the breaths per minute. A subsequent inspection of the reference values
concluded that the algorithm produces the right results on the reference signal.
Therefore, it was hypothesized that the error was not caused by algorithm design,
but rather by other underlying mechanisms.
This lead to an investigation of why the accelerometer signals from the Sleep
Recorder produce bad results with regards to respiration rate. During this, it
was discovered that intrinsic hardware and software issues caused the logging of
values from the device to become inaccurate, and thereby sampling with a variable
time duration within each sample.
Even though the period between samples
only varied around 1 ms, this causes many issues when having to analyze the
signals in the frequency domain. The variable duration between samples, and
thereby variable sampling frequency, were determined for all windows created
when performing respiratory rate estimations for one of the recording session.
The length of each window, set to 60 seconds, and the sampling frequency, set to
100 Hz, can be seen in figure 8.22.
==PAGE:103==

used for estimation of respiration rate.
It is evident that the windows and sampling frequency of each window vary a
lot, which is clearly not intended. Because of the limited frequency resolution,
inconsistencies in sampling rate will cause subsequent frequency bins and PSD
estimations to become inaccurate.
Therefore, the authors of this project are
confident that solving these issues will lead to a reliable estimate of respiratory
rate, as seen for the reference device.
8.7
Discussion
In this chapter, the validation of heart rate and oxygen saturation values were
performed, yielding great results. The in-ear PPG signals proved to provide oxy-
gen saturation values of high accuracy when compared to reference values by an
approved device.
The heart rate estimation values likewise showed promising
results. In-ear placement of sensors for monitoring of vital signs with high ac-
curacy is, therefore, determined to be a viable solution for continuous wearable
monitoring systems, and also applicable in a clinical setting.
However, intrinsic errors were present, causing some imprecision.
This had a
limited impact on SpO2 since the method does not depend on the timing of the
samples. HR estimates were affected slightly since the implementation relies on
peak detection at specific lags which translates into heartbeats. This means that
the slightly elevated sampling frequency between the drops, seen in figure 8.22,
will lead to a slight overestimation of heart rate. This concurs with the small
==PAGE:104==
90
8 Signal Validation
offset, at around 1 bpm, seen for the estimated heart rates across all activities.
Performing respiration rate estimations using the proposed algorithm proved to
be impossible on the Sleep Recorder data, due to the resulting consequences of
frequency scattering.
After the recordings used for this study, Oticon implemented a Real-Time Op-
erating System into the software, as explained in section 5.5, which eliminates
these problems.
Therefore, subsequent recordings and validation of estimates
should improve and also make it possible to detect respiratory rate from the
accelerometer data.
==PAGE:105==
CHAPTER 9
Shutdown Algorithm
Developing a design based on wearable sensors have several important aspects.
As shown previously in chapter 8, the accuracy of heart rate and oxygen satu-
ration vary across activities, due to noise artifacts in the PPG which can result
from high activity and specific motions. When monitoring is performed in pri-
vate and uncontrolled environments, the quality and interpretation of the data
acquired can be questioned. Clinicians will rarely be available to supervise or
act upon alarms from continuous devices [63]. PPG signals acquired by mobile
sensors, which are especially prone to noise artifacts, might negatively impact
measurement accuracy and ultimately result in a misleading diagnosis performed
by the system [64]. This is especially true for reflective PPG signals, which have
small amplitudes, and are easily corrupted by motion-induced variations [59].
Identification of PPG segments which contain a high-quality signal will, there-
fore, lead to reliable estimations and increased diagnostic accuracy of the device.
Moreover, identification of signals of high or low quality could be used in power
management and sampling strategies, if a high sampling frequency is not needed
or wanted. Developing an algorithm for signal quality indexing used for shut-
down and exclusion of signals of low quality could be beneficial. It was initially
proposed to set up an experimental protocol where subjects performed different
noise inducing activities, and develop a shutdown algorithm based on multiclass
classification. However, due to time restrictions, this was not possible to perform.
Instead, signal segments labeled as ”Clean” or ”Noise” from signal validation
chapter 8 will be used to develop the framework for a simple yet powerful shut-
down algorithm.
Different methods for signal quality assessment have been used and proposed in
this field of research. Several signal quality indices have shown great promise in
classifying signal segments [64]. Measures such as correlation coefficients by the
use of template matching or autocorrelation of the PPG signal has also shown to
have impact [65][63]. Based on these methods and by incorporating additional ac-
celerometer data features for activity and noise estimation, a shutdown algorithm
==PAGE:106==
92
9 Shutdown Algorithm
will be developed in this study.
In figure 9.1 it is apparent that summation of the 3-axial accelerometer data is
a good indicator of movement, and the correlation with the signal quality of the
PPG signal is also visible. Using the standard deviation, a noise threshold is set.
In the upper subplot, the red line indicate when the threshold is exceeded and
indicates a lot of movement which can cause noise. In the lower subplot, it can
be seen how the quality of the PPG signal changes during the noise indicated
segments.

9 Shutdown Algorithm

From these illustrations, it is evident that the SQI features are useful separators
of the two classes. This is very promising for the prospects of the proposed algo-
rithm.
The shutdown algorithm design is determined by the best performing machine
learning model, by testing various architectures including different kNN’s, SVM’s,
Decision Tree’s, etc. The optimal shutdown algorithm is a support vector machine
model, using a Gaussian kernel. The kernel parameters have a Kernel Scale = 3.5
and Box Constraints = 1. To discover the theory and methods regarding support
vector machine, refer to section 10.3. The model is trained using clean and noisy
data, the specific information about data generation can be found in section 8.2.
The performance of the developed shutdown algorithm is evaluated using 10-fold
cross-validation.
In table 9.2 the performance of the algorithm for noise detection can be seen.

Although the exploration into shutdown algorithm design using PPG and ac-
celerometer data is based on a limited number of data points, it highlights the
enormous potential of developing shutdown algorithms which can effectively de-
termine when to sample and not to sample data, to acquire signals and subsequent
estimates of the highest quality.
In Appendix D an additional shutdown algorithm based on multiclass classifica-
tion is proposed.

==PAGE:111==
CHAPTER 10
Machine Learning
In this chapter the theory for three different machine learning methods, the neces-
sary preprocessing and feature development is described. Initially, it was intended
to use the In-Ear Sleep Recorder on patients with suspected OSA, but due to
time constraints, data was downloaded from a database.
10.1
Machine Learning Intro and Database
The goal of the thesis is to make a portable system, which makes it possible for
patients to avoid tests in overnight sleep-labs, and to create automated detection
of OSA in a home environment. The development of machine learning techniques
to predict OSA would thereby decrease the workload of the medical doctors.
Machine learning is a statistical technique which makes it possible for computers
to ”learn” from input data, without being explicitly programmed. These meth-
ods are used for tasks that are too complex, if not impossible, for humans to
code explicitly. Instead, a large amount of data is provided for the algorithm to
explore and to search for model parameters that would achieve the programmer’s
requirements, which in this case is to predict OSA with as few errors as possible.
The data used in this study is downloaded from the MESA sleep data on National
Sleep Research Resource (NSRR) database [19]. The data consists of overnight
PSG data of 27 different signals. Of these, only four are used to simulate data
gathered from the Sleep Recorder. Acceleration from the thorax, PPG from the
finger, SpO2 and HR, which can be derived from PPG. The sampling rates of the
signals are 32, 256, 1 and 1 Hz, respectively. The signals from the database and
their corresponding signal from the Sleep Recorder are seen in figure 10.1. The
PPG, HR, and SpO2 are gathered from a Nonin 8000 sensor which lowpass filters
the PPG signal, and then calculates HR and SpO2. The thorax acceleration data
is lowpass filtered with a cut off frequency of 10 Hz. The MESA database consists
of over 2000 patients, of which 186 was used in this thesis for machine learning.
Of this data, roughly 3% of it consist of OSA events.
==PAGE:112==
98
10 Machine Learning
Figure 10.1:
Flowchart of the corresponding signals between the prototype
and the database. SpO2 and HR is calculated from raw PPG signal using the
prototype, whereas SpO2 and HR are independent signals in the database.
The patients in the dataset are labeled by medical doctors with different types of
events, only the OSA event labels are extracted. The event is labeled with a start
time and duration for each specific event per patient. These events are then used
to binary label the data with 0’s and 1’s, for non-apnea and apnea respectively.
This binary label is considered the ground truth when predicting.
10.1.1
Preprocessing
Data preprocessing can have a significant impact on the generalization perfor-
mance of supervised machine learning algorithms. The elimination of noise in-
stances is one of the most challenging problems in inductive ML [66].
Before applying ML algorithms, the data has to be preprocessed. Firstly the
algorithm loads a specified number of patients into MATLAB and the four signals
and their specific sampling rates.
Afterward, each patient is investigated for
OSA events, if the medical doctors label them with OSA, the algorithm extract
those specific patients for further processing. Otherwise, the patient is discarded.
Pseudocode shows the simplified data acquisitions presented in algorithm 1.
==PAGE:113==
10.1 Machine Learning Intro and Database
99
Algorithm 1 Preprocessing
1: procedure loadPatients(numPat)
▷Load specified number of patients
2:
while i < numPat do
3:
if correct signal then
4:
extract signal, Fs and label
▷Fs = sampling rate
5:
end if
6:
end while
7: end procedure
8: procedure findApnea(data, Fs)
▷load extracted data
9:
for i = 1 : numPat do
10:
if OSA then
▷for patients with OSA
11:
Remove noise
12:
Remove first and last hour of signal
13:
Make overlapping-windows & extract features
14:
end if
15:
end for
16: end procedure
For each patient with OSA, acceleration, PPG, HR and SpO2 data is extracted.
The duration of the signals for a given patient varies between six to 12 hours. The
first and last hour of each signal is removed because these are contaminated with
lots of artifacts. The nurse sometimes equips the patients with the sensors hours
before they go to bed. It is crucial that as much noise as possible is removed
from the signals, without altering the data in the process.
Going through several hours of data manually, for each patient is unfeasible, but
data has to be cleaned. Hence, a simple yet effective threshold to remove noise
was implemented based on physiological constraints on HR and SpO2.
If a given sample has an HR of < 45 bpm or a SpO2 level of < 50%, that sample
is classified as an artifact and removed throughout all signals, keeping different
sample rates in mind. While it is possible to have a resting heart rate below 45
bpm physiologically, it is highly unlikely unless the patient is a top athlete, and
having a SpO2 level below 50% will render a person unconscious. Xie et al. used
the same threshold for SpO2 [36]. When manually inspecting the different signals
for a number of patients, an HR of less than 45 bpm nor a SpO2 level of less than
50% was encountered.
After removing artifacts, the PPG and acceleration data is segmented into two-
second windows with one-second overlap, each window then contains two to three
PPG peaks and valleys (as with the Sleep Recorder data). The SpO2 data is
segmented into windows of 60 seconds, with 59 seconds of overlap, consistent
==PAGE:114==
100
10 Machine Learning
with Xie et al. The longer window is created to increase the temporal resolution,
as SpO2 values have a delayed reaction as discussed previously. For each of the
one-second sliding windows, features are then extracted to be used for machine
learning. Hence, features are calculated for every second of data.
10.1.2

In the following, the 60 features used as ML input for the different classifiers are
presented. Feature 1-58 are calculated from windows, which simulates a sampling
frequency of 1 Hz. The remaining two features, raw HR and raw SpO2 already
have a sampling frequency of 1 Hz, hence all of the 60 features align for optimal
detection of OSA.

10.1.3
Machine Learning Initialization
A final important step in preprocessing, before being able to feed the data to the
ML algorithms, is transforming the data. The algorithm needs to be as unbiased
and general as possible, to make the best predictions of new and unseen data.
Because of this, all the patient data is normalized between one and zero, also
known as feature scaling by the following equation.
X′ =
X −Xmin
Xmax −Xmin
(10.1)
Since the range of raw data can vary widely, not normalizing the data will im-
pact the objective functions to not work correctly. The majority of classifiers use
the Euclidean distance to calculate the distance between two points, therefore, if
some features have a broad range of values, the distance of that particular feature
will be weighted differently and thereby have another importance that might not
be valid. Another significant reason why normalizing the data is relevant, is be-
cause the gradient descent algorithm (used by classifiers such as neural networks)
converges faster.
The feature scaled data is partitioned into training- and testing sets, split 80/20%
respectively. The training data is for training the classifier or model to learn how
the data looks during OSA events. The testing data is for making predictions
on new data which the model have not yet encountered, to see if the data is
==PAGE:117==
10.2 Neural Network
103
overfitted by the model. Furthermore, because the data is predominantly non-
apnea (97%), all the apnea data is extracted, and then combined with an equal
size of non-apnea, making the baseline 50% instead of 97%. Otherwise having a
classifier saying non-apnea (0) to every sample presented would give an accuracy
of 97%. Because of this, ML results are often evaluated by other means that
accuracy, since accuracy does not always give a valid representation.
10.1.4
Feature Reduction
Selection of features can be an essential tool to minimize the amount of data
needed to perform classification and to remove redundancy. Since this study is
performed offline and aim at highest predictive capability, feature reduction has
not been performed. However, if low computational power restricts a system, this
selection of best-performing features might be preferable. A small investigational
study using neighborhood component analysis (NCA) on the features presented
in this study can be found in Appendix B.2.
10.1.5
Model Complexity
When building classification models, the model complexity has to be taken into
account. Exploration of how to obtain the optimal model complexity, the bias-
variance trade-off, has been performed by the authors and can be seen in Ap-
pendix B.3.
10.2
Neural Network
Neural networks, also known as Artificial Neural Networks (ANNs), is a compu-
tational algorithm made up of many layers of highly interconnected processing
elements which converts a series of inputs into a single state response. The ter-
minology neural network is inspired by how the neurons in the human brain
communicate and how the brain processes information. An illustration of this is
seen in figure 10.4.
==PAGE:118==
104
10 Machine Learning
Figure 10.4: Biological neuron (left) and a common mathematical neural model
(right) [68].
Neural networks have seen a wild increase in popularity in the past decade, and
are often used in the industry for complicated tasks such as speech recognition,
text recognition, and image processing. Because of the rapid progression of com-
putational power, deep neural networks have in recent years become a powerful
tool in the development of artificial intelligence, used throughout most, if not all
industries.
Neural networks can be set up as a single layer perceptron, which only consists
of input and output layers. Neural networks can also be set up as multilayer
perceptron, that consists of a predetermined number of so-called ”hidden layers”
between the input and output layers, which are interconnected.
An NN can
be set up as ’feedforward’, meaning that information moves through the chain
of layers from input to output layer in one direction, which does not cycle or
become recurrent, i.e., recurrent neural network [69][70].
In this project, an NN will be developed in the form of a feedforward Multi-Layer
Perceptron which can be used for the classification of OSA. An illustration of
such a feedforward NN using three input features, two hidden layers, and a single
output layer is in figure 10.5.
Figure 10.5: Illustration of the architecture of a neural network, with one input
and output layer and two hidden layers [71].
==PAGE:119==
10.2 Neural Network
105
Such networks can be extended in order to incorporate a greater number of input
features, hidden layers, and output states if necessary. As illustrated above, all
the nodes between adjacent layers are connected. Each connection has a speci-
fied weight and bias, which determines how much each input to a given neuron,
influences the output to be used further in the network. Firstly M linear combi-
nations of input vector x = [x1, x2, ..., xD], the weight vector w = [w1, w2, ..., wj]
and the bias wj0, the value of a single neuron in the first layer is computed as

In order to optimize performance of predictions, different learning functions can
be implemented.
The overall goal of these learning functions are to optimize
weights and biases throughout each layer in order to minimize the overall predic-
tion error. This method of optimization is known as backpropagation.
==PAGE:120==
106
10 Machine Learning
10.2.1
Neural Network Architecture
As described above, neural networks can be configured in many different ways,
using different learning functions and numbers of layers. Furthermore, learning
functions can use a subset of so-called performance functions in order to estimate
prediction error which is used in parameter optimization during backpropagation.
In order to determine which network to utilize, a search throughout the available
MATLAB supported training and cost functions is performed.
There are no
explicit set of rules or norms to follow to determine the number of hidden layers
to be used in a network given a specific classification problem. However, the
maximal number of hidden layers for a given network and the maximal number
of layers for the data in this study can be seen in Appendix B.4.
A large number of hidden layers will be inefficient concerning computational
power and might lead to overfitting and redundancy. Therefore, the number of
possible layers to be considered for the network in this project is evaluated in
the range between 1 and 20, deemed to be sufficient for classification problem at
hand.
An overview of the different functions used and the number of hidden layers
explored to determine the optimal configuration setting during the grid-search
can be seen below in table 10.2.

To avoid overfitting, neural networks can employ a method called early stopping
when performing training. This is done by splitting training data into train, test
and validation data. Error measured for independent validation data will drop
until the network starts to overfit. Hence, the training will cease at minimal error
concerning validation data, which gives the best general performance.
Using these methods, along with the optimal configuration found in the previous
section, the final network can be seen in figure 10.8.

From this figure, it is clear that the algorithm stops the training when the valida-

accordance with the early stopping rule, and with respect to model complexity,

using 10-fold cross-validation.
10.3
Support Vector Machine
Support vector machine is a well-known and commonly used computational al-
gorithm.
This model is well suited for binary classification problems such as
distinguishing between apnea and non-apnea. The algorithm for the two-class
classification problem utilizes linear models which are given by
==PAGE:124==
110
10 Machine Learning
y(x) = wT ϕ(x) + b
(10.10)
where wT is the weight vector which is perpendicular to the decision surface, ϕ(x)
denotes a fixed feature-space transformation, and b is the bias. The algorithm
consists of input vector xn = [x1, . . . , xN], N being the total number of inputs.
The training data is supplied with corresponding target values given by tn =
[t1, . . . , tN] where tN ∈{−1, 1}. New data points x evaluated by the algorithm
will subsequently be classified depending on the sign of y(x) [72].
The SVM
architecture is seen in figure 10.10.
Figure 10.10:
The architecture of a support vector machine.
The optimal
hyperplane is found by maximizing the width of the margin which separates the
two classes.
As illustrated above, the decision boundary determined by the algorithm is the
result of a grid-search for the optimal hyperplane. This hyperplane is the plane for
which the margin between itself and the points (classes) around it is maximized.
For the binary classification problem presented in this paper, the form of the
hyperplane is given by
wT ϕ(x) + b = 0
(10.11)
For a given data point x where the hyperplane is defined as equation (10.11), and
y(x) is given by equation (10.10), the perpendicular distance between these will
be given by y(x)
∥w∥. The aim of the SVM is to maximize this perpendicular distance
between data points of either class. The optimization is done by determining the
optimal values for weight vector wT and the bias b.
==PAGE:125==
10.3 Support Vector Machine
111
The optimal hyperplane is determined by maximizing the margins given by the
maximization problem in equation (10.12).
arg max
w,b
{ 1
∥w∥min
n [tn(wT ϕ(xn) + b)]
}
(10.12)
For the binary classification problem, the nearest data points to the optimal
hyperplane for each class will have the labels 1 and -1 respectively. Therefore,
each class has a minimum distance to the optimal hyperplane by
1
∥w∥, which
results in the nearest points of either class having a margin of
1
∥w∥+
1
∥w∥=
2
∥w∥
between them.
The above equation can be expressed as a quadratic problem, giving a more
compact equation [72].
arg min
w,b
1
2∥w∥2
(10.13)
The equivalent and constrained optimization problem are solved by utilizing La-
grange multipliers and applying Karush-Kuhn-Tucker (KKT) conditions. Solving
equation (10.13) determines the support vectors to be used in the resulting model.
In most real-world problems, feature distributions of different classes overlap,
making an estimation of an optimal decision boundary more complicated where
clear separation might lead to worse generalization. To take this into account,
the SVM is modified by introducing a slack variable, ξn ≥0. This variable is
used to control the trade-off between overfitting to training data and generalized
performance. The value of the variable influences the algorithm tolerance of data
points which are allowed to be misclassified. Each data point has its own slack
variable, set to ξn = 0, if the point is on or inside the correct margin boundary,
and set to ξn ≥1 if the point is on or outside the decision boundary, based upon
the Euclidean distance. This means that the problem given by equation (10.13)
can now be expressed as
arg min
w,b
{
C
N
∑
n=1
ξn + 1
2∥w∥2
}
(10.14)
where C > 0 controls the trade-off between penalization from the slack variable
and the margins.
Since input features can be hard to separate linearly, the SVM algorithm utilizes
a kernel to create a non-linear decision boundary. This is done by projecting data
into higher dimensions which can be linearly separable, using a kernel which then
transforms the obtained boundaries back to the original feature space. In order
to find the optimal kernel for the dataset in this study, several different kernels
==PAGE:126==
112
10 Machine Learning
are investigated. The best performing SVM kernel is then implemented in the
final model.

obtained for each of the models, seen in table 10.3.

10.4
Decision Trees
A decision tree is a supervised learning algorithm that can be used for classifica-
tion or regression, which is why it is also known as a classification and regress
==PAGE:127==
10.4 Decision Trees
113
tree (CART). The decision tree works by partitioning the feature space into rect-
angles, and then fit a simple model in each one. The model can be represented
by a binary tree as seen in figure 10.11.
Figure 10.11: Illustration of the architecture of a binary decision tree classifier.
Adapted from [73].
Decision trees are typically drawn upside down, as seen in the above figure, where
the leaves (terminal nodes) lies at the bottom of the tree with the predictions
(apnea/non-apnea), and the roots at the top with the splitting criteria.
The
class obtained in the leaves is the mode of observations (most observed) in that
region of the tree. The trees follow a recursive binary partitioning known as a
top-down greedy approach. ’Top-down’ because it begins from the top of the tree
where all observations are available in the single region and successively splits the
feature space into two new (binary) branches. ’Greedy’ because the algorithm
is exclusively concerned about which feature fits best for the current split, and
no future splits, which might make a better tree model. The splitting process is
continued until a stopping rule is applied.
A key advantage of the recursive binary tree is how easy it is interpreted. The
feature space partition is fully described by a single tree, as shown in figure 10.11.
For classification and regression trees, the estimate of accuracies is calculated by
different formulas. For classification (categorical dependent variable) trees, which
will be investigated here, accuracy is measured in terms of true classification rate
of the classifier.
The algorithm needs to decide on the splitting variables automatically and split
points, and which topology the tree should have.
Firstly a partition into M
regions {R1, R2, ..., RM} is made, and the constant response cm is modeled in
each region.
==PAGE:128==
114
10 Machine Learning
f(x) =
M
∑
m=1
cmI(x ∈Rm)
(10.17)
The best binary partition is done by starting with all the data and having a split
variable j and split point s, and define the pair of half-planes (greedy method)
R1(j, s) = {X|Xj ≤s}
and
R2(j, s) = {X|Xj > s}
(10.18)
where R1 and R2 is region one and two respectively. Then finding a splitting
variable j and a split point s which solves
minj,s
[

∑
xi∈R1(j,s)
(yi −c1)2 + minc2
∑
xi∈R2(j,s)
(yi −c2)2]
(10.19)
where c1 and c2 is the response modeled as a constant for region one and region
two respectively. Having found the best split, the data is partitioned into the
two resulting regions and the splitting process is repeated on each of the regions.
This process is repeated on all of the resulting regions until the stopping criteria
is reached (like minimum node-size of a certain size).
Figuring out how large to grow the tree, is done by a so-called impurity measure,
the tree needs to be large enough to capture all the important features of the
data, yet not so large that it overfits the data. In a node m, representing a region
Rm with Nm observations let
ˆpmk =
1
Nm
∑
x∈Rm
I(yi = k)
(10.20)
be the proportion of class k observations in node m. The observations in node
m belonging to class k(m) = arg maxk ˆpmk, which is the majority (mode) class
in node m [74]. There are different measures of node impurity of which some of
them include:
Misclassification: 1 −ˆpmk(m)
Gini index:
K

Cross-Entropy:

The different functions are shown in figure 10.12, Gini index is used for the
classification tree in this thesis.

10.5
Random Forest
Random forest is a modification of the bootstrap aggregation (bagging) algorithm,
so-called because it is constructed from various decision trees, as seen in figure
10.13. It is a type of ensemble learning method, where a large collection of de-
correlated trees are averaged to give predictions, which is done by averaging or
majority voting for regression- and classification problems, respectively.
==PAGE:130==
116
10 Machine Learning
Figure 10.13: Ensembles of classification trees, showing the random forest al-
gorithm graphically. Adapted from [75]
The essential idea in bagging is to average many noisy but approximately un-
biased models, and hence reduce the variance.
Trees are ideal candidates for
bagging since they can capture complex interaction structures in the data, and
if grown sufficiently deep, have a relatively low bias (refer to model complexity).
Since trees are notoriously noisy, they benefit significantly from the averaging.
Moreover, since each tree generated in bagging is identically distributed, the ex-
pectation of an average of B such trees is the same as the expectation of any
one of them. This means the bias of bagged trees is the same as that of the in-
dividual trees, and the only hope of improvement is through variance reduction.
The idea in random forests is to improve the variance reduction of bagging by
reducing the correlation between the trees, without increasing the variance too
much. This is achieved in the tree-growing process through random selection of
the input variables [74]. When growing a tree on a bootstrapped dataset, before
each split, a selection m ≤p of the input variables at random as candidates for
splitting. Generally, for classification, the default value for m is √p, and the
minimum node size is one, this setting is used in the thesis which comes out to
be
√
60 features which is rounded to 8 features.
10.5.1
Random Forest Training
The training algorithm for random forests applies the general technique of bag-
ging, to decision trees. Given a training set Z = [(x1, y1), (x2, y2), ..., (xn, yn)],
bagging repeatedly (B times) selects a random sample with replacement of the
==PAGE:131==
10.5 Random Forest
117
training set and fits trees to these samples. Random forest differs from bagging
in one way, by selecting a random subset of the features at each candidate split,
this is shown in pseudocode in algorithm 2.
Algorithm 2 Random Forest for Regression or Classification
1: for b=1:B do
1. Draw a bootstrap sample Z∗of size N from the training data.
2. Grow a random-forest tree Tb to the bootstrapped data, by recursively
repeating the following steps for each terminal node of the tree, until
the minimum node size nmin is reached.
(i) Select m variables at random from the p variables.
(ii) Pick the best variable among the m.
(iii) Split the node into daughter nodes.
Output the ensemble of trees [Tb]B
2: end for
where Z∗b, b = 1, 2, ..., B is the bootstrap sample. After training, predictions for
unseen samples x′ can be made by taking the majority vote, or the average
Classification: ˆCB(x′) = mode([ ˆCb(x′)]B)
(10.21)
Regression: ˆf B(x′) = 1
B
B
∑
b=1
Tb(x′)
(10.22)
where ˆCb(x′) is the class prediction of the bth random-forest tree. This bagging
technique leads to better model performance because it decreases the variance of
the model, without increasing the bias [74].
An essential feature of random forests is the out-of-bag (OOB) samples, which
are samples that did not appear for training a specific tree. These are almost
identical to K-fold cross-validation; hence when the OOB error stabilizes, the
training can be terminated. Because of the way the random forest classification
uses bagging, it rarely overfits the data, even when growing full trees [74].
A final feature of the random forest is its ability to use the OOB samples to con-
struct a different feature importance measure, to measure the prediction strength
of each variable. When the bth tree is grown, the OOB samples are passed down
the tree, and the prediction accuracy is recorded. Then the values for the jth
splitting variable are randomly permuted in the OOB samples, and the accuracy
is computed again. The decrease in accuracy as a result of this permuting is
==PAGE:132==
118
10 Machine Learning
averaged over all trees and is used as a measure of the importance of variable j
in the random forest. These are expressed as a percent of the maximum in figure
10.14. At each split in each tree, the improvement in the split-criterion is the
importance-measure attributed to the splitting variable and is accumulated over
all the trees in the forest separately for each variable.
Figure 10.14:
Feature importance measured by the OOB samples from the
random forest algorithm. Used to determine the most valuable features for clas-
sification.
Performance of the model on the training data is assessed using 10-fold cross-
validation.
10.6
Postprocessing
It was stated previously that the binary OSA label was used as ground truth
whether a given window contains apnea or not. However, since medical doctors
do the signal analysis and labeling, human errors do occasionally occur. Because
of this, some of the labels specifying apnea or non-apnea might be incorrect,
which affect the accuracy of the ML algorithm. If an apnea event occurs, which is
classified by the algorithm as apnea, but is miss-classified (or simply not detected)
==PAGE:133==
10.6 Postprocessing
119
by the medical doctors as non-apnea, the performance of the classifier is affected
negatively. This specific scenario can be seen in figure 10.15.
Figure 10.15: Nasal airflow of a patient with apnea. The orange color indicates
where the medical doctor has labeled the data as OSA. The initial part, where
the signal is flat, is 15.7 seconds where the patient stops breathing but is not
labeled as OSA.
Furthermore, the time stamps of the labels are not necessarily precise, which
means that the duration noted during an event might be too long, or too short, or
even have an error with regards to when it starts, this can be seen in figure 10.16.
These human errors will impact the predictive powers of the models negatively.
==PAGE:134==
120
10 Machine Learning
Figure 10.16: Nasal airflow of patient with apnea. The orange color indicates
where the medical doctor has labeled the data as OSA. The time stamp indication
of when the apnea event ends appears to be wrong.
To improve the performance of the different machine learning methods a post-
processing algorithm was implemented. OSA was defined in section 3 previously.
Following this definition, if not at least ten consecutive windows, i.e., ten con-
secutive seconds, are classified as OSA, then the window is labeled as non-apnea.
E.g., if five consecutive windows (five seconds) are classified as OSA, the label is
changed from apnea to non-apnea. By definition, for it to be OSA, the duration
of an event has to last at least ten seconds. On the flip-side, if non-apnea seg-
ments are located within an apnea, these are changed from non-apnea to apnea.
In example, if an apnea of 17 seconds is followed by three seconds of non-apnea,
after which another 15 seconds of apnea occurs, then it is labeled as a 35 seconds
apnea event.
The guidelines given by the American Academy of Sleep Medicine does not specify
if the above example should be given as two apneas or one. Hence it is up for
debate which scenario is correct.
==PAGE:135==
CHAPTER 11
Results
This chapter gives a presentation of the results for each of the three different
machine learning algorithms trained and tested on the MESA data, using the
proposed feature set. The models will be evaluated in terms of area under ROC
curve (AUC), accuracy, specificity, sensitivity and F1 score. Only the best in-
trinsic classifier will be evaluated in each section, i.e., not all the different NN’s
or SVM’s with different cost functions and hyperparameters, only the highest
scoring. Furthermore, some of the features will be explored and discussed, and
lastly, a new random forest model, trained on structurally different data will be
investigated. To sum up the results and evaluate performance, a comparison of
similar studies is provided in the last part of the chapter.
• True positive (TP): Person with apnea (sick) correctly identified as sick
• False positive (FP): Person without apnea (healthy) incorrectly identified
as sick
• True negative (TN): Person without apnea correctly (healthy) identified as
healthy
• False negative (FN): Person with apnea incorrectly (sick) identified as
healthy
Accuracy =
TP + FN
TP + FP + FN + TN
(11.1)
Specificity =
TN
TN + FP
(11.2)
Sensitivity =
TP
TP + FN
(11.3)
F1 =
2 × TP
2 × TP + FP + FN
(11.4)

The receiver operating characteristic (ROC) curve is a commonly used summary
for assessing the tradeoff between sensitivity and specificity. It is a plot of the
sensitivity (true positive rate) versus 1-specificity (false positive rate) as the pa-
rameters of a classification rule vary.
The relationship between SpO2 and an obstructive sleep apnea event can be seen
in figure 11.1. The start and stop of the apnea event are indicated on the figure by
dotted vertical lines, and the nasal flow is also shown to visualize when the patient
stops breathing. The orange line is the SpO2, during the first 15 seconds, the
SpO2 level increases, because a previous apnea event had just occurred. During
the apnea event itself, the SpO2 level remains pretty much constant, but after
approximately 20 seconds of no breathing the SpO2 level drops to 93%. This is
consistent with what Eichhorn et al. found in their study. After a steady state
period, during which the oxygen stores of the lungs were being consumed, SpO2
started to decrease, at the end of apnea, SpO2 ceased to decrease and started to
increase again with a delay of 24 seconds after the first inhalation [76].

The classification models in the next section consist of 49.3 hours of data with a
50/50 ratio of apnea and non-apnea data. All of the models have been validated
using 10-fold cross-validation, to ensure that the model does not overfit and is as
general as possible. The data is split into train and test, with a distribution of
80% training data and 20% testing data.
11.1
Neural Network
The results of the best performing neural network are shown in this section. To
visualize the optimal classification point, and the interaction between sensitivity
and specificity of the final model, a ROC curve is displayed in Appendix B.7,
with an AUC of 0.9209. The value of the AUC indicates that the neural model
created is well suited for detection of obstructive sleep apnea.
Table 11.1 gives an overview of the performance by the neural network model.

different methods; 10-fold cross-validation, test data predictions, and postpro-
cessed (PP) test data predictions.

11.3.1
Counting Apneas
Predicting labels are important, this is often seen in machine learning competi-
tions such as those created on Kaggle, which has been getting much attention
lately. These competitions are purely evaluated in terms of the area under the
ROC curve results.
However, in medical practice that is not always relevant,
another vital aspect of the results is being able to count how many apneas occur
during the night and how long each event occurs. This is investigated in this
section, and all of the results will be compared to the ground truth, scored by
the medical doctor.
In order to do this, a new training and testing dataset was constructed, which
consisted of a more regular proportion of non-apnea versus apnea data, meaning
that of the 249.3 hours of (new) test data, 4.6 hours (476.4 minutes) consisted of
OSA, which is roughly 2% of the data. One of the challenges when training the
previous model, consisting of a 50/50 distribution of apnea/non-apnea, was the
fact that only 3% of the data was apnea, therefore, far less data was available
for training. The new model consists of primarily non-apnea data, which allows
for a larger training set for the algorithm to learn from. Specifically, the training
data changed from approximately 10.6 million data points, in the old model, to
nearly 200 million data point, in the new model.
Due to time constrictions, an ’apnea counting model’ was only created for the
best performing machine learning algorithm, the random forest. The new ran-
dom forest model was tasked with predicting this unseen, imbalanced data, and
to report back how many apneas were encountered, and the total-, mean- and
maximum apnea-duration. Even though the model was trained on very imbal-
anced data, a great performance was achieved, proving the importance of big
datasets. Results for the new test data predictions are shown in table 11.4 and
apnea counting statistics are shown in table 11.5.

There are listed two results without postprocessing in the table 11.5. The 1825
’apneas’ are not physiological apneas, in the sense that they do not meet the
requirements of the OSA definition. Hence, the actual apneas encountered are
483, which by definition, has a duration of non-breathing of at least ten seconds,
see section 3 for the precise definition.
This is why the predictions get postprocessed, to remove all the labels that are
not concatenated into an apnea event. The postprocessing algorithm removes any
1’s that are not part of a concatenation of at least ten seconds (ten 1’s). To clarify,
the first portion of results under ’Model w.o. PP’ is the actual predictions, but
is not actual apneas by definition, the ’events’ might last only one second. The
second portion is the results which do fit the definition of OSA, hence 483 apnea

If results are purely given in terms of counting apneas (concatenated 1’s with
a duration of at least ten seconds) as opposed to counting labels (1 or 0), the
detected 483 apneas out of 533 apneas gives an accuracy of 90.6%, and an accu-
racy of 64.7% on the total duration of the apneas, before postprocessing. After
postprocessing, detection of 520 apneas out of 533 apneas was counted, achieving
an accuracy of 97.5% and accuracy of 99.4% on the total duration, which seems
very promising. It has to be noted that the postprocessing algorithm not only
removes 1’s that are not connected to other 1’s, it also stitches 1’s together, as
stated in a previous chapter. In example, if a series of consecutive 1’s are within
10 seconds of another series of consecutive 1’s, these series will be concatenated
together, and the labels in between are set to apneas (1) as well. This will result
in a single and longer duration apnea. This method has been very beneficial,
hence the high postprocessing accuracy.
This is why the postprocessed maximum duration is 89 seconds, and the actual
maximum duration is 72 seconds. An apnea event of 72 seconds, followed by
another event of 17 seconds occur within 10 seconds of each other. Whether the
event actually is 89 seconds, or two events of 72 and 17 seconds respectively, is
arguable.
Figure 11.6 is around three minutes of nasal flow data with indications of when
a medical doctor has annotated start and stop of three apnea events, shown as
vertical green lines, whereas the vertical red dotted lines are the predictions by
the model of the same three apnea events. Generally the model starts a bit later
than the true label, and stops a bit earlier, this however might also be contributed
to the fact that the doctors are not highly accurate when labeling the data and
last but not least it has to be taken into account that the windows are made
in integers, meaning that if the doctor has labeled an apnea event as last 23.8
seconds, the actual timestamp given is 24 seconds giving small rounding errors.

Based on these results, it is concluded that using pulse oximetry and accelerome-

tive sleep apnea, is possible. Furthermore, to the knowledge of the authors, the
results achieved in this study are among the best in the literature.
==PAGE:147==
CHAPTER 12
Discussion &
Conclusion
An overview and discussion of the results of this study can be divided into two
main parts. The first part consists of the development and testing of an in-ear
multimodal prototype system, which could be used for vital sign assessment for
continuous monitoring. Ultimately, the system is supposed to be integrable in
Oticon’s hearing aid devices, making it possible to screen for several diseases or
disease-management of subjects, who are hearing impaired. The target demo-
graphic, therefore, mainly consists of the older segment of the population, where
the rate of chronic diseases and need for disease management are also the highest.
Ideally, such a system can be used for any purpose, integrated into a hearing aid
device or as a stand-alone device for screening and monitoring of diseases, which
can also be used clinically. The second part consists of the development of a
detection- and classification system of chronic diseases, OSA in this case. The
use of modalities and features of this system are solely based on the signals and
features which can be estimated by the proposed in-ear multimodal system. An
application to the regulatory and governmental entities in Denmark have been
drafted, allowing the system to be used in a clinical setting. However, due to
long approval time and the time restrictions of this study, development of such
a system could not be done with data recorded by the In-Ear Sleep Recorder.
Instead, a large online database was used, and the signals thoracic effort, PPG,
oxygen saturation, and heart rate were acquired in order to mimic the data from
the Sleep Recorder. This resulted in a classification algorithm which can be used
for screening of OSA using few modalities as opposed to the current diagnostic
methods.
In the first part, the developed In-Ear Sleep Recorder shows great potential as
a system which can be used for recording and estimation of vital signs, using
wearable sensors. In-ear placement of sensors provides a practical and highly ef-
fective solution for wearable sensor systems. The in-ear PPG signals have shown
to produce results and estimates that have high similarity with existing clinical
==PAGE:148==
134
12 Discussion & Conclusion
devices. The signals sampled from the reflective PPG sensor can be used for
estimation of heart rate and oxygen saturation using relatively simple algorithms.
Signal validation proved that in-ear measurements have the prospect of replacing
current diagnostic techniques while improving comfortability.
Although intrinsic software implementation errors of the system did not allow
the accelerometer data from the Sleep Recorder to be used for estimation of res-
piratory rate, however, it has been shown that the algorithm produces excellent
results using the reference (hospital) signal. The implementation of a Real-Time
Operating System for the elimination of the aforementioned sampling inconsis-
tencies will, therefore, make it possible to perform this estimation using the Sleep
Recorder. Additionally, as explored in the state of the art section, accelerometer
data can be used for several estimations which can improve parts of the system.
These additional implementable features include activity recognition, body posi-
tion, respiratory effort, noise-cancellation and much more.
Since wearable sensors are often prone to noise due to their small size and non-
obtrusive placement, the development of a shutdown algorithm and signal quality
indexing is vital to obtain high signal-to-noise (SNR) ratio. Using the raw PPG
signals combined with accelerometer data, a shutdown algorithm was developed
to distinguish and classify clean from noisy data. This has proven to be effective,
although conducted on limited data.
Furthermore, in-ear placement and orientation of the sensor is crucial for high-
quality recordings. Previous studies had issues with low SNR, due to poor skin
contact with the sensor. The earpiece needs to provide high comfortability as well
as good skin contact, which an ear-plug seems to achieve. However, for long-term
use, an ear-plug is not ideal, as the user is unable to hear their surroundings due
to occlusion. This is why Oticon is in the process of creating a new earpiece, to
provide a design offering a high degree of fit and secure placement within the ear
canal while avoiding occlusion.
The algorithm developed in this study promotes further investigation into shut-
down algorithm design, where a larger test group and several activities can lead
to an effective multiclass classification of signal quality and activity assessment.
In the second part, a comprehensive feature set was developed and used for detec-
tion of OSA. The features displayed an excellent predictive power for distinguish-
ing apnea from non-apnea events, providing separation of target distributions
in the feature space. Three different machine learning approaches were inves-
tigated, of which, the Random Forest algorithm achieved the best performance.
The results of the final classifier demonstrated an accuracy, sensitivity, and speci-
==PAGE:149==
12.1 Future Work
135
ficity of 100%, 97%, and 100% respectively, after applying post-processing of the
predicted labels. The post-processing algorithm was created to enhance the clas-
sification rate, by applying physiological restrictions to the predictions. These
results indicate the promising prospects of a home-screening system, which can
be used for early detection of OSA. Such a system will provide added accessibility
of screening and detection of OSA on a global scale and can drastically impact
how disease screening and management is performed.
Moreover, devices based on in-ear placement can also have benefits in a clinical
setting. Most conventional systems work in transmission mode, which is strongly
affected by reduced peripheral perfusion, especially in life-threatening situations,
where blood is usually centralized away from the limbs. In situations when vital
signs monitoring is the most crucial, the system might be inhibited. Because of
this, placing the sensor in areas unaffected by reduced blood perfusion is useful,
such as the proposed system. The inner ear canal seems to be an ideal mea-
surement site due to its proximity to the brain. Furthermore, the ear canal is
less affected by motion artifacts and provides high comfortability. As stated by
Webster: ”Research of pulse oximetry as a monitoring device for clinical practice
has shown that for determining hypoxemia, any reasonably small loss in accuracy
that may be attributed to measuring arterial oxygen saturation transcutaneously
is outweighed by the advantages of non-invasiveness and continuous, immediate
availability of data” [2].
12.1
Future Work
Despite the encouraging results achieved in this pilot study, some of the devel-
opments have some intrinsic limitations. Given more time, these could be solved
and additional functionalities implemented for increasing the future usability of
the system. The aforementioned implementation of a Real-Time Operating Sys-
tem is a must for the Sleep Recorder to have a steady sampling rate, furthermore,
having a comfortable earpiece without occlusion is required for long-term daily
use.
In order to have a proper validation in regards to the prototype, a vast number
of participants would have to undergo a controlled breathing study for recali-
bration of the SpO2 algorithm. Moreover, to perform validation of heart rate,
simultaneous ECG recordings should be performed and used as the reference de-
vice. However, for the scope of this study, a comparison with hospital equipment
seemed sufficient and possible to achieve.
Implementing more acceleration features such as body position, respiration rate,
==PAGE:150==
136
12 Discussion & Conclusion
activity recognition, and more, could add many functionalities to the existing
system and prove to increase the performance of the classification algorithms.
However, these options were not explored further due to the fundamental sam-
pling error in the Sleep Recorder and time constrictions. Additionally, research
during recent years has shown that physiological measures such as heart rate
variability (HRV) or blood pressure (BP) can be estimated from the PPG signal.
Developing methods for these additional vital signs computations could have a
significant impact on the detection of OSA and several cardiovascular diseases.
Lastly, a more complex machine learning algorithm could be developed but
seemed out of scope for this study. The idea was to create a comfortable and
straightforward screening system using wearable sensors to detect OSA using
a limited number of modalities. Therefore, the investigated computational al-
gorithms are chosen to have a limited complexity for less computational effort,
which proves to have a very high and acceptable performance. Complex deep
learning algorithms and feature extension to train on might improve the good
results even further. These machine learning algorithms could be implemented
as a cloud solution, performing the OSA screening. Subsequent notification to
a medical doctor or caretaker could be given by the system, indicating that a
subject might be ill.
12.2
Conclusion
The purpose of this study is to design a non-invasive in-ear prototype for the
screening of OSA patients. Equipped with multimodal sensors, the prototype
shows promising results for estimating heart rate and oxygen saturation, how-
ever, lacks a consistent sampling rate for precise respiration rate estimations.
The validation of the heart rate and oxygen saturation recordings display remark-
able statistical similarity to the hospital equipment and highlight the promising
prospect of in-ear vital signs measurements. The comfortable placement and high-
quality signal acquisition promote the use of such systems in a clinical setting.
The development of a shutdown algorithm for classification of clean and noisy
signal segments can further improve the accuracy of the device, excluding noisy
measurements, which ultimately leads to higher diagnostic accuracy. Based on
modalities of the Sleep Recorder, numerous features are extracted and machine
learning algorithms are developed displaying encouraging results for the screen-
ing and detection of OSA. The best performing classification algorithm, Random
Forest, achieved an accuracy, sensitivity, and specificity of 100%, 97%, and 100%
respectively. The ease of use of the proposed system makes it possible to screen
undiagnosed subjects suspected of having OSA.
==PAGE:151==
12.2 Conclusion
137
While the current system is used for detection of OSA, the possibilities are vast.
Incorporating additional sensors such as temperature and EEG, could make a
battery of diseases possible for detection, and provide overall health-monitoring
of vital signs. This study suggests that an inconspicuous in-ear wear-and-forget
device for screenings of chronic diseases, or as an early warning system, is pos-
sible. However, to become a commercial device, further, development has to be
performed. Due to the promising results in this study, a journal paper draft for
future publication has been included in Appendix A.

APPENDIX A
Journal Paper Draft
In this chapter, is a very early draft for a journal paper to be published, based
on the promising results achieved in this thesis. The draft was done with very
limited time at disposal, and errors and flaws will be corrected in future work.
==PAGE:162==
1
In-ear Multimodal Portable Screening System for
Early Detection of Obstructive Sleep Apnea
Rasmus L. Bendtsen1, Nicolai Westergren1, Helge B.D. Soerensen1, Poul Jennum2, and Anders E. Petersen3
Abstract—It is estimated that 4% of men and 2% of women in Denmark, above the age of 45, suffer from obstructive sleep apnea (OSA)
syndrome. OSA is a sleep disorder with short- and long-term consequences, which include sleep-related issues, social implications,
and cardiovascular diseases. OSA is diagnosed in an overnight sleep laboratory with a polysomnogram (PSG). Monitoring can be
expensive and time-consuming. Treatment for OSA is usually performed with Continuous Positive Airway Pressure (CPAP), which
helps the subjects with breathing during the night. At least 50% of all OSA patients are undiagnosed. Sixty features were derived
from photoplethysmography (PPG), arterial blood oxygenation (SpO2), abdominal accelerometer and heart rate (HR) from 186
patients, used as input for three different machine learning (ML) methods. Random Forest (RF) achieved the highest results with an
accuracy, sensitivity, and speciﬁcity of 99%, 97% and 99% respectively. The results prove that new, easy-to-use, low-cost wearable
sensors could provide valuable OSA screenings.
Keywords—PPG, Accelerometer, Machine Learning, OSA screening.
I. INTRODUCTION
R
ESEARCH within the ﬁeld of sleep disorders have in
recent years shown that suffering from obstructive sleep
apnea increase the likelihood of developing several cardiovas-
cular diseases. Moreover, general sleep deprivation can cause
several unwanted consequences, such as accidents and reduced
quality of life. Studies also suggest, that long-term oxygen
deprivation during sleep might lead to mental health issues,
hence early detection and treatment is crucial.
American Academy of Sleep Medicine provides guidelines
for scoring sleep-related events, and for classiﬁcation of sleep
disorders. The term sleep-disordered breathing encompasses
a range of disorders, of which most fall into the categories
obstructive sleep apnea, central sleep apnea, or sleep-related
hypoventilation. Due to the high prevalence of OSA, eval-
uation of the affected population, with PSG, will have a
signiﬁcant cost. Moreover, the availability of testing equipment
and sleep laboratories in some regions might be limited.
Undiagnosed and untreated OSA is a signiﬁcant burden on
the healthcare system. Innovation and optimization can prove
to have a vital impact on early and accurate diagnosis of this
common disorder [1].
The diagnosis based on the current golden standard for
detection of obstructive sleep apnea is cumbersome and
expensive because it relies on extensive polysomnography
readings, which has to be reviewed by a trained professional.
This imposes several practical and ﬁnancial limitations when
screening the general public. Therefore, most patients are
undiagnosed.
Developing an easy to use diseases screening system for
wear-and-forget devices, can provide an inexpensive screening
tool for obstructive sleep apnea. Based on a related work
by the authors, the development of a device using in-ear
PPG sensor and accelerometer placed at the thorax made
contionous monitoring of vital signs heart rate and oxygen
1. Department of Electrical Engineering, DTU
2. Department of Clinical Neurophysiology, Rigshospitalet - Glostrup
3. Oticon A/S, Kongebakken Smorum, Denmark
saturation, derived from the PPG signal, possible. Furthermore,
the chest based accelerometer adds further value to the system
by giving a representation of the respiratory effort which is
highly correlated to the cessation of breathing in OSA.
Based on this design, it is sought to create a machine learn-
ing classiﬁer for the detection and screening of OSA events,
by the use of only the PPG and accelerometer modalities. The
data used for creation of the classiﬁer is based on extracting the
relevant signals from the PSG of OSA patients in a database
available at the National Sleep Research Resource (NSSR).
II. RELATED WORKS
The papers reviewed in this section, use features completely
or partly from modalities which are identical to those proposed
in this study
In the paper by Almazaydeh et al. detection of OSA
using only one modality showed a very promising result,
achieving an accuracy of 93.3% only using SpO2 signals with
a neural network classiﬁer [2]. Sweeney et al. investigated
the combination of ECG and acceleration for detection of
sleep apnea, using a body sensor [3]. The authors achieved
very promising results scoring an accuracy of 91.2% using
a decision tree classiﬁer. PPG derived features such as heart
rate or heart rate variability are valuable for detection of OSA.
Uc¸ar et al. managed an accuracy of 97% using a MLFFNN
with features from PPG signals [4]. In another study by Behar
et al. they achieved accuracy of 92% based on only the PPG
signal [5].
The studies have shown that signals such as PPG, which can
be used in order to estimate heart rate and oxygen saturation,
is a very signiﬁcant feature for detection of obstructive sleep
apnea. In addition, multimodal studies have shown great
promise, incorporating data from accelerometers, which is also
one of the proposed modalities for this study. The systems and
classiﬁers described in the reviewed studies have shown great
performance and encourages further study of the research ﬁeld.
A Journal Paper Draft
148
==PAGE:163==
2
III. METHODOLOGY
A. Database
The data used in this study is downloaded from the MESA
sleep data on National Sleep Research Resource (NSRR)
database [6]. The data consists of overnight PSG data of
27 different signals. Acceleration from the thorax, PPG from
the ﬁnger, SpO2 and HR, which can be derived from PPG.
The sampling rates of the signals are 32, 256, 1 and 1 Hz,
respectively. The PPG, HR, and SpO2 are gathered from a
Nonin 8000 sensor which lowpass ﬁlters the PPG signal, and
then calculates HR and SpO2. The thorax acceleration data is
lowpass ﬁltered with a cut off frequency of 10 Hz. The MESA
database consists of over 2000 patients, of which 186 was used
in this thesis for machine learning. Of this data, roughly 3%
of it consist of OSA events.
The patients in the dataset are labeled by medical doctors
with different types of events, only the OSA event labels are
extracted. The event is labeled with a start time and duration
for each speciﬁc event per patient. These events are then used
to binary label the data with 0’s and 1’s, for non-apnea and
apnea respectively. This binary label is considered the ground
truth when predicting.
B. Preprocessing
Before applying ML algorithms, the data has to be pre-
processed. Firstly the algorithm loads a speciﬁed number of
patients into MATLAB
TM and the four signals and their speciﬁc
sampling rates. Afterward, each patient is investigated for
OSA events, if the medical doctors label them with OSA, the
algorithm extract those speciﬁc patients for further processing.
Otherwise, the patient is discarded. Pseudocode shows the
simpliﬁed data acquisitions presented in algorithm 1.
Algorithm 1 Preprocessing
1: procedure LOADPATIENTS(numPat)
▷Load speciﬁed
number of patients
2:
while i < numPat do
3:
if correct signal then
4:
extract signal, Fs and label
▷Fs = sampling
rate
5:
end if
6:
end while
7: end procedure
8: procedure FINDAPNEA(data, Fs)
▷load extracted data
9:
for i = 1 : numPat do
10:
if OSA then
▷for patients with OSA
11:
Remove noise
12:
Remove ﬁrst and last hour of signal
13:
Make overlapping-windows & extract features
14:
end if
15:
end for
16: end procedure
For each patient with OSA, acceleration, PPG, HR and
SpO2 data is extracted. The duration of the signals for a given
patient varies between six to 12 hours. The ﬁrst and last hour
of each signal is removed, because these are contaminated with
lots of artifacts. It is crucial that as much noise as possible
is removed from the signals, without altering the data in the
process.
Simple yet effective thresholds to remove noise was imple-
mented based on physiological constraints on HR and SpO2.
If a given sample has an HR of < 45 bpm or an SpO2 level
of < 50%, that sample is classiﬁed as an artifact and removed
throughout all signals While it is possible to have a resting
heart rate below 45 bpm physiologically, it is highly unlikely,
and having a SpO2 level below 50% will render a person
unconscious. Xie et al. used the same threshold for SpO2 [7].
After removing artifacts, the PPG and acceleration data is
segmented into two-second windows with one-second overlap,
each window then contains two to three PPG peaks and
valleys. The SpO2 data is segmented into windows of 60
seconds, with 59 seconds of overlap, consistent with Xie
et al. The longer window is created to increase the tem-
poral resolution, as SpO2 values have a delayed reaction
as discussed previously. For each of the one-second sliding
windows, features are then extracted to be used for machine
learning. Hence, features are calculated for every second of
data.
C. Feature Extraction
In the following, the 60 features used as ML input for the
different classiﬁers are presented. Feature 1-58 are calculated
from windows, which simulates a sampling frequency of 1 Hz.
The remaining two features, raw HR and raw SpO2 already
have a sampling frequency of 1 Hz, hence all of the 60 features
align for optimal detection of OSA.

The relationship between SpO2 and an obstructive sleep
apnea event can be seen in ﬁgure 2. The start and stop of
the apnea event are indicated on the ﬁgure by dotted vertical
lines, and the nasal ﬂow is also shown to visualize when the
patient stops breathing. The orange line is the SpO2, during
the ﬁrst 15 seconds, the SpO2 level increases, because a
previous apnea event had just occurred. During the apnea event
itself, the SpO2 level remains pretty much constant, but after
approximately 20 seconds of no breathing the SpO2 level drops
to 93%. This is consistent with what Eichhorn et al. found
in their study. After a steady state period, during which the
oxygen stores of the lungs were being consumed, SpO2 started
to decrease, at the end of apnea, SpO2 ceased to decrease and
started to increase again with a delay of 24 seconds after the
ﬁrst inhalation.

D. Classiﬁcation Model
An overview of the machine learning process is given in
the ﬂowchart in ﬁgure 3.
Fig. 3. Flowchart of database signals, feature extraction and event detection.
Note that SpO2 is a feature itself, hence the 33 additional features.
The random forest model consists of a forest of 300 grown
trees with a split selection of
√
60 ≈8 variables for each
tree and a minimum node size of one, using Gini index as
a measure of node impurity and the classiﬁcation is done by
majority voting. The resubstitution loss is a function of each
additional tree added to the random forest .The loss is still
declining, but minimally, hence why it is stopped after 300
cycles.
E. Postprocessing
To improve the performance of the different machine learn-
ing methods a postprocessing algorithm was implemented.
AASM has a deﬁned deﬁnition of an OSA event. Following
this deﬁnition, if not at least ten consecutive windows, i.e., ten
consecutive seconds, are classiﬁed as OSA, then the window
is labeled as non-apnea. E.g., if ﬁve consecutive windows
(ﬁve seconds) are classiﬁed as OSA, the label is changed
from apnea to non-apnea. By deﬁnition, for it to be OSA, the
duration of an event has to last at least ten seconds. On the
ﬂip-side, if non-apnea segments are located within an apnea,
these are changed from non-apnea to apnea. In example, if
an apnea of 17 seconds is followed by three seconds of non-
apnea, after which another 15 seconds of apnea occurs, then
it is labeled as a 35 seconds apnea event.

V. DISCUSSION & CONCLUSION
The features displayed an excellent predictive power for
distinguishing apnea from non-apnea events, providing sepa-
ration of target distributions in the feature space. The Random
Forest algorithm achieved the best performance. The results of
the ﬁnal classiﬁer demonstrated an accuracy, sensitivity, and
speciﬁcity of 99%, 97%, and 99% respectively, after applying
post-processing of the predicted labels. The post-processing
algorithm was created to enhance the classiﬁcation rate, by
applying physiological restrictions to the predictions. These
results indicate the promising prospects of a home-screening
system, which can be used for early detection of OSA. Such
a system will provide added accessibility of screening and
detection of OSA on a global scale and can drastically impact
how disease screening and management is performed.

B.1
Feature Table Explanation
ODISx is the total number of SpO2 samples thgat fall at least {2,3,4,5} below
the baseline.
ODIxy, x ∈{2, 3, 4, 5}, y ∈{1, 3, 5} is the number of occurrences that SpO2 level
declines at least x below the baseline and lasts at least y seconds.The baseline is
the mean value of the top 20% of the SpO2 data within one minute.
TSAx, x ∈{70, 80, 85, 90, 95} is the accumulative time that SpO2 level stays be-
low x.
CTMx is the central tendency measure with radius {0.25, 0.50, 0.75, 1.00}
For further explanation see [44] and [36] for feature 1-22 and 23-55 respectively.
B.2
Feature Reduction
In this project, proposed feature selection is performed using the MATLAB™
function fscnca, which is feature selection for classification using neighborhood
component analysis (NCA). This is a supervised learning technique for classifying
data into target classes based on feature metrics of the data, working similar to
kNN.
In figure B.1 below, optimization of the regularization parameter used for feature
selection is shown.
==PAGE:168==
B.2 Feature Reduction
154
Figure B.1: Optimization of lambda parameter for feature selection.
The regularization parameter chosen is the one that produces the lowest over-
all loss using 5-fold cross-validation on training data.
This resulted in best
λ = 7.112 × 10−55 having a average loss of 0.0886.
In figure B.2 below, the NCA, can be seen. The NCA is performed using the
optimal regularization parameter. In the plot, the features are rated in terms of
weight.
==PAGE:169==
B.3 Model Complexity
155
Figure B.2: Neighbourhood Component Analysis of features. Feature weight
and index are displayed in order to determine best features to be used for mod-
elling and classification.
Reduction of features can be done in terms of threshold, where only features
having a weight over a certain threshold should be used for modeling. However,
it should be noted that using this method or other methods for feature reduction,
which might be based on modeling very different from the intended classification
algorithm, might cause important features to be discarded. The test set is used
to validate, yielding a loss of = 0.0985 when performing reduction of features.
B.3
Model Complexity
When increasing the model complexity one has to take into account the bias-
variance tradeoff, seen in figure B.3. When the model complexity increase, the
variance tends to increase and the squared bias tends to decrease and the opposite
behavior occurs as the model complexity decrease. The optimal model complexity
would then be to trade bias off with variance in such a way as to minimize the
test error.
==PAGE:170==
B.3 Model Complexity
156
Figure B.3: Bias-Variance trade-off, showing model complexity as a function of
error. When the bias becomes too large the model overfits to the data.
Figure B.4 shows the typical behavior of the test and training error when the
model complexity is increased or decreased. The training error tends to decrease
whenever the model complexity is increased, that is when the data is fitted even
harder. However, when the data is fitted too hard, the model adapts itself too
closely to the training data, and will not generalize well, which will increase the
test error. Inversely when the model is not complex enough, it will underfit and
may have a large bias, which again will result in poor generalization [74]. Figure
10.9 shows this trend where the neural network complexity is being increased
but only the training error is going down, while the test error remains the same,
increasing the complexity further would result in worse test error, hence why the
model is done training at this point as indicated on the figure.
==PAGE:171==
B.4 Neural Network Hidden Layers
157
Figure B.4: Test and training error as a function of model complexity.
B.4
Neural Network Hidden Layers
The maximal number of hidden layers for a given network can be computed using
equation B.1
Nh =
Ns
(α × (Ni + No))
(B.1)
where Ns is the number of samples in the training data, Ni is the number of
input neurons, Ni is the number of input neurons, and α is an arbitrary scaling
factor, usually between 2-10.
Using our training data and an α = 5, the maximal number of hidden layers for
the classification problem presented in this paper is determined to be
Nh =
6978960
(5 × (60 + 1)) = 22880

1 Introduction 
 
This document covers a description and specification of the “In-Ear measuring prototype 
SmartRITE Sleep Recorder” produced by Oticon. The purpose of the prototype is to do in-ear 
measurement of sleep disordered breathing and store the data. Furthermore it is connected, by 
wire, to a Recorder box, which will be located on the chest of the patient. The in-ear part of the 
prototype will be a standard Oticon hearing aid, with the addition of a temperature and 
photoplethysmogram (PPG) sensor.  The Recorder box will consist of a microcontroller, battery 
and an accelerometer attached to the body using a chest strap.  
We consider the content of this Clinical Investigators Brochure as confidential information, 
intended for the Investigator and sub-investigator only, and request that it be treated as such by 
the Medical Products Agency.  
1.1 Sponsor and manufacturer of the SmartRITE Sleep Recorder 
The in-ear prototype is manufactured by Oticon A/S (Kongebakken 9, 2765 Smørum, Denmark). 
Oticon A/S is part of the William Demant Holding group that holds a group of hearing aid and 
diagnostics companies providing different types of hearing aids to millions of people each year. 
Furthermore the prototype is made in collaboration with Rigshospitalet, Glostrup, and Technical 
University of Denmark (DTU), Lyngby. 
 
 
E.2 Investigators Brochure
171
==PAGE:186==
Page 4 of 17 
2 Device information – SmartRITE Sleep Recorder 
2.1 Overall description 
In-ear system with optics and digital thermometer attached to external Recording box containing 
the rest of the system setup encased in a 3D-printed box. The in-ear design has the approximate 
dimensions of the already existing Oticon hearing aids. The external Recording box is meant to be 
placed on the chest, having the approximate size of 100 x 70 x 20 mm. Below are listed 
components of the system, setup and specifications.  
2.2 Components

o Microcontroller: Adafruit M0 feather

2.3 Setup 
The proposed prototype is based on the design of regular hearing aid with added modalities. The 
Maxim PPG sensor is attached to the in-ear piece, which also includes a temperature gauge. In the 
proposed design, the in-ear piece is connected to an external tri-axial accelerometer placed in the 
Recording box at the chest. 
Figure 1: In-Ear piece (left) and external hearing aid part (right) 
Figure 2: Recording box to be attached by a strap to the chest 
E.2 Investigators Brochure
173
==PAGE:188==
Page 6 of 17 
 
Figure 3: More pictures of the complete system 
The hearing aid is connected to the Recording box on the chest with a detachable wire for easy 
swapping during testing, setup and recording of a subject. The Recording box contains an SD-card 
next to the microcontroller, Adafruit M0 feather, for data storage. Furthermore, a Bluetooth 
module can be included for live testing of data. 
2.3.1 Sensors 
Three sensors are included in the prototype. The Maxim MAX30101 is the optical sensor used in 
this study. It works by illuminating the skin and then measuring the light intensity with an infrared, 
green and red wavelength. 
 
 
Figur 4: Functional Diagram and picture of the Maxim MAX30101 sensor. 
 
 
E.2 Investigators Brochure
174
==PAGE:189==
Page 7 of 17 
 
The tri-axel accelerometer from InvenSense MPU-6050 is a high accuracy accelerometer with a 
high sampling rate.  
 
 
Figur 5: Block Diagram ash picture of the InvenSense MPU-6050 Accelerometer. 
 
The PCT2202 thermometer from NXP is an accurate digital thermometer: 
 
Figure 6: PCT2202 Temperature Sensor from NXP 
 
2.4 Classification 
 
The classification is done according to FDA and European classification schemes for medical 
devices. The SmartRITE Sleep Recorder is determined to be a medical device type I. The device has 
to follow the harmonized standards and regulations, which are applicable under the Council 
Directive 93/42/EEC of 14 June 1993 concerning medical devices.
