2.5.4 Mechanism of action for the SmartRITE Sleep Recorder 
A photoplethysmogram (PPG) have two components, a light source that illuminates the skin, and a 
photodetector which measures vibration in light intensity associated with changes in perfusion. 
The light is emitted from a red, a green and an infrared Light Emitting Diode, LED. The figure below 
shows how a reflectance pulse oximeter works. 
 
 
Figure 11: PPG mechanism of action [https://www.researchgate.net/figure/9-Reflectance-pulse-oximeter-measuring-the-amount-
of-light-reflected-back-to-the-probe-in_fig1_278711880] 
 
2.5.5 Storage and handling of the SmartRITE Sleep Recorder 
The SmartRITE Sleep Recorder is non-sterile and is only to be handled by the trained professionals 
carrying out the experiment. 
 
2.5.6 Clinical performance 
Results/measurements from similar or comparable studies using same sensors or modalities have 
not been found.

2.7 Existing clinical data 
 
No clinical data has been recorded using the device and prototype setup proposed in this 
investigative brochure. 
3 Risk management 
The benefits of the SmartRITE Sleep Recorder come without added risks compared to modern 
polysomnography, PSG, readings which is the golden standard for detecting sleep events. The 
residual risks of the SmartRITE Sleep Recorder are the same as for standardized PSG readings. An 
assessment of risk has been done by the use of risk analysis. The risk analysis can be found in 
Appendix A. 
4 Standards 
The SmartRITE Sleep Recorder has been developed in compliance with the following harmonized 
standards under the Council Directive 93/42/EEC of 14 June 1993 concerning medical devices. 
• EN ISO 10993-1:2009 Biological evaluation of medical devices - Part 1: Evaluation and 
testing within a risk management process 
• EN ISO 14971:2012 Medical devices - Application of risk management to medical devices 
• EN 60601-1:2006 Medical electrical equipment - Part 1: General requirements for basic 
safety and essential performance

1. Purpose 
Purpose of this document is to create a report for a device being used in device that is classified as a 
medical device. It documents that no biological risk is present or cause any adverse reaction when 
using the instrument/device defined in section 3. 
 
2. Scope 
This document contains all the relevant information or links to it that are needed to make a biological 
evaluation report that complies with the requirements defined by EN ISO 10993-1:2009/AC: 2010 
(abbreviated as ISO 10993-1).

3. Specific Device Information

SmartRITE Sleep Recorder 
Drawing or Picture of Device 
 
 
Intended use and how used 
In-ear monitoring of PPG signal to extract/calculated heart rate and oxygen saturation 
(SpO2) and temperature, furthermore, acceleration from chest to monitor respiration. 
 
The black hearing instrument is worn behind the ear on the side of the head and the 
whitish box is worn on the body. The two parts are connected with the black cable with 
the blue plugs. The end user will wear the device for in the night when sleeping. 
Categorization of instrument according to ISO 10993-1

5. Manufacturing/Processing  
The raw material is purchased in stock shapes and used for manufacturing parts as received. Parts 
are made using micro milling to obtain the complex geometries. No lubricants or other process 
additives are used during micro milling. After micro milling the parts are first grit blasted and then 
polished to obtain a uniform smooth surface. Grit blasting is performed with an inorganic inert grit. The 
polishing is performed using a polishing mop made of cotton without the using of any polishing wax. 
To ensure no contamination a completely new polishing mop was selected for polishing.  
 
6. Tissue contact 
The instrument is intended to be worn in skin contact on the side of the head during all the end users 
waking hours. 
 
7. Evaluation of ISO 10993-1 compliance 
The biocompatibility of the device has been verified and validated according to ISO 10993-1, 
Biological evaluation of medical devices and relevant sub-standards. 
 
Device has been classified according to EN ISO 10993-1:2009/AC: 2010, Biological Evaluation of 
Medical Devices as surface devices having skin contact for above 30 days.  
 
For each material in contact with the end user it has been ensured that the materials fulfil the 
requirements of ISO 10993-1 by assessing the materials individually. When the assessment has 
resulted in biological safety testing is needed to determine biocompatibility then this has been 
documented by having the three biological safety tests described in table just below conducted or by 
ensuring that the supplier/manufacturer of the materials has supplied information documenting 
compliance to ISO 10993-1 for permanent skin contact.  
 
Relevant Biological Safety Tests 
Test 
Standard 
Model 
Cytotoxicity 
ISO 10993-5 
In vitro cytotoxicity 
Irritation or Intracutaneous Reactivity ISO 10993-10 
In vivo irritation 6.3 – 6.5 
Sensitization 
ISO 10993-10 
Skin sensitizing potential using a guinea pig 
assays (7.3-7.6) 
Pass/fail criteria for each of the required are as specified in the standards where the requirement is that 
the materials must not show potential for the effect being tested against. 
 
Biological safety testing of parts/materials has been performed been conducted on materials 
according to these principles: 
• 
Tested materials must always be final sales ready parts that consist of all the correct materials 
including the lacquer and have been processed the same way as mass production using the 
same production conditions.

• 
Parts/materials in a specific test have been chosen based on the biological safety evaluation 
plan for the specific product in the development project. The biological safety evaluation plan 
includes investigation of all parts and the materials they consist of that then end user can 
come into contact with during normal use. Materials that previously have been used in other 
product in a similar part type manufactured using the same process with test documentation 
showing skin contact biocompatibility are considered approved for usage in the new product. 
So essentially only parts consisting of new materials or parts manufactured in a new process 
are tested for specific product. If the new material(s) is part of an assembly the entire 
assembly is tested even though most of the materials in the assembly previously have been 
tested. Therefore the new part(s)/material(s) are in general overrepresented when tested since 
it is only a small part of the final assembled product. This testing principle has been 
implemented to avoid unnecessary tests, to ensure interactions in assemblies caused by the 
combination of materials or the assembly manufacturing process are examined and to ensure 
the suitability with regards to biocompatibility of new materials. 
• 
When more than one part is combined in a test the ratio between the parts always 
corresponds to the ratio which they are present in the final assembled product. 
• 
Test performed on individual raw materials in unprocessed state are only used for screening 
purpose never used for approval in of a specific part or material in the final product. 
 
For some materials we have decided to use documentation for biocompatibility supplied by the 
manufacturer/supplier. The documentation is always carefully analysed to ensure that the materials 
are in compliance with the requirements for permanent skin contact in either European Medical 
Device Directive 93/42/EEC (which for biocompatibility refers to ISO 10993) or in ISO 10993-1 or that 
it contains documentation for passed tests for cytotoxicity, irritation and sensitisation. The 
methodology where usage of manufacturer/supplier documentation has been found to be sufficient is 
for the different raw material types dependent on how they are processed. For the different types of 
raw materials the following processing criteria must be fulfilled: 
Thermoplastic 
materials: 
No additives can be added. No processing additives such as mould lubricants that 
might be incorporated into the final part can be used. 
Thermoset/crossli
nking rubbers 
Must be cured according to the conditions specified by the manufacturer. Curing 
agents used must either be removed during post curing or be documented to be 
part of the manufacturer tested material. No additives that changes the final 
composition of the material be may added. 
Thermosetting 
adhesives 
Must be cured according to the conditions specified by the manufacturer in order 
to ensure a complete cure. For two component materials the correct manufacturer 
mixing ratio must be used. 
Light curing resins 
(mould and 
lacquer 
materials):  
Materials are used and processed according to the conditions specified by the 
supplier/manufacturer and the how handle, use and process is documented 
through the appropriate work instructions. During development materials of this 
type are examined to ensure that our internal processing results in materials/parts 
with properties corresponding to suppliers/manufacturers 
 
No interaction of materials used for the parts in the final assembled product that might give rise to 
leachables or to materials composition change take place due the assembly process and the 
materials used. Assembly process of the individually tested parts to the final product is done 
mechanically meaning that all parts in skin contact are either jointed by a click feature or kept in place 
by a pin or a screw leaving the parts in close contact with each other but not linked completely such 
as adhesive joint or a moulded joint would be.  
 
Furthermore all the materials used for the different instrument parts are at storage and usage 
temperature in solid state and considered stable either because of their thermoplastic nature or 
thermosetting materials/crosslinking rubbers that have been processed to be completely cured. The

stability/suitability of the materials in the different parts has been verified during the reliability testing of 
the final product where no change in materials that leads to performance degradation is allowed. 
 
8. Device Mechanical Failure Impact on Biological Safety 
All testing and evaluation has been performed on new parts or complete functioning device and no 
testing has been performed on mechanically failed devices. The reason for not doing that is that any 
severe mechanical failure will cause the device not to function as intended and since it is a skin 
contact device the end user can remove it without any kind of issues. Furthermore all the materials 
and components used for the device are used in solid state where no additional release of substances 
can be expected even if the any of components is severely mechanically damaged. 
 
9. Accordance with Biological Safety Evaluation Plan  
Biological Safety Evaluation Plan: BSEP-2018.01 SmartRITE Sleep Recorder 
Biological Risk Assessment: 
No biological risk of using the Fusion SP can be found. The parts in skin 
contact are all made of the same material and since this material has 
been evaluated to risk free for use in skin contact according to ISO 
10993-1 (see Doc-00076972_Bio for details) and the processing does 
not change the composition of the material and therefore of the parts that 
the device consists of. 
 
10. Conclusion 
Since all the materials and therefore parts used in skin contact during normal use for the instrument in 
the SmartRITE Sleep Recorder device according to the section 4 in this report all can be considered 
biocompatible because they comply with the requirements in ISO 10993-1 the entire device can be 
considered biocompatible for permanent skin contact. Therefore the device complies with the 
requirements from IEC 60601-1 and directive 93/42/EEC, Annex I

APPENDIX F
State of the Art
Review
F.1
Hearables and Photoplethysmography
”Cardiovascular Monitoring Using Earphones and a Mobile Device”,
Poh et al., IEEE Pervasive Computing, 2012 [24].
In this study, the MIT team suggests a socially acceptable earphone sensor to
avoid social awkwardness when monitoring cardiovascular activity long-term. Al-
though not often emphasized there is a social aspect to designing wearable com-
puters and biosensors, people do not want to look odd, which Poh et al. tries to
address in their ”heartphones”. The classic white Apple earphones are modified
with a reflective photosensor for heart rate monitoring using PPG.
To evaluate the system’s accuracy the authors collected data from 31 healthy
subjects with the heartphone and compare them with ECG, first standing sta-
tionary for five minutes and then in a subsequent experiment the system was
tested during physical activity. The subjects were asked to sit quietly on a re-
cumbent bicycle for two minutes followed by five minutes of cycling and then
finally a two minutes rest period.
During the stationary standing, the PPG signal was in close agreement with the
reference ECG signal. For every QRS complex detected, there was a correspond-
ing peak of the peripheral pulse that was clearly distinguishable. For the cycling,
the blood volume pulse waveform remained clean, and the peaks are still clearly
discernable and synchronous with the QRS complex from the ECG.
Frequent and noninvasive cardiovascular monitoring is important in surveillance
for cardiovascular catastrophes and treatment therapies of chronic disease. There-
fore, the development of wearable biosensors suitable for comfortable and contin-
uous cardiovascular assessment could change the future of medicine by producing
exquisitely detailed individual physiological data. Despite significant advances in
wearable technology in the past few years, wearable computers still struggle for
==PAGE:210==
F.1 Hearables and Photoplethysmography
196
social acceptance. It was concluded that the wearable biosensors present a conve-
nient platform for cardiovascular monitoring that promises social acceptance and
widespread adoption outside of the lab. Moreover, the system can be extended
to measure other physiological parameters such as breathing rate, and arterial
blood oxygen saturation, which are crucial for cardiovascular and general vital
signs monitoring.
”A Feasibility Study Evaluating Innovative In-ear Pulse Oximetry for
Unobtrusive Cardiovascular Homecare Monitoring during Sleep”, Ven-
ema et al., IEEE Point-of-Care Healthcare Technologies, 2013 [12].
Venema et al.
demonstrates that heart rate, arterial oxygen saturation, and
respiratory-related information can be measured with reliable accuracy for clin-
ical appliance and may allow diagnosis of sleep apnea in the future. Data were
recorded in a sleep laboratory on patients with a clinical suspicion of sleep apnea,
with an in-ear reflective pulse-oximetric sensor. The PPG sensor is sealed into
an individual customized, biocompatible, ear mold, for optimal comfort of wear
and signal recording.
The in-ear PPG data was correlated with the standard PSG measurements and
demonstrate that nocturnal long-term measurement of oxygen saturation and
heart rate is possible with sufficient accuracy. Achieving a high SNR between
26-36 dB and a high correlation when comparing in-ear PPG to the golden stan-
dard PSG.
”The human ear canal: investigation of its suitability for monitoring
photoplethysmographs and arterial oxygen saturation”, Budidha et al.,
Institute of Physics and Engineering in Medicine, 2014 [25].
Budidha et al. carried out a pilot study on 15 healthy volunteers to validate
the feasibility of measuring PPGs and SpO2 from the ear canal while comparing
them with the same signals from the left index finger and the right index finger
in conditions of induced peripheral vasoconstriction (right hand immersed in ice
water). It is a well-known fact that SpO2 measurements made from extremities
such as the finger, earlobe and toes become susceptible to inaccuracies when pe-
ripheral perfusion is compromised. A dual wavelength PPG sensor is used (red
and infrared) from which the SpO2 is derived. The raw PPG signals were filtered
using a band-pass filter. To acquire the ac component of the signal Budidha et al.
used a fourth order Butterworth filter with an upper and lower cut-off frequency
at 5 and 0.5 Hz respectively, furthermore a second order Butterworth low-pass
filter with a cut-off frequency of 5Hz were used to extract the dc component.
==PAGE:211==
F.1 Hearables and Photoplethysmography
197
Good quality PPG signals with high signal-to-noise ratio were obtained from the
prototype ear canal and finger probes. The morphology of the PPG signals ob-
tained from the ear canal was slightly different to the PPG signals acquired from
the finger sensors, however, all the signals correspond accurately with the cardiac
cycle. These changes in the morphology of the PPG signals are thought to be due
to variations in vascular compliance and peripheral resistance of various arteries.
This is expected, as the ear is closer to the heart than the finger, the time it takes
for arterial pulsations to travel from the heart to the ear canal is less than the
time taken for the pulsations to reach the finger.
The average drop in amplitude of red and infrared PPG signals from the right
index finger, when immersed in ice water, was 52.7% and 58.3%. Similarly, the
left index finger PPG signal amplitudes have reduced by 47.52% and 46.8% re-
spectively. In contrast, no significant changes were seen in the red and infrared
ear canal PPG amplitude measurements, which changed by +2.5% and −1.2%
respectively. The right index finger and left index finger pulse oximeters have
failed to estimate accurate SpO2 in seven and four volunteers respectively, while
the ear canal pulse oximeter has only failed in one volunteer. These results sug-
gest that the ear canal may be a suitable site for reliable monitoring of PPGs
and SpO2s even in the presence of peripheral vasoconstriction.
”Robustness, Specificity, and Reliability of an In-Ear Pulse Oximetric
Sensor in Surgical Patients”, Venema et al., IEEE Journal of Biomed-
ical and Health Informatics, 2014 [11].
In this paper, a trial was performed on 28 adult patients undergoing surgery.
In-ear SpO2 data are compared with SaO2 obtained by blood gas analysis, and
with three reference pulse oximeters applied to the finger, earlobe, and forehead.
Additionally, a SpO2-independent perfusion index by means of the wavelengths
used was derived.
Since the pulse oximetry is recorded by an in-ear sensor, the peripheral perfu-
sion is not affected, which might happen when using common sensors placed on
peripheral body parts such as the finger or earlobe.
The feasibility and robustness of in-ear SpO2 measurement is demonstrated under
challenging clinical conditions. SpO2s shows good accordance with SaO2, a high
level of comparability with the reference pulse oximeters and was significantly
improved by introducing a new algorithm for artifact reductions. The perfusion
index also shows a good correlation with the reference data.
Despite the fact that the in-ear pulse oximetric sensor was exposed to one of the
most challenging conditions for medical product: a test during the entire dura-
tion of 28 surgical procedures and including a wide spectrum of varying situations
==PAGE:212==
F.1 Hearables and Photoplethysmography
198
in different clinical rooms. A standard deviation for the SpO2 of −2.1 ± 4.9%,
after applying a new artifact reduction strategy, compared to the golden stan-
dard, blood gas analysis, was achieved which are impressive results for a scientific
prototype.
”In-Ear Photoplethysmography for Mobile Cardiorespiratory Monitor-
ing and Alarming”, Venema et al., IEEE 12th International Conference
on Wearable and Implantable Body Sensor Networks, 2015 [26].
In this paper Venema et al. propose a novel, reflective PPG sensor system that
can be worn in the ear channel. The system evaluates the feasibility of a compre-
hensive cardiorespiratory monitoring, it looks at heart rate and the feasibility of
a breathing cycle classification which is demonstrated by means of a Naive Bayes’
classifier. Furthermore, possibilities for an automatic detection of heart arrhyth-
mias as an alarming system for people with cardiovascular risk is discussed. Two
different types of human studies were performed for clinical evaluation. A human
hypoxia study where the SpO2 signal from the pulse oximeter is calibrated ac-
cording to the regression with SaO2 reference values. The SaO2 was influenced
by changing the relation of oxygen to nitrogen by means of a breathing mask.
It was varied step-wise in a physiologically relevant range from 70% to 100%.
The second study was a recompensation study where 26 patient which suffered
from cardiac insufficiency were used in order to develop an automatic arrhythmia
detection algorithm. Using a Naive Bayes’ classifier it was possible to make a
respiratory cycle classification using a combination of signal amplitude variation
with heart rate variability for robust breathing cycle estimation. The moments
of inspiration and expiration were estimated, scoring a sensitivity and specificity
of 81.4% and 86% respectively. The heart activity was measured with a minimal
error of 1.2 heartbeats per minute and a regression coefficient of 0.9975 compared
with ECG. In human trials including patients who suffer from heart insufficiency,
it could be demonstrated that also cardiological alarming is feasible without indi-
vidual calibration. Based on PPG amplitude and frequency variation, a feature
space is defined. The separability of normal heart rhythm and heart insufficiency
could clearly be demonstrated.
By means of this results, an automatic heart
alarming system could be realized that might be life-saving, especially regarding
a continuous monitoring during sleep with risk patients (homecare).
”Motion Artifact Removal from Photoplethysmography based on Weighted
Band-pass Filter”, Suzuki et al., Industrial Electronics Society, 2015
[27].
In this paper, a method for reducing motion artifacts from PPG measured from an
==PAGE:213==
F.2 Accelerometer
199
in-ear sensor is presented. Because the motion artifacts and the main frequency
components of PPG are both within the same frequency band, simply band-pass
filtering will not remove the motion noise. Therefore, a two-stage motion artifact
reduction filter consisting of a weighted band-pass filter and adaptive filter is
proposed. The weighted band-pass filter removes moving average by emphasizing
and suppressing the frequencies within 0.5-4.0 Hz, which is the band-pass domain
for PPG signals. Then the output of the filtered signal is applied with an adaptive
filter to recover its amplitude and consequently, a motion artifact suppressed PPG
signal is obtained.
Evaluation of the proposed method was implemented in a computer simulation.
PPG was measured with the in-ear sensor at rest, with closed eyes, this is used
as the ground truth data. Afterward, 0.5-10 Hz noise was added to the signal,
followed by the proposed filtering. The ground truth pulse rate was measured
to 54.1 ± 11.1 bps, and the output signal of the proposed method was given as
56.6 ± 2.4 bps concluding the method to be satisfactory.
”Hearables: Multimodal physiological in-ear sensing”, Goverdovsky et
al., Scientific Reports, 2017[28].
In this study, Goverdovsky et al. developed a multimodal physiological in-ear
device to track the neural and physiological functions of a user over long periods
of time. The earpiece benefits from being in a relatively stable position of the ear
canal with respect to vital organs. It is shown that the multimodal sensor can
robustly measure the brain, cardiac and respiratory functions. Furthermore, it is
shown that combining data from multiple sensors within an integrated wearable
device improves both the accuracy of measurements and the ability to deal with
artifacts in real-world scenarios. It is shown that earpieces based on memory foam
ensure a comfortable fit and straightforward insertion, together with reduced me-
chanical artifacts from pulsatile ear canal movements due to blood vessel pulsa-
tion. The electret condenser microphones (ECM), which is integrated into the
viscoelastic earpiece, provides mechanical disturbance measurements within the
multimodal sensor to deal with motion artifacts and has the ability to record
speech and breathing activity from inside the ear canal. This has proven to give
good EEG signals, with help from the artifact removal of the microphone.
F.2
Accelerometer
”An Accelerometer-Based Device for Sleep Apnea Screening”, Sánchez
Morillo et al., IEEE Transactions on Information Technology in Biomedicine,
==PAGE:214==
F.2 Accelerometer
200
2010 [29].
In this paper, an accelerometer-based device is designed in order to screen for
sleep apnea in patients suspected of having sleep apnea. The study consisted of
15 adult patients who were monitored overnight using standard PSG readings as
well as a body-fixed-accelerometer placed at the suprasternal notch. Accelerom-
eter data was measured using a sample rate of 8192 Hz.
The accelerometer data was first passed through a 30th-order lowpass FIR filter
to remove noise. A 40th-order lowpass filter with a Hamming window was used
in subsequent analysis, using a cutoff frequency 1 Hz, in order to extract the res-
piratory signal, since normal respiration rate varies between 6 to 30 breaths/min
(0.1-0.5 Hz) [29]. Respiratory rate from the accelerometer signal was computed
using autocorrelation on segments containing 75 s of data each.
However, even though estimation of respiration rate computed by the accelerom-
eter was done with low error, the data collected was not correlated with the PSG
airflow signals. The study indicates the ability to provide cheap and portable
system design for a screening of sleep apnea using an accelerometer-based device,
but requires further work.
”Accelerometer Data Preparation For Activity Recognition”, Gjoreski
et al., In Proceedings of the International Multiconference Informa-
tion Society, 2011 [30].
In this study, a system for automatic recognition of activity using wearable ac-
celerometer sensors is investigated. The study is primarily focused on methods
for processing, denoising and extracting movement data from 3-axis accelerome-
ter signals. The dataset used consists of 11 people, performing two different test
scenarios including different activities.
Movements and signals sensed by the accelerometer were categorized into seven
different target activities. In order to use the data, a lowpass filter was applied
in order to smooth out the signal. In addition, a highpass filter was employed
in order to remove low-frequency variations, which also allows for elimination of
the gravity component, in order only to detect sudden changes in acceleration
[30]. 25 attributes were computed from the filtered accelerometer data, using
overlapping sliding windows.
Activity recognition was classified into the seven activities using a machine learn-
ing classifier. Random Forest was the chosen algorithm for this study. In order
==PAGE:215==
F.2 Accelerometer
201
to assess the performance of the algorithm, leave-one-out cross-validation was
used. The F-measure evaluation showed that a single accelerometer achieved a
93% detection accuracy.
”Monitoring torso acceleration for estimating the respiratory flow and
efforts for sleep apnea detection”, Dehkordi et al., 2012 Annual Inter-
national Conference of the IEEE Engineering in Medicine and Biology
Society, 2012 [31]
In this paper, ensemble learning is used in order to estimate respiratory flow,
thoracic and abdominal respiratory effort, using an accelerometer placed a the
suprasternal notch. Flow estimations were used in order to detect the cessation
of breathing and classify these cessations into either obstructive or central sleep
apnea. The subjects of this study consist of 17 men and 2 women, all non-smoker
between 27 to 35 years of age.
Accelerometer data was filtered using a bandpass filter using the cutoff frequencies
0.1 and 10 Hz and downsampled to 20 Hz in order to remove baseline fluctua-
tions [31]. Subsequently, Savitzky-Golay smoothing was employed on segments
containing 100 ms of data.
In order to estimate the respiratory flow, two re-
gression models were customized for each of the involved subjects, employing a
non-causal FIR model and a neural network model respectively. Finally, both
models were combined using simple average linear combiner.
In order to assess the performance of the designed setup, well-established nasal-
oral flow measurement methods attached to the subjects were used as a reference.
Using the estimated flow from the accelerometer sensor, a flow estimation error
was calculated. This system proved to have an overall mean error of 16% for es-
timation of flow. This study highlights the possibilities of providing easy-to-use
and economic alternatives for long-time measurements of respiratory function in
order to aid in the diagnosis of sleep apnea.
”Ambulatory respiratory rate detection using ECG and a triaxial ac-
celerometer”, Chan et al., 2013 35th Annual International Conference
of the IEEE Engineering in Medicine and Biology Society (EMBC),
2013 [16].
This article investigates the possibility of performing continuous monitoring of
respiratory in an ambulatory setting. This is done using patch sensors collecting
ECG and 3-axis accelerometer data, using a sampling rate of 125 Hz. The data
streamed in real time using BLE in order to display and store data. The data
==PAGE:216==
F.2 Accelerometer
202
consists of measurements performed on 15 subjects between 63 and 79 years of
age.
Respiration rate is determined by combining respiration rates, calculated using
ECG and accelerometer data respectively, using a weighted average of the sig-
nals based on signal qualities. In order to obtain the respiration signal from the
accelerometer data, raw data is filtered using a 3-stage elliptical lowpass biquad
filter having a passband corner frequency of 0.7 Hz, passband ripple of 1 dB and
stopband corner frequency at 0.9 Hz. The attenuation in the stop band is of 30
dB magnitude. Filtered data were downsampled and projected into a respiration
vector. Subsequently, a peak finding algorithm was employed to detect respira-
tion rate in the projected accelerometer data. This was done using a 45-second
window being shifted every 5 seconds.
The performance of respiratory rate estimation is evaluated by comparing the re-
sults with the well-established capnography-derived reference signal. The study
proves that it is possible to obtain good respiratory rate estimates using both
ECG and accelerometer data individually and combined. The performance showed
a mean absolute error of 1.02 breaths per minute (BrPM) during metronome
breathing, and 1.67 BrPM during spontaneous breathing. This highlights the
possibilities of using accelerometer sensors in order to detect respiratory rate.
”Real-time apnea detection using pressure sensor and tri-axial accelerom-
eter”, Pierleoni et al., IEEE-EMBS International Conference on Biomed-
ical and Health Informatics (BHI), 2014 [32].
In this study, the authors propose a real-time system for fast and reliable detec-
tion of sleep apnea events. To do this, the system consists of both a pressure
sensor and a 3-axis accelerometer. The device is placed on the chest of the sub-
jects and fixated using a chest strap. Data was streamed wireless using Bluetooth
2.1 and sent to a PC for data storage and processing in MATLAB. The dataset
consists of 10 data acquisitions of 10 different subjects recorded for 2 hours each.
In order to perform detection of events, the noise was removed from the signal
using a 4th-order low-pass Butterworth filter with a cut-off frequency of 0.45 Hz.
Subsequently, each window of data is averaged and subtracted from the signal
data in order to remove spectral components at zero frequency. Following this,
Fast Fourier transform is applied to the filtered signal in order to calculate the
frequency components. Respiratory rate was then calculated by determining the
fundamental value of the frequency spectrum and analyzing the signals based on
the estimated respiratory rate.
==PAGE:217==
F.2 Accelerometer
203
In order to evaluate algorithm performance, the estimated rate of apneas and
events are compared to a well-established BioHarness™which was used as a ref-
erence. The results showed that events detected had a high correlation to the
reference signal, r > 0.85 and p < 0.01 with a sensitivity of a 100% when the
subject is in the sitting or lying position. The overall sensitivity for all positions
is 99%. The algorithm is able to perform detection of events in real-time with a
maximum delay of 10 s. This study proves that it is possible to perform reliable
and low-cost monitoring of apnea events, which could replace the use of more
complex and higher cost diagnostic methods.
”Portable prescreening system for sleep apnea”, M. K. Guul et al.,
2016 38th Annual International Conference of the IEEE Engineering
in Medicine and Biology Society (EMBC), 2016 [17].
In this study, a system is developed in order to perform screening of sleep ap-
nea patients and classify subjects as either belonging to a high risk or low risk
of having OSA. This is done by using an Android-based smartphone in order to
record 3-axis acceleration and sound signals from the phone. The phone is placed
at the sternum and fastened to the subject by using a chest strap. The data is
sampled using a sample rate of 16 kHz for the audio signal and 10 Hz for the
3-axis accelerometer. Data is stored directly on the phone using self-developed
application and later loaded into a MATLAB GUI for signal presentation and
processing. Initial system test was performed using three test subjects having full
night recordings. Features for detection of breathing was based on measurements
from 5 other test subjects.
In order to detect respiratory rate, the accelerometer data was filtered using a
6th-order Butterworth band-pass filter, using the cutoff frequencies of 0.1 Hz and
0.8 Hz. The data from the axes are merged using principal component analysis
in order to determine weights and upsampled from 10 Hz to 40 Hz by using in-
terpolation. Subsequently, the filtered signals were used in order to determine
heart rate and respiratory rate. Respiratory rate was calculated by further filter-
ing accelerometer signals using A band-pass filter with the cutoff frequencies 0.2
Hz and 0.45 Hz, computing the frequency spectrum and determine the rate by
determining the most predominant peak in frequency. In addition, accelerometer
data was used in order to determine sleep position of the subjects. The position
was classified into 5 categories by the use of decision tree.
In order to perform classification, classification results from accelerometer data
and audio was combined and use as a count for each frame, in order to catego-
==PAGE:218==
F.2 Accelerometer
204
rize frames of 0.25 s into ”breath taken” or ”no breath taken”. 10 seconds, 40
consecutive samples, being classified as ”no breath taken”, will to the detection
of apnea events. Performance of the classifier showed a 99.6% sensitivity, 97.8%
specificity, and 98.7% accuracy. this study shows that it is possible to perform
reliable detection of respiration and cessation of breathing to diagnose OSA using
integrated smartphone accelerometer and audio signals.
”Sleep apnea detection via smart phones”, Camcı et al., 2017 25th Sig-
nal Processing and Communications Applications Conference (SIU),
2017 [33].
In this paper, a smartphone based system for monitoring and detecting sleep ap-
nea is proposed. In order to do this, integrated phone sensor 3-axis accelerometer
is utilized as well as audio signals recorded by the microphone. The smartphone
is strapped to the subject using a chest-belt and placed on the diaphragm in
order to detect breathing patterns. The data is stored using available mobile ap-
plications. The data consists of measurements of four subjects each of 35 minutes
duration. The test subjects were instructed to imitate general apnea characteris-
tics.
Accelerometer data is sampled at a 100 Hz and features such as the mean, vari-
ance and range values are extracted. These features were used in order to detect
sleep apnea events. It was shown that these features generally increase when sleep
apnea events occur. In order to classify events, a K-Nearest Neighbor (KNN) and
Classification And Regression Trees (CART) algorithms are used. The KNN algo-
rithm showed a performance of 97.7% accuracy and the CART a 98.2% accuracy
when detecting sleep apnea events.
This study highlights the capabilities of a system solely relying on accelerometer
data in order to perform classification of events.
”SleepMonitor: Monitoring Respiratory Rate and Body Position Dur-
ing Sleep Using Smartwatch”, Sun et al., Proceedings of the ACM on
Interactive, Mobile, Wearable and Ubiquitous Technologies 1, 2017
[34].
In this study, the authors propose a smartwatch based system in order to de-
termine respiration rate and sleep position in order to monitor sleep in subjects
with suspected sleep disorders. This is done by data collection of the accelerom-
eter data integrated into the smartwatch. The accelerometer is a 3-axis with a
sampling rate of 16 Hz. Measurements were performed using 16 test subject and
==PAGE:219==
F.3 Sleep Apnea
205
resulted in 70 nights of collected sleep data. One of the people in the study is
previously diagnosed of having OSA.
Processing of accelerometer data has multiple steps.
In order to denoise the
signal, a total variation filter is employed. This filter is chosen over widely used
low-pass filters since this type of filter both removes high and low-frequency noise
while preserving the peaks and through which are related to respiratory signals.
FFT is applied to the filtered signal in order to estimate the dominant frequency
and thereby respiratory rate. Dominant frequencies for each axis are fused and
historical information is extracted and applied to accelerometer data using a
Kalman filter in order to perform reliable estimation of respiratory rate. In order
to detect position, accelerometer data from the smartwatch are compared to a
reference ”ground truth” placed on the test of the subjects. The position is clas-
sified into four different positions by using four different classification algorithms
Naive Bayes (NB), Bayesian Network (BN), Decision Tree (DT), and Random
Forest (RF), in order to determine the optimal classification technique.
Measurements from the smartwatch are compared to reference methods, where
respiration rate estimates show an overall mean absolute error of 0.72 beats per
minute. In order to evaluate the classification algorithms for body position, a
leave-one-out method is used. It is shown that Random Forest is the best per-
forming classifier showing a 90-95% classification accuracy for the four different
target positions. This paper present very useful methods for extracting respira-
tion signals as well as body position estimation.
F.3
Sleep Apnea
”Real-Time Sleep Apnea Detection by Classifier Combination”, Xie et
al., IEEE Transactions on Information Technology in Biomedicine 16,
2012 [36].
In this study, the aim is to develop alternative methods for the monitoring and
detection of sleep apnea. This is done using ECG signals and oxygen saturation
SpO2 measurements. The data comes from the University College Dublin Sleep
Apnea Database (UCD) which is available at Physionet. The dataset consists of
overnights recording of 25 subjects which are all suspected of having sleep disor-
ders. The ECG signal is sampled using a sampling rate of 128 Hz and the SpO2
sampled at 8 Hz.
==PAGE:220==
F.3 Sleep Apnea
206
The SpO2 is downsampled to 1 Hz, and artifacts are removed. In order to perform
detection, the 39 features are calculated from the oxygen saturation. A subset of
31 features is determined by employing feature selection in order to avoid over-
fitting, redundancy and complex classification. Several classification algorithms
were chosen and tested on the data. For each algorithm, performance was calcu-
lated both on the ECG and SpO2 signals individually as well as combined. Best
performance was achieved by the use of classifier combination, which achieved
sensitivity, specificity, and accuracy all around 82%.
This study highlights the possibilities of performing monitoring and detection of
apnea events only using a subset of the signals which are usually recorded using
the gold-standard PSG readings for diagnosing OSA. It is also shown that it is
possible to perform screening of OSA with relatively high accuracy using solely
SpO2 signals.
”Classifying obstructive sleep apnea using smartphone”, Al-Mardini et
al., Journal of Biomedical Informatics 52, 2014 [38].
In this paper, a low-cost, reliable, easy-to-use, and portable system for the screen-
ing of OSA is presented. The modalities used for prediction include oxygen satu-
ration, body movement, and respiratory effort. Tests on the system are performed
using 15 subjects, whereof 8 have a prior diagnosis of OSA.
Oxygen saturation is measured using a pulse oximeter and data is continuously
streamed to the smartphone. Apnea detection using these signals are defined as
a 4% deviation or higher from the patient oxygen saturation baseline computed
as a mean. In order to eliminate variation from pulse oximetry caused by body
movement, 3-axis accelerometer data is used in order to classify the patient as
moving or at rest, in order to determine if oxygen saturation estimates should
be included for further processing. Measurements performed on the test subjects
are compared to Gold Standard PSG readings.
The estimations performed by the proposed algorithm show promising results
and good correlation with actual signals. These estimates were used in order to
classify if the patient suffers from OSA or not. The system achieved a 100% sen-
sitivity and an 85.7% specificity. This demonstrates the possibility of low-cost
monitoring system relying on a subset of the PSG signals which can improve
monitoring and diagnosis of OSA.
”Monitoring Obstructive Sleep Apnea by means of a real-time mobile
system based on the automatic extraction of sets of rules through Dif-
==PAGE:221==
F.3 Sleep Apnea
207
ferential Evolution”, Sannino et al., Journal of Biomedical Informatics
49, 2014 [39].
In this study, the aim is to develop a simple, easy, low-cost, and reliable portable
monitoring device for patients who suffer from obstructive sleep apnea.
The
analysis is based only on single-lead ECG data. The apnea-ECG database comes
from Physionet and consists of 70 overnight recordings of patients diagnosed with
having OSA. The processing is done using a mobile real-time monitoring system
capable of displaying live data and generating apnea based alarms.
Apnea event detection is based on a rule-based approach. Extraction rules are
based on a differential evolution algorithm. The set of rules are based on the ex-
isting dataset and transferred to the mobile device to perform detection of events
on live data. Features such as heart rate, heart rate variability, respiration, and
other related signals are extracted from the ECG data and are used as input
in a decision layer. In the decision layer, the rule-based approach is applied to
incoming data and used in order to determine if any action is needed, such as
alarms or other warnings.
In order to determine the performance of the classification, the algorithm was
applied to several test sets, as well as the entire database. Performance of clas-
sification using the test set showed an accuracy of 92.26%, sensitivity of 82.14%,
and specificity of 79.40%. These results promote further investigation into the
field of portable, easy to use ECG sensors for mobile monitoring of OSA.
”Wearable SpO2 and sleep posture monitoring system for Obstructive
Sleep Apnea patients”, Brugarolas et al., 2015 IEEE Virtual Confer-
ence on Applications of Commercial Sensors (VCACS), 2015 [42].
In this article a training system is developed in order to monitor and alert pa-
tients of occurring oxygen desaturation and when sleeping a long time in the
supine position. The study utilizes a finger clip pulse oximeter in order to record
SpO2 and heart rate. In addition, an accelerometer placed on the chest of the
patient is used in order to track sleeping position. Data is streamed to MATLAB
for processing and a wristband is used in order to alert the patient to the above-
mentioned events.
The 3-axis accelerometer is sampled at a rate of 10 Hz. The accelerometer data
is used in order to predict sleep posture. The sleep posture prediction is based on
decision tree classifier trained on previously labeled data. The sleeping posture
is classified into 5 different target postures.
==PAGE:222==
F.3 Sleep Apnea
208
The finger clip pulse oximeter was used in order to determine oxygen saturation
and heart rate of the patient. The detection mechanism for determining desatura-
tion events is based on the apnea event definition of AASM, that the obstruction
lasts for 10 seconds or more and that the oxygen desaturation is 3% or more. In
order to reduce the influence of noise, a threshold level of 5% desaturation was
used.
The performance of the sleep posture prediction algorithm showed a sensitivity
between 99.59-100% and specificity between 99.77-100% for all 5 target postures
based on 3544 instances. Patient baseline estimation was used in order to detect
desaturation, and a desaturation lasting 8.2 seconds or more using the 5% thresh-
old is required in order to alert the patient of desaturation events.
”Comparison of standard and novel signal analysis approaches to ob-
structive sleep apnea classification”, Roebuck et al., Frontiers in Bio-
engineering and Biotechnology 3, 2015[43].
In this study, a new approach to speech analysis for obstructive sleep apnea clas-
sification is presented. Speech analysis is based on the audio signal analysis. The
data used in this study is based on 858 overnight recordings of patients diagnosed
of having OSA.
Several approaches were used in order to perform estimates and event detection.
Based on state of the art, analysis of baseline comparison utilizes linear discrimi-
nant analysis in order to discriminate between auditory events. A novel approach
which is based on multiscale entropy (MSE) is used on raw audio data. This ap-
proach is used with a random forest classifier in order to determine if patients are
in need of treatment or not. Five-fold cross-validation was performed on the data.
The linear discriminant analysis approach achieved an out-of-sample accuracy of
76.9%, sensitivity of 29.2%, and specificity of 88.7% for classification of auditory
events. The novel MSE approach achieved an out-of-sample accuracy of 79.9%,
sensitivity of 66.0%, and specificity of 88.8% for classification of apnea and treat-
ment. The results of this study show the potential of using audio signals for OSA
monitoring and classification.
”Portable obstructive sleep apnea detection and mobile monitoring”,
Çakmak et al., Proceedings of SPIE, 2017 [45].
In this study, a portable monitoring system for the detection of sleep apnea is
==PAGE:223==
F.3 Sleep Apnea
209
proposed. The system utilizes a thermocouple in order to record nasal and oral
respiratory information and a pulse oximeter in order to acquire oxygen satura-
tion and heart rate. The data is streamed using Bluetooth to an Android mobile
application in order to display real-time data. Test of measurements are per-
formed on 5 subjects.
The PPG data is sampled at a rate of 25 Hz and low-pass filtered using a cutoff
frequency of 3.2 Hz in order to remove noise and a subsequent low-pass filter
with a cutoff frequency of 0.6 Hz in order to remove the DC component. Ther-
mocouple data is sampled at a rate of 100 Hz and low-pass filtered with a cutoff
frequency of 1.06 Hz in order to extract respiratory signals.
In order to assess the performance of the measurements, data estimates per-
formed by the portable device are compared with measurements performed with
standard measurement devices. The results showed that the number of apneas
was detectable with a 100% accuracy, and estimation of oxygen saturation and
heart rate were performed with a 99% accuracy.

APPENDIX G
Pervasive Technology
Study
==PAGE:225==
1.
Introduction
The purpose of this study is to review and analyze the ﬁeld of in-ear health moni-
toring and screening. This is done by performing state of the art study of the most
prominent research ﬁelds and to identify chronic diseases which can be used in a
case study for monitoring. In addition, a prototype for a tri-modal monitoring
system will be presented. Lastly, the conclusions, choice of chronic disease and
problem statement will be presented.
1.1
Purpose & Collaboration
The aim of this paper is to present an overview of the research done as a part
of a premaster special course at the Technical University of Denmark with the
supervision of professor Helge B.D. Sørensen. The study is done in collaboration
with Oticon A/S and Rigshospitalet - Glostrup.
Medical hearing aid company, Oticon A/S, have been heavily involved in the de-
velopment of hardware and system prototypes and sensor selection, facilitated by
senior researcher Anders E. Petersen.
Professor and chief physician at Rigshospitalet - Glostrup, Poul Jennum, has been
the main source of knowledge regarding chronic disease pathophysiology and mon-
itoring, and have had a big impact on disease and signal selection.
The work done in this paper is meant to lay the groundwork for a master thesis
within the ﬁeld biomedical engineering, and based on the ﬁndings of this paper,
help determine scope and problem statement for further development.
1.2
Modalities
In this study, a tri-modal in-ear device is proposed.
The device is capable of
measuring photoplethysmography (PPG), acceleration, and temperature.
3
G Pervasive Technology Study
211
==PAGE:226==
January 23, 2018
1.2.1
Photoplethysmography
Photoplethysmography is a low-cost optical technique that can be used to detect
blood volume changes.
The PPG technique requires two components, a light
source to illuminate the skin, and a photodetector to measure variations in light
intensity associated with changes in perfusion.
PPG often uses red, green or
infrared wavelength, often obtained by a pulse oximeter.
PPG has many diﬀerent clinical settings, of which two will be used in this study,
blood oxygen saturation and heart rate[1].
Figure 1.1: The pulsatile component of the PPG signal and corresponding elec-
trocardiogram (ECG). The PPG signal represents a increasing light attenuation
associated with an increasing blood volume for each heartbeat [1].
1.2.2
Blood oxygen saturation
Blood oxygen saturation utilizes PPG measurements to obtain information about
the arterial blood oxygen saturation (SpO2). SpO2 is estimated by emitting light
through vascular tissue, by rapidly switching between red and infrared wave-
lengths. The SpO2 is calculated as follows:
SpO2 =
HbO2
HbO2 + Hb
Where HbO2 is oxygenated hemoglobin and Hb is deoxygenated hemoglobin.
There is a diﬀerence in light absorption of HbO2 and Hb at the two wavelengths,
which is used to calculate the SpO2.
Pulse oximeters can measure SpO2 using both the reﬂection and transmission
modes of operation, in this study only reﬂection modes will be evaluated.
4
G Pervasive Technology Study
212
==PAGE:227==
January 23, 2018
1.2.3
Heart rate
The AC component (pulsatile) of the PPG pulse is directly correlated with heart-
beats, hence it can be a source of heart rate information. The main problem with
estimating heart rate from PPG signal is that during signiﬁcant movements or
cardiac arrhythmia the reliability is signiﬁcantly reduced [1].
1.2.4
Temperature
Temperature is an important measurement of vital signs, and aids in the detection
of diseases.
1.2.5
Accelerometer
An accelerometer is used to measure accelerations of a body in reference to itself.
In this study, a tri-axial accelerometer is used.
1.3
Disease Monitoring
Monitoring of chronic disease can be of very high value in terms of diagnosis and
treatment. This can help in terms of monitoring healthy persons in order to detect
and perform early diagnosis of development of chronic diseases. It can also be used
in order for chronically ill patients to monitor the progression and status of their
chronic disease and aid in treatment.
The potential for long-term monitoring can help establish a baseline for healthy
and sick people, and to detect a deviation and possibly alert medical staﬀto the
development or exacerbation of chronic diseases. The potential for a multi-modal
system, composed of several sensors and sources of signals, can help to detect dif-
ferentiated symptoms based on the given disease. Such systems can also be used
in order to detect or classify the severity of the disease.
As opposed to devices used for monitoring at the hospitals and private clinics, this
monitoring system is meant to be used in the private sphere, allowing monitoring
at all times. This will improve system performance, prediction and allow a better
classiﬁcation of disease severity based on prior patient data and a given patient’s
baseline. Such systems have the advantage of large and longstanding data col-
lection, which can be used in large-scale analysis of chronic disease trends and
developments globally.
5
G Pervasive Technology Study
213
==PAGE:228==
January 23, 2018
1.4
Goals
The goals of this paper are to examine and analyze the ﬁeld of in-ear health
monitoring and to determine a chronic disease which can be used as a case study
for chronic disease monitoring using an in-ear device. The in-ear device will be
designed as a prototype system capable of recording the three diﬀerent modalities
listed in section 1.2. The goals listed below, are the result of the labour put into
analyzing this speciﬁc research area.
1. State of the art
• Review leading research and studies within diﬀerent ﬁelds of chronic
disease monitoring
• Assess the level of general development within each ﬁeld, methods, and
modalities
• Determine chronic disease for a case study based on ﬁndings
2. System design
• Perform sensor selection and determine sensor placement
• Develop system design including a microcontroller, battery, storage,
interconnection and more
• Develop system prototype and sensor testing
3. Problem statement
• Determine scope of research
• Create a problem statement based on the chosen chronic disease and
ﬁndings
• Determine further work and development of the system design for test-
ing

”Moving prediction of exacerbation in chronic obstructive pulmonary
disease for patients in telecare”, Jensen et al, Journal of Telemedicine
and Telecare 18, 2012[5]
In this study, researchers at Aalborg University investigated physiological data
in order to determine if they were able to predict chronic obstructive pulmonary
disease exacerbations. The study included longtime home measurement of a 111
people, whereof 57 patients were diagnosed with chronic obstructive pulmonary
disease (COPD). The remaining part of the test group was part of a control group.
The modalities used in this study for home measurement included FEV1, oxygen
saturation, blood pressure, heart rate, and weight/BMI. In the data 10 exacerba-
tions were detected in a total 9 patients.
In order to perform classiﬁcation 273 distinct features were extracted. These fea-
tures were used in a pattern recognition model in order to classify periods of
exacerbation and periods without exacerbation. In order to avoid overﬁtting to
the data, cross-validation using the leave-one-out method was used.
Using the best performing pattern recognition model, the researchers were able
to discriminate between periods with a sensitivity level of 70%, speciﬁcity level of
9
G Pervasive Technology Study
217
==PAGE:232==
January 23, 2018
95% and an AUC of 0.73. These results are based on prediction algorithm pre-
dicting the risk of exacerbation in a patient, within the next 30 days. This study
shows that it is possible to predict and thereby provide support for COPD patients
using a telemonitoring device. However, this study lacks further development, as
well as the data being slightly undersampled for accurate interpretation.
”Prediction of Chronic Obstructive Pulmonary Disease Exacerbation
using Physiological Time Series Patterns”, Xie et al, 35th Annual In-
ternational Conference of the IEEE, 2013[6]
In this paper, the aim is to predict COPD exacerbation based on patient data.
Data were obtained from seven patients all between 63-87 years of age at the Black-
town Hospital in Sydney, over a period of more than a year. On each patient, 6
measurements using diﬀerent modalities were performed daily. The measurements
used were weight, diastolic blood pressure, systolic blood pressure, heart rate, oxy-
gen saturation and temperature.
Features were extracted from the data and used for trend ﬁts in order to train
a classiﬁer in order to predict the onset of an exacerbation. In order to achieve
better performance, a subset of the most optimal of the original features was used.
In order to improve the model further, a double-loop ten-fold cross-validation was
performed.
The classiﬁer is constructed in this study is a regression-based machine learning
technique. The results show that the employed classiﬁer had an average accuracy
of 79.27% when classifying subjects into “low-risk” and “high-risk” categories, in-
dicating their risk of suﬀering a COPD exacerbation event.
”Predicting the risk of exacerbation in patients with chronic obstructive
pulmonary disease using home telehealth measurement data”, Mohktar
et al, Artif. Intell. Med. 63, 2015[7]
The aim of this paper is the development of an algorithm which is capable of pre-
dicting the onset of exacerbations in patients suﬀering from COPD. Patient data
were collected using a home monitoring device and send for remote analysis. The
test group consisted of 21 patients suﬀering from moderate to severe COPD.
Predictions are based on several modalities including FEV1, oxygen saturation,
heart rate, and weight. Features were extracted from these signals in order to
perform CART analysis. CART analysis is a nonparametric classiﬁer model based
10
G Pervasive Technology Study
218
==PAGE:233==
January 23, 2018
on regression tree structure. The purpose of the model is to classify data into
either ”high risk” or ”low risk” categories depending on the risk of suﬀering from
an exacerbation event in the near future.
Results of this study displayed the performance of the CART algorithm having a
classiﬁcation accuracy of 71.8%, with an 80.4% speciﬁcity and a 61.1% sensitivity.
These results were obtained using the model to predict a day ahead. This proves
that home monitoring and continuous prediction could have a big impact on the
early detection and management of COPD exacerbations.
”Computerised Analysis of Telemonitored Respiratory Sounds for Pre-
dicting Acute Exacerbations of COPD”, Fernandez-Granero et al, Sen-
sors Journal 15, 2015[8]
The purpose of this study was to provide remote early detection of acute exac-
erbations in patients suﬀering from COPD. This pilot study is based on 16 patients.
A single sensor in this project is used to record respiratory sounds. This is done
using an electret condenser microphone with analog to digital signal conversion.
The signal was preprocessed using an equi-ripple band-pass ﬁnite impulse response
ﬁlter of 100 to 2000 Hz with 80 dB attenuation. For feature selection, two diﬀer-
ent methods were used. The ﬁrst method consists of windowing followed by short
time Fourier transform, Mel-frequency cepstral coeﬃcients and discrete wavelet
transform. The second method is based on the Hilbert Huang transform.
The classiﬁcation was based on principal component analysis and development of
a support vector machine. The classiﬁer is used to perform early prediction of on-
set of exacerbations. The performance of this classiﬁer showed a 75.8% prediction
accuracy with an early detection of an average of 5 ± 1.9 days in advance.
”Assessing machine learning algorithms for self-management of asthma”,
Kocsis et al, The 6th IEEE International Conference on E-Health and
Bioengineering, 2017[9]
The purpose of this study is to provide an assessment of machine learning tech-
niques for self-management of asthma using a home support system. Control and
monitoring of disease progression in asthma patients could as for other respiratory
disorders provide valuable management of exacerbations and treatment.
Most of the data in this study are comprised of questionaries and examinations
11
G Pervasive Technology Study
219
==PAGE:234==
January 23, 2018
performed by a trained physicians. In addition, lung function tests, spirometry,
is also a big part of the data collection. In particular, FEV1, which is monitored
twice daily through home-spirometry, and FeNO, which is also monitored daily at
home. The data used consist of daily assessment of asthma control using a test
group of 39 patients.
The data was analyzed and classiﬁed using three distinct machine learning algo-
rithms which include support vector machine, random forest, and AdaBoost, in
order to determine the best performing algorithm. The results showed this was the
random forest algorithm providing an 84% prediction accuracy for asthma control
status. However, compared to the other articles in this section, this study is based
on limited self-monitoring.

subjects ECG data and alert when encountering irregular activity. This is done

”Straightforward and robust QRS detection algorithm for wearable car-
diac monitor”, Manikandan et al, Healthcare Technology Letters 1,
2014[11]
In this paper, the aim is to present a simple and robust QRS detection algorithm
for use in wearable cardiac monitoring systems. These algorithms can help pro-
vide better interpretation of cardiac signals acquired wireless for home monitoring.
The proposed detection algorithm consists of four methods. Firstly, sparsity ﬁlter-
ing of the raw signal. Secondly, smooth QRS feature extraction using squaring and
smoothing ﬁlters. Thirdly, peak ﬁnding using a Gaussian derivative ﬁlter. Lastly,
determining negative zero-cross location collection and peak location adjustment.
The algorithm was tested performing experiments on the standard MIT-BIH ar-
rhythmia database.The algorithm achieved an average sensitivity of 99.91% and
positive predictivity of 99.92%. Comparable to existing methods, this algorithm
provides improved detection performance under noisy conditions and on oddly
looking data.
”Ultra-Low Power, Secure IoT Platform for Predicting Cardiovascular
Diseases” Yasin et al, IEEE Transactions on Circuits and Systems–I:
Regular Papers 64, 2017[12]
The purpose of this paper is to develop an ultra-low power and system for predic-
tion of ventricular arrhythmia. The data in this study is based on public datasets.
One of the sets included is 5 single-lead normal ECG records available at Phys-
ioNet NSRDB.
13
G Pervasive Technology Study
221
==PAGE:236==
January 23, 2018
In order to perform detection of cardiovascular events, the system design is com-
posed of three main stages. Firstly, ECG is preprocessed by ﬁltering out baseline
drift, power line interference, and high-frequency noise. ECG-wave are delineated
and QRS detection is performed by using the Pan and Tompkins algorithm. Sec-
ondly, seven unique ECG-wave features and QRS complexes are extracted and
used for classiﬁcation. The classiﬁer is based on a Naive Bayes algorithm for event
detection.
The proposed system showed good performance being able to predict the onset of
an impending critical cardiovascular event with 86% accuracy. This performance
is based on predictions being made 3 hours prior to the time of the event.
