”A Neural Network System for Detection of Obstructive Sleep Apnea
Through SpO2 Signal Features”, Almazaydeh et al, International Jour-
nal of Advanced Computer Science and Applications, 2012[13]
14
G Pervasive Technology Study
222
==PAGE:237==
January 23, 2018
In this study, the authors develop and validate a neural network using SpO2 mea-
surements obtained from pulse oximetry to predict OSA. The data used in this
research was from PhysioNet containing 8 recordings with SpO2 signals.
The
recording varies in length, from less than seven hours up to ten hours each. The
sleep recording originated from 32 subjects, but unspeciﬁed the number of pa-
tients which were healthy and which had OSA. The authors use three diﬀerent
SpO2 features for classifying patients. The results show very good network val-
idation performance of 85% and high diagnostic performance with an accuracy
of 93.3% correct detection rate (sensitivity 87.5%, and speciﬁcity 100%) hence
concluding that neural network using SpO2 features is a useful screening test to
estimate whether patients have sleep apnea or not.
”Identiﬁcation of Sleep Apnea Events using Discrete Wavelet Trans-
form of Respiration, ECG and Accelerometer Signals”, Sweeney et al,
IEEEInternational Conference on Body Sensor Networks, 2013[14]
Sweeny et al investigates combinations of respiration ECG and acceleration signals
to detect sleep apnea events. The three signals were recorded using a ”Smartex
Wearable Wellness System (WWS)” chest strap. The ECG signal was recorded at
a sampling rate of 250 Hz, the acceleration signal was recorded using a tri-axial ac-
celerometer with a sampling rate of 25 Hz, and the respiration signal was recorded
using a piezoresistive sensor on the chest, with a sampling frequency of 25 Hz.
Feature extraction was done using the discrete wavelet transform (DWT) with a
Daubechies 5 mother wavelet. A decision tree was used in this work to perform the
classiﬁcation of apnea/non-apnea. Using all three signals achieved a classiﬁcation
accuracy of 91.2%, but including the respiration features only improved the classi-
ﬁcation accuracy of 1.1%. The accelerometer provides the best result for a single
sensor to classify data, however, more accurate results are available if features from
ECG data is included in the analysis. They concluded that the proposed system
provides a high accuracy for detecting apnea events and that eventually, the full
PSG system will be redundant.
”Automated Recognition of Obstructive Sleep Apnea Syndrome Using
Support Vector Machine Classiﬁer”, Al-Angari et al, IEEE Trans Inf
Technol Biomed, 2015[15]
The two engineers from Northwestern University proposed a support vector ma-
chine (SVM) classiﬁer with a linear and a second-order polynomial kernel to predict
OSA. The goal of the study was to evaluate classiﬁcation of whole-night normal
15
G Pervasive Technology Study
223
==PAGE:238==
January 23, 2018
and apneic epochs using features from the phase and magnitude of the respira-
tory eﬀorts signals compared and combined with other features from heart rate
variability (HRV) and oxygen saturation signals.
It was based on the physiological fact that during normal breathing the abdominal
and thoracic eﬀorts happen simultaneously, but during OSA syndrome the phase
diﬀerence between the abdominal and thoracic movement increases, because of
the airway being obstructed, and at total obstruction, these two signals get into
counterphase in a situation called paradoxical breathing. This is due to the fact
that, during an obstructive apnea, the air volume in the lung remains constant
but the motion of the diaphragm continues.
A one-minute classiﬁcation and a subject classiﬁcation was made for 100 subjects,
50 with OSA and 50 control patients from the Sleep Heart Health Study database.
The one-minute classiﬁcation, the respiratory features had the highest sensitivity
while the oxygen saturation gave the highest speciﬁcity. The polynomial kernel
always had better performance and the highest accuracy of 82.4% (Sen: 69.9%,
Spec: 91.4%) was achieved using the combined-feature classiﬁer.
The subjection classiﬁcation, the polynomial kernel had a clear improvement in
the oxygen saturation accuracy as the highest accuracy of 95% was achieved by
both the oxygen saturation (Sen: 100%, Spec: 90.2%) and the combined-feature
(Sen: 91.8%, Spec: 98.0%).
Further analysis of the SVM with diﬀerent kernels is suggested to optimize the
classiﬁer for an OSA automated detection algorithm.
”SleepAp: An Automated Obstructive Sleep Apnoea Screening Appli-
cation for Smartphones”, Behar et al, IEEE Journal of Biomedical and
Health Informatics, 2015[16]
Behar et al made a prototype phone application for screening of patients with
OSA. A SVM classiﬁer was used to classify subjects as having moderate or severe
OSA versus being healthy. Features were derived from audio, actigraphy, photo-
plethysmography (PPG), and demographics as input to the SVM. The classiﬁer
was trained on 735 patients with close to 50% healthy and 50% patients with
OSA respectively. The best results were obtained using a combination of features,
having an accuracy of 92.3%. It was concluded that the simple home-use system
would provide a good generic framework for health screening in low-resource envi-
ronments. The application provides a good estimation of whether a patient should
get further analysis in a hospital for OSA.
16
G Pervasive Technology Study
224
==PAGE:239==
January 23, 2018
”Automatic detection of respiratory arrests in OSA patients using PPG
and machine learning techniques”, Ucar et al, The Natural Computing
Applications Forum, 2016[17]
The aim of this study was to determine the connection between the respiratory
arrests and the PPG signal in OSA patients. Using the Mann-Whitney U test 32
out of the 34 time-domain features were found to be statistically signiﬁcant. These
32 features were classiﬁed with k-nearest neighbors classiﬁcation algorithm, radial
basis function neural network, probabilistic neural network, multilayer feedforward
neural network (MLFFNN) and ensemble classiﬁcation method.
The database used in the study was formed in the Sleep Laboratory of Sakarya
Hendek State Hospital, Department of Chest Diseases. The data consists of PPG
signal sampled at a frequency of 128 Hz from 5 individuals during 7-8 hours of
sleep.
Figure 2.2 shows the sampled PPG signal after being bandpass ﬁltered in the
time-domain. The diﬀerence between the apnea and control group is easily dis-
tinguishable.
The purple and yellow line shows the averages of the maximum
amplitudes for the control and apnea group respectively.
Figure 2.2: Sample PPG signal after being bandpass ﬁltered [17].
The best performance was obtained with the MLFFNN with an accuracy of 97.07%
and it was concluded that respiratory arrests can be recognized through the PPG
signal hence the PPG signal can be used for the diagnosis of OSA.
”Multimodal Classiﬁcation of Obstructive Sleep Apnea using Feature
Level Fusion”, Memis et al, IEEE 11th International Conference on
Semantic Computing, 2017[18]
In this paper three diﬀerent classiﬁers are proposed, Naive Bayes (NB), SVM and
k-nearest neighbor (kNN) with a multi-modal approach that performs feature-level
fusion of two physiological signals, SpO2 and ECG for eﬃcient OSA classiﬁcation.
17
G Pervasive Technology Study
225
==PAGE:240==
January 23, 2018
The dataset from PhysioNet consists of 70 records, 35 records divided into train-
ing and testing set respectively. For the SVM kernel radial basis function is used,
furthermore, a grid search algorithm to optimize the kernel parameters is used.
The patients are divided into categories of same severity for a better analysis. The
best classiﬁcations where achieved with SVM, with an average accuracy of 97.64%
for all test scenarios (severity’s).
2.5
Hearables
”Hearables: Multimodal physiological in-ear sensing”, Goverdovsky et
al, Scientiﬁc Reports, 2017[19]
In this study, Goverdovsky et al developed a multimodal physiological in-ear device
to track the neural and physiological functions of a user over long periods of time.
The earpiece beneﬁts from being in a relatively stable position of the ear canal
with respect to vital organs. It is shown that the multimodal sensor can robustly
measure the brain, cardiac and respiratory functions. Furthermore, it is shown
that combining data from multiple sensors within an integrated wearable device
improves both the accuracy of measurements and the ability to deal with artifacts
in real-world scenarios.
2.6
Discussion
In this section, a discussion of the relevance of each disease ﬁeld will be presented
based on the reviewed articles. Each ﬁeld will also be evaluated in terms of similar-
ity between modalities used in the research articles as compared to the modalities
chosen for this study. Studies will also be evaluated based on the current develop-
ment within the ﬁeld as well as performance of event detection.
2.6.1
Respiratory
In this ﬁeld of research, most state of the art and recent relevant studies are
focused on the early detection and prediction of exacerbation events. An exacer-
bation refers to a worsening of disease and an increase in the resulting symptoms.
Providing monitoring and early detection of these exacerbation has a great po-
tential for increasing patient life quality by supporting treatment management.
In addition, this ﬁeld has a great potential for impact based on the large disease
18
G Pervasive Technology Study
226
==PAGE:241==
January 23, 2018
population and growing incidence of disease globally.
Using the modalities heart rate, oxygen saturation, FEV1, BMI, and blood pres-
sure, the authors of Jensen et al, 2012 [5], were able to discriminate between
periods with and without exacerbations within the next 30 days, with a pattern
recognition model which scored a sensitivity level of 70% and a speciﬁcity level
of 95%. This study proved that it is possible to predict and provide support for
COPD patients using personal monitoring devices. In the study from 2014 by the
authors Mohktar et al [7], it was possible to classify COPD patients into ”high
risk” and ”low risk” categories from suﬀering from an exacerbation one day ahead
with a 71.8% classiﬁcation accuracy. Predictions were based on several modalities
including FEV1, oxygen saturation, heart rate, and weight. By analyzing respi-
ratory sounds using a single microphone, the authors of Fernandez-Granero et al,
2015 [8], showed that it was possible to perform early detection of exacerbation
of COPD, having a classiﬁcation accuracy of 75.8% with an early detection of an
average of 5±1.9 days in advance. Many of the studies within this ﬁeld displayed
good results, and have the potential for further development and future manage-
ment systems.
However, within the current ﬁeld of research, few studies are using comparable
modalities to those proposed in the in-ear design. Many studies include data from
spirometry, pulmonary function tests, such as the FEV1, which is an important
measure in disease progression. Other prominent studies within this ﬁeld include
questionnaires, respiratory rate, sound, blood pressure and other sources of signals
which cannot be assessed with the setup developed for this study. In addition,
many of these studies have been done in a time frame largely exceeding that of
a master thesis.
Data collection has to be done over a long time in order to
establish a baseline for detection of exacerbations, which occurs relatively rarely
(2-3 per/year).
2.6.2
Cardiovascular
Within the research of cardiovascular diseases, a lot of recent studies are focused
on the development of algorithms for wearable heart disease monitor and alert sys-
tems. Monitoring of cardiac signals and events have a great potential to prevent
fatal incidents and to allow early treatment of cardiac illnesses. The causes and
types of cardiac events are various, and having a large disease population makes
it ideal to pursue with the purpose of global and big impact disease management.
In a study done by the authors Watanabe et al, 2012[10], the proposed algorithm
were able to perform R-wave detection with a classiﬁcation accuracy of 99%, al-
19
G Pervasive Technology Study
227
==PAGE:242==
January 23, 2018
lowing wireless and mobile detection of heart arrhythmia. In another paper by
the authors of Manikandan et al, 2014[11], they were able to perform QRS de-
tection on arrhythmia database with an average sensitivity of 99.91%. Compared
to previous methods, the proposed algorithm provided increased performance of
detection under when processing noisy associated with wearable cardiac monitors.
Other studies within this ﬁeld, such as Yasin et al, 2017[12], provides a monitoring
system for prediction of the onset of impending critical cardiovascular events. This
can be done 3 hours in advance with an 86% classiﬁcation accuracy. All of the
above-mentioned studies have used previously recorded ECG databases as their
data source.
Almost none of the studies within this ﬁeld, and none of the reviewed studies
use modalities other than ECG in order to perform monitoring of cardiovascular
diseases. Classiﬁcation is usually performed based on ECG-wave features. Most
studies are in early development and have only been concerned with providing
eﬃcient, low-power algorithms for future devices. A lot of studies have been done
using previously recorded and public ECG datasets. Based on this, it is not very
likely that the proposed setup and modalities in this study are suitable for the
monitoring and detection of cardiovascular diseases.
2.6.3
Sleep Apnea
In this ﬁeld of research, most state of the art and recent relevant studies are fo-
cused on the monitoring and detection of obstructive sleep apnea events. Providing
an eﬃcient monitoring system and classiﬁcation of disease severity can be a huge
aid in the treatment and management of obstructive sleep apnea. This area of
research has a great potential, as a lot of cases of sleep apnea remain undiagnosed
worldwide, due to the expensive and impractical limitations of current diagnostic
measures.
In the paper by Almazaydeh et al, 2012 [13], detection of OSA using only one
modality showed a very promising result, achieving an accuracy of 93.3% only
using SpO2 signals with a neural network classiﬁer. Sweeney et al, 2013 [14], in-
vestigated the combination of ECG and acceleration for detection of sleep apnea,
using a body sensor. The authors achieved very promising results scoring an accu-
racy of 91.2% using a decision tree classiﬁer. Furthermore, U¸car et al, 2016 [17],
managed an accuracy of 97.07% using a MLFFNN with features from PPG signals.
Most of the studies within the current research of obstructive sleep apnea have been
performed using many of the same modalities as proposed in this study. The stud-
ies have shown that signals such as PPG, which can be used in order to estimate
20
G Pervasive Technology Study
228
==PAGE:243==
January 23, 2018
heart rate and oxygen saturation, is a very signiﬁcant feature for detection of ob-
structive sleep apnea. In addition, multi-modal studies have shown great promise,
incorporating data from accelerometers, which is also one of the proposed modal-
ities for this study. The systems and classiﬁers described in the reviewed studies
have shown great performance and encourages further study of the research ﬁeld
using an in-ear wearable monitoring system. Combining these proposed modalities
into a single device can promote even greater screening accuracy’s for OSA.
2.6.4
Choice of Disease
Based on the presented discussion of diseases, it is determined that obstructive
sleep apnea is the best choice based on available research and modalities. Arti-
cles within this ﬁeld have shown great promise and capability of detection using
machine learning classiﬁcation algorithms of speciﬁc modalities related to this re-
search. Furthermore, Goverdovsky et al, 2017[19], showed that signals obtained
from in-ear sensors provide high accuracy readings with little to no noise and caus-
ing minimal discomfort of wear with memory foam.
The proposed modalities presented in section 1.2, combined with methods pre-
sented in this chapter, will be used in order to develop a system which is able to
take patient monitoring from the hospitals to the private sphere, providing an easy
and cheap screening of obstructive sleep apnea, without the use of expensive and
time-consuming PSG readings.
21
G Pervasive Technology Study
229
==PAGE:244==
3.
Sleep Apnea
Sleep apnea is a common sleep disorder, which aﬀects people all over the world. It
is characterized by brief interruption of breathing, thereby interrupting the sleep
of the aﬀected person. In people with severe sleep apnea, this can occur hundreds
of times a night. Many people who suﬀer from sleep apnea is not aware of the
condition, and is often alerted to it by their bed-partner caused by symptoms such
as loud snoring.
Continuous sleep deprivation caused by sleep apnea can lead to a serious decline
of physical and mental health [20].
3.1
Types
There are three main types of sleep apnea, which are listed below.
1. Obstructive sleep apnea: The most common type of sleep apnea. Caused
by blockage of the throat and obstruction of airﬂow. Often accompanied by
loud snoring
2. Central sleep apnea: A much less common type of sleep apnea. Involves
the central nervous system, which fails to produce signals to the muscles
which are responsible for breathing. Often not accompanied by snoring
3. Complex sleep apnea:
A combination of obstructive sleep apnea and
central sleep apnea
3.2
Obstructive Sleep Apnea
The most common type of sleep apnea is obstructive sleep apnea, OSA, which will
be the focus of this study. Most of the sleep apnea cases are currently undiagnosed
because of the practical limitations of polysomnography (PSG) and the expenses
involved. In this section, a brief overview of OSA will be presented.
22
G Pervasive Technology Study
230
==PAGE:245==
January 23, 2018
3.2.1
Etiology
The causes of obstructive sleep apnea are various and are very patient speciﬁc.
Some can be due to hormone alterations during sleep, others may be due to
anatomic abnormalities around the neck area.
However, the risk factors listed
below are thought to increase the chance of having obstructive sleep apnea in the
general population [20].
• Overweight
• Male
• Family history of sleep apnea
• Smoking
• High blood pressure
• Neck circumference greater than 40 cm
In addition, other factors have also been known to increase the risk of having ob-
structive sleep apnea. These include a receding chin, enlarged tonsils or adenoids,
increased relaxation of throat muscles, and allergies, or other medical condition
which can cause blockage and congestion.
3.2.2
Pathophysiology
In obstructive sleep apnea, breathing is repeatedly interrupted caused by obstruc-
tion and collapse of the pharynx within the upper airway. This happens because
of an increased relaxation of the throat during sleep. The blockage can cause loud
snoring and gasp for air. Anatomical and neuromuscular irregularities or disorders
are often associated with the underlying pathophysiology[21]. An example of an
obstructed airway can be seen in ﬁgure 3.1 below.
23
G Pervasive Technology Study
231
==PAGE:246==
January 23, 2018
Figure 3.1: Anatomical diﬀerence comparing non-obstructed and obstructed air-
way [22].
OSA is characterized by an obstruction of airﬂow which causes a person to stop
breathing for 10 seconds or more during sleep. Repeated incidents causes systemic
oxygen saturation to drop and a change in heart rate, both of which are detectable.
The continuous sleep deprivation in more severe cases of OSA can lead to seri-
ous physical and mental disorders if left untreated. Short-term symptoms include
daytime sleepiness, poor concentration, and mental instability. Long-term symp-
toms are not well established yet but are thought to include the development of
diabetes, heart diseases, liver problems and neurological disorders [1].
3.2.3
Classiﬁcation and Prevalence
The severity of sleep apnea is measured using the apnea-hypopnea index (AHI),
which is the number of apneas and hypopneas per hour of sleep. OSA is deﬁned
when AHI is ≥5, when it is accompanied by daytime sleepiness it is referred to as
OSA syndrome. The prevalence of OSA of an AHI ≥5 was 22% in men and 17% in
women in eleven published epidemiological studied, published between 1993 and
2013. OSA is more prevalent in men than women, age and obesity increase the
risk. Smoking and alcohol might also be risk factors, but results are conﬂicting
[23].
3.2.4
Signs & Symptoms
Signs and symptoms of obstructive sleep apnea may vary from person to person.
However, it is thought that the major signs include the following [20].
24
G Pervasive Technology Study
232
==PAGE:247==
January 23, 2018
• Loud, chronic snoring
• Snorting, gasping for air
• Waking up with dyspnea
• Daytime sleepiness and fatigue
• Diﬃculty concentrating and headaches
Obstructive sleep apnea is also followed by other symptoms such as insomnia, fre-
quent nightly urination, and mental instability.
Diagnosis of obstructive sleep apnea is usually done by polysomnography, PSG,
recordings performed at a hospital or clinic. Treatment of severe obstructive sleep
apnea usually consists of sleeping with a CPAP device, which allows continuous
positive airway pressure to be applied to force airways open and ensure normal
breathing. In addition, treatment and reduction of risk factors and causes must
be pursued if possible.
3.2.5
Polysomnography
Polysomnography(PSG) is the golden standard method for detection of sleep ap-
nea and is generally used in sleep studies. A standard PSG device records elec-
troencephalography (EEG), electrooculography (EOG), electromyography (EMG),
ECG, nasal and oral airﬂow, pulse oximetry, snoring, and chest wall- and upper
abdominal wall movement. This is a cumbersome and expensive process, normally
with long wait times, which requires the patient to stay in an overnight sleep-
lap for the recording to happen throughout the night, after which, the trained
physician has to look through the data of all the signals in order to diagnose the
patient.
25
G Pervasive Technology Study
233
==PAGE:248==
4.
System Design
In this chapter, an overview of the selected sensors and prototype development
for the system design will be given. All the components and hardware listed in
this section is a part of the preliminary prototype design and might be subject to
change in subsequent designs.
4.1
Components
Below is the preliminary parts list for the proposed prototype design. Parts and
prototype design have been selected and developed at Oticon A/S.
• Micro controller board:
Adafruit M0 feather
• Accelerometer:
InvenSense MPU6500
• Optical:
Maxim MAX30101
• Thermometer:
NXP PCT220
• Battery:
Lithium Ion Polymer Battery - 3.7v 1200mAh
• Micro SD: 4-16 GB
4.2
Prototype
The proposed prototype is based on the design of regular hearing aid with added
modalities. The Maxim PPG sensor is attached to the in-ear piece, which also
includes a temperature gauge. In the proposed design, the in-ear piece is connected
to an external tri-axial accelerometer attached to the chest, in a 3D-printed box
with a battery. An image of the actual in-ear prototype can be seen in ﬁgure 4.1.
26
G Pervasive Technology Study
234
==PAGE:249==
January 23, 2018
Figure 4.1: Prototype for the in-ear piece of proposed device.
The prototype has to be able to record a full night sleep of around 10 hours, while
not being prone to noise from the users moving around during the night. It is also
required that the device looks and could function as a normal hearing aid, as that
is the main function of the device.
The hearing aid is connected to the chest-box with a detachable wire for easy
swapping if the circuit should break during testing, setup or recording of a sub-
ject. An SD-card will be placed next to the microcontroller, Adafruit M0 feather,
for data storage. Furthermore, a Bluetooth module has been looked into for live
testing of data, but is not included in the current prototype
The aim of prototype development is to design a device which is capable of ac-
curately monitoring and detecting sleep apnea with a prototype system closely
resembling an original functional hearing aid.
4.3
Speciﬁcations
After thorough study, a combination of methods and signals based on the state of
the art study were used in order to select the appropriate sensors for the diﬀerent
modalities presented in this prototype.

January 23, 2018
All the design speciﬁcations of the prototype are shown in the block diagram in
ﬁgure 4.2.
Figure 4.2: Block diagram of the proposed design.
4.3.1
Sensors
In table 4.1 are the three sensors included in the prototype. The Maxim MAX30101
is the optical sensor used in this study. It works by illuminating the skin and then
measuring the light intensity with an infrared, green and red wavelength. Accord-
28
G Pervasive Technology Study
236
==PAGE:251==
January 23, 2018
ing to Lee et al [24], the red light is better for accurate readings whereas green
light has the potential to be superior for everyday life due to its relative freedom
from artifacts. In this study both green and red light PPG will be tested.
The tri-axel accelerometer from InvenSense MPU6500 is a high accuracy accelerom-
eter with a high sampling rate. According to several studies reviewed, the ac-
celerometer has a very high accuracy for detection sleep apnea, which is why this
module is added. The PCT2202 thermometer from NXP is an accurate digital
thermometer, this can hopefully provide some additional information and help in
the detection and classiﬁcation of obstructive sleep apnea. In table 4.1, an overview
of the selected sensors can be seen.
Table 4.1: Overview of selected sensors.

5.
Problem Statement & Aims
5.1
Problem Statement
Disease monitoring is of major importance to the public with the general popu-
lation getting older and the increasing prevalence of developing chronic diseases
causing the taxpayers a bigger burden.
Therefore, early detection and proper
monitoring and treatment of these diseases are very crucial. Patients suﬀering
from sleep apnea have been shown to have increased risk of several cardiovascular
diseases, which gets progressively worsened over the years, hence early detection
is critical. Detection of sleep apnea can be cumbersome, because of the diﬃculty
of the golden standard diagnostic method, PSG, most patients are undiagnosed.
Developing a new technique for screening of sleep apnea is crucial, saving the
government a lot of money and decreasing the likelihood of accidents from sleep-
deprived related accidents.
Developing a personal disease monitoring system using an in-ear device can revolu-
tionize the way disease management is supported and evaluation of progression is
made, as well as provide an eﬃcient and inexpensive screening tool. Such systems
can utilize big and continuous data streams in order to improve performance and
perform data analysis on a global scale.
5.2
Problem Aims
The aim of this research is to develop a tri-modal monitoring system for detection
and interpretation of chronic disease vital signs. The system is intended for use
by people who suﬀer from obstructive sleep apnea.
The system will be developed as a prototype, which has the aim of accurately
monitoring and detecting sleep apnea events, using a design, closely resembling
a functioning hearing aid. The modalities used in the study will include photo-
plethysmography, accelerometer, and temperature gauge. The general aims of this
30
G Pervasive Technology Study
238
==PAGE:253==
January 23, 2018
project can be quantiﬁed using the following sub-objectives:
• Design prototype for testing
• Acquire knowledge of signals and processing of data
• Perform detection of events
• Develop machine learning algorithm for classiﬁcation of obstructive sleep
apnea
• To evaluate system performance in terms of:
1. Quality of signal
2. Choice of classiﬁcation algorithm
3. System accuracy, sensitivity, and speciﬁcity
• Create a system for screening and detection of obstructive sleep apnea
• Perform case study of monitoring patients with sleep apnea using the design
prototype.
This study will investigate the possibility of monitoring obstructive sleep apnea
using an in-ear device, as well as assessing the performance of detection and capa-
bilities of the system.

H.1.2
HRalgorithm.m
1 function
[HR_median, time_window]= HRalgorithm(N_min, N_max ,...
2
IRWindowHR , MilisHR, Fs)
3 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
4 % Project: Master Thesis In-Ear Sleep Recorder
5 %
6 % Authors: Rasmus Lund Bendtsen & Nicolai Westergren
7 %
8 % Date: 12-04-2018
9 %
10 % This script is made by the authors of the project.
11 %
12 % Main script for estimating heart rates based on applied windows using
13 % the autocorrelation function
14 %
15 %
Input:
16 %
N_min: Lower boundary of the HR to be estimated
17 %
N_max: Upper boundary of the HR to be estimated
18 %
IRWindowHR: Array of windowed IR signals for HR estimations
19 %
MilisHR: Array of windowed timestamp signals used to correct some
HR
20 %
estimations
21 %
Fs: Sampling frequency of the windowed IR signal data
22 %
23 %
Output:
24 %
HR_median: Heart rate output values computed by the algorithm for
25 %
each window in the input data
26 %
time_window: Total duration of each window computed by the
27 %
timestamps of each window
28 %
29 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
30
31 % Only wanna see 1-sided ACF
32 offset = length(IRWindowHR(:,1));
33 % Loop through entire signal to determine HR
34 for i=1:length(IRWindowHR(1,:)) %Number of windows
35
% Determine variable length of segments in seconds
36
time_window(1,i) = (MilisHR(end,i)-MilisHR(1,i))/1000;
37
% Normalized ACF
38
[r(:,i), lags(:,i)] = xcorr((IRWindowHR(:,i)), 'coeff');
39
% Compute maximal peak of ACF
40
try
41
[pks(:,i),locs(:,i)] = findpeaks(r((offset+N_min...
42
-10:offset+N_max+10),i),lags((offset+N_min -10:offset...
43
+N_max+10),i),'NPeaks',1,'SortStr','descend');
44
catch % To avoid errors, catch using the previous value
45
locs(i) = locs(i-1);
46
if i < 2
47
locs(i) = 100;
48
end
49
end
==PAGE:258==
H.1 Heart Rate Algorithm
244
50
% Determine corrected HR using the time estimated for each window
51
HBR(i) = (60*Fs)/(locs(i)*(time_window(1,i)/2));
52 end
53
54 % Smoothening of estimated signals in order to decrease the influence of
55 % noise
56 % Estimate HR as the mean and median of the last 5 estimates.
57 for i = 1:length(HBR)
58
if i < 6
59
HR_median(i) = HBR(i);
60
else
61
HR_median(i) = median([HBR(i-4) HBR(i-3) HBR(i-2) HBR(i-1) HBR(i)
]);
62
63
end
64 end
65 % MA smoothing of median values
66 HR_median = movmean(HR_median ,4);
67
68 end

bandpassHR.m
1 function [RedHR, IrHR] = bandpassHR(Red, IR, Fs)
2 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
3 % Project: Master Thesis In-Ear Sleep Recorder
4 %
5 % Authors: Rasmus Lund Bendtsen & Nicolai Westergren
6 %
7 % Date: 08-03-2018
8 %
9 % This script is mad by the authors of the project.
10 %
11 % Filter function for the bandpass filtering of PPG signals in
12 % order to perform estimation of heart rate. Returns a discrete-time
filter
13 % object
14 %
15 %
Input:
16 %
Red: Array of raw red LED PPG signal
17 %
IR: Array of raw infared LED PPG signal
18 %
Fs: Sampling frequency of input signals
19 %
20 %
Output:
21 %
RedHR: Array of filtered red LED PPG signal
22 %
IrHR: Array of filtered infared LED PPG signal
23 %
24 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

H.2 SpO2 Algorithm
245
26
27 % Lower cutoff frequency
28 Fcp_low=0.5;
29 % Higher cutoff frequency
30 Fcp_high=5;

33 % Create filter
34 [sos,g]=zp2sos(o,p,k);
35 % Apply filtering to each of the PPG signals
36 RedHR = filtfilt(sos, g, Red);
37 IrHR = filtfilt(sos, g, IR);
38 end

detectSpO2ACDC.m
1 function [SpO2, R] = detectSpO2ACDC(ACRed, DCRed, ACIr, DCIr)
2 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
3 % Project: Master Thesis In-Ear Sleep Recorder
4 %
5 % Authors: Rasmus Lund Bendtsen & Nicolai Westergren
6 %
7 % Date: 05-04-2018
8 %
9 % This script is made by the authors of the project.
==PAGE:263==
H.2 SpO2 Algorithm
249
10 %
11 % Function for estimation of oxygen saturation in recorded data. Linear
12 % function for convertion of R to Spo2 values found by calibration with
13 % reference data.
14 %
15 %
Input:
16 %
ACRed: AC component of the red LED PPG signal
17 %
DCRed: DC component of the red LED PPG signal
18 %
ACIr: AC component of the infared LED PPG signal
19 %
DCIr: DC component of the infared LED PPG signal
20 %
21 %
Output:
22 %
SpO2: SpO2 value computed by the algorithm based on the AC and DC
23 %
components of the red and infared input signal
24 %
R: R value computed by the algorithm based on the AC and DC
25 %
components of the red and infared input signal
26 %
27 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
28
29 % AC - DC components
30 ACDCRed = ACRed./DCRed; % AC/DC for Red LED
31 ACDCIr = ACIr./DCIr; % AC/DC for Infared LED
32 % Root mean square of each ACDC components
33 Red = rms(ACDCRed);
34 IR = rms(ACDCIr);
35 % Determine R value as the ratio between AC/DC components of Red and IR
36 R = Red/IR;
37 % Use SpO2 calibration curve to convert R values to SpO2 readings
38 SpO2 = 105.1860564975580-19.512878725417730*R;
39 end

1 function [coeffs] = calibrateSpO2(x,y)
2 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
3 % Project: Master Thesis In-Ear Sleep Recorder
4 %
5 % Authors: Rasmus Lund Bendtsen & Nicolai Westergren
6 %
7 % Date: 16-04-2018
8 %
9 % This script is made by the authors of the project.
10 %
11 % Function for calibration of R to SpO2 curve using computed R ratios and
12 % oxygen saturation values from reference device. Returns coefficients
for
13 % the linear relationship between the values. Displays the R and
reference
14 % values along with the fitted line.
==PAGE:264==
H.2 SpO2 Algorithm
250
15 %
16 %
Input:
17 %
x: R value computed by the detectSpO2ACDC algorithm developed for
18 %
SpO2 estimations in this study.
19 %
y: Oxygen saturation value given by reference equipment recorded
20 %
simultanously.
21 %
22 %
Output:
23 %
coeffs: Linear coefficients for the relationship between R and
24 %
reference values given by the polyfit MATLAB function.
25 %
26 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
27
28 figure(1)
29 % Plot R vs reference SpO2
30 plot(x, y, 'bo');
31 % Determine linear coefficients using polyfit
32 coeffs = polyfit(x, y, 1);
33 % Get fitted values
34 fittedX = linspace(min(x), max(x), length(x));
35 fittedY = polyval(coeffs, fittedX);
36 % Plot the fitted line
37 hold on;
38 plot(fittedX, fittedY, 'r--', 'LineWidth', 3);
39 % hold off
40 legend('Data points', 'Fitted line')
41 title('R to SpO_2 Calibration')
42 ylabel('SpO_2 [%]')
43 xlabel('R Ratio')
44 shg
45 end

detectRespRate.m
1 function [respRate] = detectRespRate(input, Fs)
2 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
3 % Project: Master Thesis In-Ear Sleep Recorder
4 %
5 % Authors: Rasmus Lund Bendtsen & Nicolai Westergren
6 %
7 % Date: 05-06-2018
8 %
9 % This script is made by the authors of the project.
10 %
11 % Function that computes the respiration rate for windowed signals using
12 % the frequnecy at the maximum of the PSD
13
14 %
Input:
15 %
input: Array containing windowed accelerometer signal
16 %
Fs: Sampple frequency of the windowed data
17 %
18 %
Output:
19 %
respRate: Estimated respiratory rate array computed by the
algorithm
20 %
21 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
22
23 % Compute PSD using the MATLAB function pwelch
24 [pxx,f] = pwelch(input,length(input(:,1)),[],length(input(:,1)), Fs);
==PAGE:270==
H.3 Respiration Rate Algorithm
256
25 % Find index of maximum peak
26 [M,I] = max(pxx);
27 % Determine frequency at maximum peak
28 resp_freq = f(I);
29 % Convert frequency into breaths per minute
30 respRate = resp_freq*60;
31 end

H.4.2
featSQI.m
1 function FeaturesAll = featSQI(WindowPPG, accX_win, accY_win, accZ_win
,...
2
Fs, label)
3 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
4 % Project: Master Thesis In-Ear Sleep Recorder
5 %
6 % Authors: Rasmus Lund Bendtsen & Nicolai Westergren
7 %
8 % Date: 05-06-2018
9 %
10 % This script is made by the authors of the project.
11 %
12 % Function that computes the features of the PPG and Accelerometer
signals.
13 % Features used as signal quality indices and used in classification for
14 % shutdown algorithm.
15
16 %
Input:
==PAGE:274==
H.4 Shutdown & SQI Features
260
17 %
WindowPPG: Array containing windowed PPG signal belonging to
either
18 %
signal group
19 %
accX_win: Array containing windowed x-axis accelerometer signal
20 %
belonging to either signal group
21 %
accY_win: Array containing windowed Y-axis accelerometer signal
22 %
belonging to either signal group
23 %
accZ_win: Array containing windowed Z-axis accelerometer signal
24 %
belonging to either signal group
25 %
Fs: Sampple frequency of the windowed data
26 %
label: Label data according to number, 0 = Clean, 1 = Noise
27 %
28 %
Output:
29 %
trainedClassifier: Matrix containing all of the PPG and
30 %
Accelerometer SWI features along with the correct labels
31 %
32 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
33
34 % Estimation of all PPG SQI features %
35 for i = 1:length(WindowPPG(1,:))
36
% Standard deviation and mean - Red LED
37
Sd_SQI(i) = std(WindowPPG(:,i));
38
Mean_SQI(i) = mean(WindowPPG(:,i));
39
% Perfusion SQI
40
P_SQI(i) = ((max(WindowPPG(:,i))-min(WindowPPG(:,i)))...
41
/mean(WindowPPG(:,i)))*100;
42
% Skewness SQI
43
S_SQI(i) = (1/length(WindowPPG(:,i)))*sum(power(WindowPPG(:,i)...
44
-(mean(WindowPPG(:,i))/std(WindowPPG(:,i))),3));
45
% Kurtosis SQI
46
K_SQI(i) = (1/length(WindowPPG(:,i)))*sum(power(WindowPPG(:,i)...
47
-(mean(WindowPPG(:,i))/std(WindowPPG(:,i))),4));
48
% Entropy SQI
49
E_SQI(i) = -sum(power(WindowPPG(:,i),2).*log(power(WindowPPG(:,i),2))
);
50
% P-Welch SQI - Relative power SQI
51
[pxx,f] = pwelch(WindowPPG(:,i),200,[],200,Fs);
52
P_sys = bandpower(pxx,f,[1 2.25],'psd');
53
P_rest = bandpower(pxx,f,[0 8],'psd');
54
R_SQI(i) = P_sys/P_rest;
55
% P-Welch SNR
56
P_noise = bandpower(pxx,f,[5 50],'psd'); % Signal of interest (0.5-5
Hz)
57
P_signal = bandpower(pxx,f,[0.5 5],'psd'); % Signal of MA (5-49 Hz)
58
SNR_SQI(i) = 10*log(P_signal/(P_noise));
59
% Cross-correlation
60
if i > 1
61
XCORR_SQI(:,i) = corr(WindowPPG(:,i), WindowPPG(:,i-1));
62
end
63 end
64 % Estimation of all Accelerometer SQI features %
65 for i=1:length(accX_win(1,:))
==PAGE:275==
H.4 Shutdown & SQI Features
261
66
% Total acceleration all axes
67
accTot(i) = sum(sum([accX_win(:,i), accY_win(:,i), accZ_win(:,i)]));
68
% Standard deviation of each axis
69
spreadX(i) = std(accX_win(:,i));
70
spreadY(i) = std(accY_win(:,i));
71
spreadZ(i) = std(accZ_win(:,i));
72
% Total standard deviation of all axes
73
totSpread(i) = sum([spreadX(:,i) spreadY(:,i) spreadZ(:,i)]);
74
% Mean of each axis
75
meanX(i) = mean(accX_win(:,i));
76
meanY(i) = mean(accY_win(:,i));
77
meanZ(i) = mean(accZ_win(:,i));
78
% Total mean of all axes
79
totMean(i) = sum([meanX(:,i), meanY(:,i), meanZ(:,i)]);
80 end
81

84
;XCORR_SQI;accTot;totSpread;totMean];

featLabels = zeros(1,length(Features(1,:)));
91 end

94 end

{1,k}(:,i)))/var(ppgWindow{1,k}(:,i)))^2^2);%Hjort parameter

H.5.7
approx entropy.m
1 function [apen] = approx_entropy(n,r,a)
2 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
3 % Project: Master Thesis In-Ear Sleep Recorder
4 %
5 % Author: Avinash Parnandi, parnandi@usc.edu,
6 % http://robotics.usc.edu/~parnandi/
7 % concept boorowed from http://www.physionet.org/physiotools/ApEn/
8 %
9 % This script is edited the authors of the project.
10 %
11 % Code for computing approximate entropy for a time series: Approximate
12 % Entropy is a measure of complexity. It quantifies the unpredictability
of
13 % fluctuations in a time series
14 %
15 %
Input:
16 %
n: Length of the window, which should be considered in each
17 %
iteration
18 %
r: Measure of distance between the elements
19 %
a: Data vector
20 %
21 %
Output:
22 %
apen: Approximate entropy computed by the algorithm
23 %
24 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
25 % Specify data
26 data =a;
27
28 for m=n:n+1; % run it twice, with window size differing by 1
29
30
set = 0;
31
count = 0;
32
counter = 0;
==PAGE:296==
H.5 MESA database & Feature Extraction
282
33
window_correlation = zeros(1,(length(data)-m+1));
34
35
for i=1:(length(data))-m+1,
36
current_window = data(i:i+m-1); % current window stores the
sequence to be compared with other sequences
37
38
for j=1:length(data)-m+1,
39
sliding_window = data(j:j+m-1); % get a window for
comparision with the current_window
40
41
% compare two windows, element by element
42
% can also use some kind of norm measure; that will perform
better
43
for k=1:m,
44
if((abs(current_window(k)-sliding_window(k))>r) && set ==
0)
45
set = 1; % i.e. the difference between the two
sequence is greater than the given value
46
end
47
end
48
if(set==0)
49
count = count+1; % this measures how many sliding_windows
are similar to the current_window
50
end
51
set = 0; % reseting 'set'
52
53
end
54
counter(i)=count/(length(data)-m+1); % we need the number of
similar windows for every cuurent_window
55
count=0;
56
i;
57
end
%
for i=1:(length(data))-m+1, ends here
58
59
60
counter;
% this tells how many similar windows are present for each
window of length m
61
%total_similar_windows = sum(counter);
62
63
%window_correlation = counter/(length(data)-m+1);
64
correlation(m-n+1) = ((sum(counter))/(length(data)-m+1));
65
66
67 end % for m=n:n+1; % run it twice
68 correlation(1);
69 correlation(2);
70 apen = log(correlation(1)/correlation(2));
71 end

H.6.2
NNadvanced.m
1 function [y, e, tr, net] = NNadvanced(X, Y, trainFunc, Nhidden ,...
2
performFunc)
3 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
4 % Project: Master Thesis In-Ear Sleep Recorder
5 %
6 % Authors: Rasmus Lund Bendtsen & Nicolai Westergren
7 %
8 % Date: 28-04-2018
9 %
10 % This script is made by the authors of the project.
11 %
12 % Function for creating a neural network based on the specified training
13 % function, performance function and number of hidden layers. The
function
14 % returns the trained neural network along with label and error estimates
.
15 %
16 %
Input:
17 %
X: Matrix input training data
18 %
Y: Input target data
19 %
trainFunc: Training function for the specified neural network
based
20 %
on the available MATLAB functions
21 %
Nhidden: Number of hidden layers for the specified neural network
22 %
performFunc: Performance function for the specified neural network
23 %
based on the available MATLAB functions
24 %
25 %
Output:
==PAGE:302==
H.6 Classification Algorithms
288
26 %
y: Array of predicted probabilities for each label using the
trained
27 %
model
28 %
e: Array of the estimated error for each label using the trained
29 %
model
30 %
tr: Structure containing all of the model parameters and results
31 %
net: Neural network model
32 %
33 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
34 x = X'; % Input data.
35 t = Y'; % Target data
36
37 % Choose a Training Function
38 trainFcn = trainFunc;
39 % Create a Pattern Recognition Network
40 hiddenLayerSize =Nhidden;
41 net = patternnet(hiddenLayerSize , trainFcn);
42
43 % Choose Input and Output Pre/Post-Processing Functions
44 % For a list of all processing functions type: help nnprocess
45 net.input.processFcns = {'removeconstantrows','mapminmax'};
46
47 % Setup Division of Data for Training, Validation , Testing
48 % For a list of all data division functions type: help nndivision
49 net.divideFcn = 'dividerand';
% Divide data randomly
50 net.divideMode = 'sample';
% Divide up every sample
51 net.divideParam.trainRatio = 70/100; % 70% train data
52 net.divideParam.valRatio = 15/100; % % 15% validation data
53 net.divideParam.testRatio = 15/100; % % 15% test data
54
55 % Choose a Performance Function
56 net.performFcn = performFunc;
57
58 % Choose Plot Functions
59 % For a list of all plot functions type: help nnplot
60 net.plotFcns = {'plotperform','plottrainstate','ploterrhist', ...
61
'plotconfusion', 'plotroc'};
62
63 % Train the Network
64 [net,tr] = train(net,x,t);
65
66 % Test the Network
67 y = net(x); % Perform predictions
68 e = gsubtract(t,y); % Estimate error
69 performance = perform(net,t,y); % Determine performance
70 tind = vec2ind(t); % Real label indices
71 yind = vec2ind(y); % Predicted label indices
72 percentErrors = sum(tind ~= yind)/numel(tind); % Total error in
percentage
73 end

4 % Project: Master Thesis In-Ear Sleep Recorder

18 %

19 %

20 %
endRow: End row of number of rows to load for each file
21 %
22 %
Output:
23 %
dataFeat: Matrix containing all of the imported rows from a file
24 %
loaded into MATLAB
25 %
26 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
27
28 % Initialize variables.
29 delimiter = ' ';
30 % If only filename is specified
31 if nargin <=2
==PAGE:312==
H.7 Postprocessing & Statistics
298
32
startRow = 2;
33
endRow = inf;
34 end
35
36 % Format for each line of text:
37 formatSpec = '%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%
f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%f%[^\n
\r]';
38 % Open the text file.
39 fileID = fopen(filename,'r');
40
41 % Read columns of data according to the format.
42 % This call is based on the structure of the file used to generate this
43 % code.
44 dataArray = textscan(fileID, formatSpec, endRow(1)-startRow(1)+1,...
45
'Delimiter', delimiter, 'MultipleDelimsAsOne', true, 'TextType',...
46
'string', 'EmptyValue', NaN, 'HeaderLines', startRow(1)-1,...
47
'ReturnOnError', false, 'EndOfLine', '\r\n');
48 for block=2:length(startRow)
49
frewind(fileID);
50
dataArrayBlock = textscan(fileID, formatSpec , endRow(block)...
51
-startRow(block)+1, 'Delimiter', delimiter ,...
52
'MultipleDelimsAsOne', true, 'TextType', 'string',...
53
'EmptyValue', NaN, 'HeaderLines', startRow(block)-1,...
54
'ReturnOnError', false, 'EndOfLine', '\r\n');

57
end
58 end
59

61 fclose(fileID);
62

64 dataFeat = [dataArray{1:end-1}];

H.7.1
stat scores.m
1 function [accuracy, F1_score, precision, sensitivity , specificity ,...
2
TP, TN, FN, FP, kappa] = stat_scores(Y_train, Yfit)
3 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
4 % Project: Master Thesis In-Ear Sleep Recorder
5 %
6 % Authors: Rasmus Lund Bendtsen & Nicolai Westergren
7 %
==PAGE:313==
H.7 Postprocessing & Statistics
299
8 % Date: 16-05-2018
9 %
10 % This script is made by the authors of the project.
11 %
12 % Function for computations of performance stats of a given
classification
13 % method using test and estimated labels
14 %
15 %
Input:
16 %
Y_train: Training or test labels annotated by a trained physician
17 %
Yfit: Training or test label estimates computed by the
18 %
classification algorithm
19 %
20 %
Output:
21 %
accuracy: Accuracy of classification
22 %
F1_score: F1 score of classification
23 %
precision: Precision of classification
24 %
sensitivity: Sensitivity of classification
25 %
specificity: Specificity of classification
26 %
TP: True positives of classification
27 %
TN: True negatives of classification
28 %
FN: False negatives of classification
29 %
FP: False positives of classification
30 %
kappa: Kappa value of classification
31 %
32 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
33 P = sum(Y_train); % Sum of actual labels
34 N = length(Y_train)-P; % Number of non-apnea labels
35 % Initiate variables for counting TP, TN, FP and FN
36 TP = 0;
37 TN = 0;
38 FP = 0;
39 FN = 0;
40 % Compute variables for total length of labels
41 for i=1:length(Y_train)
42
%Y_train is real values - Yfit is predictions
43
if Yfit(i,1) == 1 && Yfit(i,1)==Y_train(i,1)
44
TP = TP+1;
45
elseif Yfit(i,1) == 0 && Yfit(i,1)==Y_train(i,1)
46
TN = TN+1;
47
elseif Y_train(i,1)==0 && Yfit(i,1)==1
48
FP = FP+1;
49
elseif Y_train(i,1)==1 && Yfit(i,1)==0
50
FN = FN+1;
51
end
52 end
53
54 accuracy = (TP+TN)/(TP+TN+FP+FN); % Compute accuracy
55 F1_score = 2*TP/(2*TP+FP+FN); % Compute F1 score
56 precision = TP/(TP+FP); % Compute precision
57 sensitivity = TP/(TP+FN) % Compute sensitivity
58 specificity = TN/(TN+FP) % Compute specificity
==PAGE:314==
H.7 Postprocessing & Statistics
300
59
60 % Variables for kappa value estimation
61 pYes = ((TP+FN)/(TP+TN+FP+FN))*((TP+FP)/(TP+TN+FP+FN));
62 pNo = ((FP+TN)/(TP+TN+FP+FN))*((FN+TN)/(TP+TN+FP+FN));
63 p_E = pYes+pNo;
64 % Compute kappa value
65 kappa = (accuracy-p_E)/(1-p_E);
66 end

labelRestraint.m
1 function labelTemp = labelRestraint(est_label)
2 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
3 % Project: Master Thesis In-Ear Sleep Recorder
4 %
5 % Authors: Rasmus Lund Bendtsen & Nicolai Westergren
6 %
7 % Date: 22-05-2018
8 %
9 % This script is made by the authors of the project.
10 %
11 % Function is responsible for applying constraints on label estimation.
12 % Optimizes performance of detection by removing single apnea labels and
13 % single non-apnea labels if labels on both sides of the current label
have
==PAGE:317==
H.8 Miscellaneous
303
14 % opposite labels.
15 %
16 %
Input:
17 %
est_label: Estimated labels by a given classification model
18 %
19 %
Output:
20 %
labelTemp: Updated labels after applying constraints
21 %
22 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
23 % Preallocating new seizure vector
24 labelTemp = zeros(length(est_label),1);
25 % Loop through all labels estimates
26 for j = 2:length(labelTemp)-1
27
count = 0;
28
% Determine numbers of previous labels
29
if est_label(j) == 1 && or(est_label(j+1) == 1, est_label(j-1) == 1)
30
% If temporal constraints are met, label as apnea
31
labelTemp(j) = 1;
32
end
33
if est_label(j) == 0 && est_label(j+1) == 1 && est_label(j-1) == 1
34
% If temporal constraints are met, label a apnea
35
labelTemp(j) = 1;
36
end
37
% If temporal constraints are not met in any case, label is non-apnea
38 end
39
40 end

interpData.m
1 function ipData = interpData(input_data, FsNew, FsOld)
2 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
3 % Project: Master Thesis In-Ear Sleep Recorder
4 %
5 % Authors: Rasmus Lund Bendtsen & Nicolai Westergren
6 %
7 % Date: 10-05-2018
8 %
9 % This script is created by the authors of the project.
10 %
11 % Function that interpolates the data from the current to a new sampling
12 % frequency
13 %
14 %
Input:
15 %
input_data: Array of data which should be interpolated
16 %
FsNew: The desired "new" sampling frequency
17 %
FsOld: The existing "old" sampling frequency
18 %
19 %
Output:
20 %
ipData: Array of data after interpolation of input data
21 %
22 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
23
24 % Length of data to interpolate
25 x = 1:length(input_data);
26 % Data to interpolate
27 v = input_data;
28 % Sample vector
29 xq = 1:FsOld/FsNew:length(input_data); %Add samples (i.e. 25Hz -> 100 Hz)
30 % Interpolation
31 ipData = interp1(x,v,xq);
32 % Flip vector to look like input
33 ipData = ipData';
==PAGE:324==
H.8 Miscellaneous
310
34
35 end
H.8.4
windowData.m
1 function [dataWindow] = windowData(input, windowsize , windowstep , Fs)
2 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
3 % Project: Master Thesis In-Ear Sleep Recorder
4 %
5 % Authors: Rasmus Lund Bendtsen & Nicolai Westergren
6 %
7 % Date: 10-03-2018
8 %
9 % This script is made by the authors of the project.
10 %
11 % Function that windows input data based on specified length and window
12 % step size
13
14 %
Input:
15 %
input: Input data to perform windowing on
16 %
windowsize: Size of each window in seconds
17 %
windowstep: Step size between each window in seconds
18 %
Fs: Sampling frequency of input data
19
20 %
Output:
21 %
dataWindow: Array of the windowed data
22
23 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
24 L = length(input); % Length of signal
25 w_t = windowsize; % Window size (in seconds)
26 w_s = w_t*Fs; % Window size (in samples)
27 w_t_step = windowstep; % Window step size (in seconds)
28 w_s_step = w_t_step*Fs; % Window step size (in samples)
29 count = 0;
30 % Perform windowing on the entire length of the input signal
31 for i = w_s:w_s_step:L
32
count = count+1;
33
dataWindow(:,count) = input(i-w_s+1:i);
34 end
35
36 end

1 function [means,diffs,meanDiff,CR,linFit] = BlandAltman(var1, var2, flag)
2 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
3 % Project: In-Ear SmartRITE Sleep Recorder
==PAGE:325==
H.8 Miscellaneous
311
4 %
5 % Authors: Rasmus Lund Bendtsen & Nicolai Westergren
6 %
7 % Date: 05-04-2018
8 %
9 % This script is edited by the authors of the project.
10 % Function created by David Cumin
11 % Downloaded from
MATLAB - Mathworks
12 % https://se.mathworks.com/matlabcentral/fileexchange/24645-bland-altman-
plot?focused=5124718&tab=function
13 % Script for performing Band Altman calculations on 2 datasets
14 %
15 %
Input:
16 %
var1 and var2: vectors of the measurements
17 %
flag: how much you want to plot
18 %
0 = no plot
19 %
1 = just the data
20 %
2 = data and the difference and CR lines
21 %
3 = above and a linear fit
22 %
23 %
Output:
24 %
means: The means of the data
25 %

26 %

27 %

28 %
linfit: The paramters for the linear fit
29 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
30

33
%%%Use test data
34
disp('This function needs two datasets')
35 end
36 % If no flag is set, default 0
37 if nargin==2
38
flag = 0;
39 end
40 % Compute means
41 means = mean([var1;var2]);
42 % Compute differences
43 diffs = var1-var2;
44 % Mean difference
45 meanDiff = mean(diffs);
46 % SD of differences
47 sdDiff = std(diffs);
48 % 2SD confidence limits - 95% confidence range
49 CR = [meanDiff + 1.96 * sdDiff, meanDiff - 1.96 * sdDiff];
50 % Linear fit coefficients
51 linFit = polyfit(means,diffs ,1);
52 %%%plot results unless flag is 0
53 if flag ~= 0
54
plot(means,diffs,'o')
==PAGE:326==
H.8 Miscellaneous
312
55

56

57

58
plot(means, ones(1,length(means)).*CR(2),'r-');%Plot the lower CR
59
plot(means,ones(1,length(means)).*meanDiff,'k-');%Plot zero
60
end
61
if flag > 2
62
plot(means, means.*linFit(1)+linFit(2),'k--');%Plot the linear
fit
63
end
64
title('Bland-Altman Plot')
65
xlabel('Mean of measures')
66
ylabel('Difference')

legend('Data','Upper CI','Lower CI','Mean')

featureSelect.m
1 function nca = featSelect(Xtrain, Xtest, ytrain, ytest, header)
2 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
3 % Project: Master Thesis In-Ear Sleep Recorder
4 %
5 % Authors: Rasmus Lund Bendtsen & Nicolai Westergren
6 %
7 % Date: 14-05-2018
8 %
9 % This script is made by the authors of the project.
10 %
11 % Function for estimation of feature weight for feature selection on a
12 % given dataset in order to reduce amount of data needed for computations
.
13 % Feature importance is computed by the use of Neighborhood Component
14 % analysis (NCA) using the MATLAB function fscnca.
15 %
16 %
Input:
17 %
Xtrain: Training data containing features variables
18 %
Xtest: Test data containing features variables
19 %
ytrain: Training labels annotated by a trained physician
20 %
ytest: Test labels annotated by a trained physician
21 %
header: Header for the feature columns for identification of
feature
22 %
name
23 %
24 %
Output:
25 %
nca: Feature selection model using NCA
26 %
==PAGE:327==
H.8 Miscellaneous
313
27 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
28
29 % Determine if feature selection is necessary
30 % Compute generalization error without fitting.
31 nca = fscnca(Xtrain,ytrain,'FitMethod','none');
32 L_noFit = loss(nca,Xtest,ytest)
33
34 % Compute generalization error with fitting.
35 nca = fscnca(Xtrain,ytrain,'FitMethod','exact','Lambda',0,...
36
'Solver','sgd','Standardize',true);
37 L_Fit = loss(nca,Xtest,ytest)
38
39 % Tune the regularization parameter (lambda) for NCA using
40 % five-fold cross-validation
41 cvp = cvpartition(Y_train,'kfold',5);
42 numvalidsets = cvp.NumTestSets;
43 % Determine loss function for different values of lambda
44 n = length(Y_train);
45 lambdavals = linspace(0,20,20)/n;
46 lossvals = zeros(length(lambdavals),numvalidsets);
47 % Repeat this process for all folds and all values.
48 for i = 1:length(lambdavals)
49
for k = 1:numvalidsets
50
X = X_train(cvp.training(k),:);
51
y = Y_train(cvp.training(k),:);
52
Xvalid = X_train(cvp.test(k),:);
53
yvalid = Y_train(cvp.test(k),:);
54
% Fit nca
55
nca = fscnca(X,y,'FitMethod','exact', ...
56
'Solver','sgd','Lambda',lambdavals(i), ...
57
'IterationLimit',30,'GradientTolerance',1e-4, ...
58
'Standardize',true);
59
% Compute loss
60
lossvals(i,k) = loss(nca,Xvalid,yvalid,'LossFunction',...
61
'classiferror');
62
end
63 end
64
65 % Compute the average loss obtained from the folds for each lambda value.
66 meanloss = mean(lossvals ,2);
67 % Plot the average loss values versus the lambda values.
68 figure()
69 plot(lambdavals,meanloss,'ro-')
70 xlabel('Lambda')
71 ylabel('Loss (MSE)')
72 title('Optimize \lambda')
73 grid on
74 % Find the best lambda value that corresponds to the minimum average loss
.
75 [~,idx] = min(meanloss); % Find the index
76 bestlambda = lambdavals(idx); % Find the best lambda value
77 bestloss = meanloss(idx); % Find best loss
==PAGE:328==
H.8 Miscellaneous
314
78
79 % Fit the nca model on all data using best lambda and plot the feature
80 % weights
81 nca = fscnca(X_train,Y_train,'FitMethod','exact','Solver','sgd',...
82
'Lambda',bestlambda,'Standardize',true,'Verbose',1);
83
84 % Plot the feature weights
85 figure()
86 plot(nca.FeatureWeights ,'ro')
87 hold on
88 xlabel('Feature index')
89 ylabel('Feature weight')
90 h = gca;
91 h.XTickLabel = header(1:60);
92 h.XTickLabelRotation = 45;
93 h.TickLabelInterpreter = 'none';
94 xticks(1:60)
95 title('Neighbourhood Component Analysis')
96
97 % Compute the classification loss using the test set.
98 L = loss(nca,X_test,Y_test)
99 end

194
195 // Defines for the bits, to be able to change
196 // between bit number and binary definition.
197 // By using the bit number , programming the sensor
198 // is like programming the AVR microcontroller.
199 // But instead of using "(1<<X)", or "_BV(X)",
200 // the Arduino "bit(X)" is used.
201 #define MPU6050_D0 0
202 #define MPU6050_D1 1
203 #define MPU6050_D2 2
204 #define MPU6050_D3 3
205 #define MPU6050_D4 4
206 #define MPU6050_D5 5
207 #define MPU6050_D6 6
208 #define MPU6050_D7 7
209
210 // AUX_VDDIO Register
211 #define MPU6050_AUX_VDDIO MPU6050_D7
// I2C high: 1=VDD, 0=VLOGIC
212
213 // CONFIG Register
214 // DLPF is Digital Low Pass Filter for both gyro and accelerometers.
215 // These are the names for the bits.
216 // Use these only with the bit() macro.

MPU6050_D0
218 #define MPU6050_DLPF_CFG1
MPU6050_D1

MPU6050_D2

MPU6050_EXT_SYNC_SET0))

673 // Default I2C address for the MPU -6050 is 0x68.
674 // But only if the AD0 pin is low.
675 // Some sensor boards have AD0 high, and the
676 // I2C address thus becomes 0x69.
677 #define MPU6050_I2C_ADDRESS 0x69
678
679 // Declaring an union for the registers and the axis values.
680 // The byte order does not match the byte order of
681 // the compiler and AVR chip.
==PAGE:343==
I.1 Firmware Pulse Oximetry
329
682 // The AVR chip (on the Arduino board) has the Low Byte
683 // at the lower address.
684 // But the MPU -6050 has a different order: High Byte at
685 // lower address , so that has to be corrected.
686 // The register part "reg" is only used internally ,
687 // and are swapped in code.
688 typedef union accel_t_gyro_union
689 {
690
struct
691
{
692
uint8_t x_accel_h;
693
uint8_t x_accel_l;
694
uint8_t y_accel_h;
695
uint8_t y_accel_l;
696
uint8_t z_accel_h;
697
uint8_t z_accel_l;
698
uint8_t t_h;
699
uint8_t t_l;
700
uint8_t x_gyro_h;
701
uint8_t x_gyro_l;
702
uint8_t y_gyro_h;
703
uint8_t y_gyro_l;
704
uint8_t z_gyro_h;
705
uint8_t z_gyro_l;
706
} reg;
707
struct
708
{
709
int16_t x_accel;
710
int16_t y_accel;
711
int16_t z_accel;
712
int16_t temperature;
713
int16_t x_gyro;
714
int16_t y_gyro;
715
int16_t z_gyro;
716
} value;
717 };

883 //}
I.2
MPU-6050
1 /*
2 ------------------------------------------------------
3 Project: Master Thesis In-Ear Sleep Recorder
4
5 Date: 25-01-2018 to 01-06-2018
6
7 This function is made by the authors of the project & collaborators.
8
9 Function for Motion MPU6050. Read, write and setup the motion sensor
10 using this script.
11
12 ----------------------------------------------------------
13
*/
14 void motion ()
15 {
16
int error;
17
double dT;
18
accel_t_gyro_union accel_t_gyro;
19
20
//
Serial.println(F(""));
21
//
Serial.println(F("MPU -6050"));
22
23
// Read the raw values.
24
// Read 14 bytes at once,
25
// containing acceleration , temperature and gyro.
26
// With the default settings of the MPU-6050,
27
// there is no filter enabled , and the values
28
// are not very stable.
29
error = MPU6050_read (MPU6050_ACCEL_XOUT_H , (uint8_t *) &accel_t_gyro ,
sizeof(accel_t_gyro));
30
//
Serial.print(F("Read accel, temp and gyro, error = "));
31
//
Serial.println(error,DEC);
32
33
34
// Swap all high and low bytes.
35
// After this, the registers values are swapped ,
36
// so the structure name like x_accel_l does no
37
// longer contain the lower byte.
38
uint8_t swap;
==PAGE:348==
I.2 MPU-6050
334
39 #define SWAP(x,y) swap = x; x = y; y = swap
40
41
SWAP (accel_t_gyro.reg.x_accel_h, accel_t_gyro.reg.x_accel_l);
42
SWAP (accel_t_gyro.reg.y_accel_h, accel_t_gyro.reg.y_accel_l);
43
SWAP (accel_t_gyro.reg.z_accel_h, accel_t_gyro.reg.z_accel_l);
44
SWAP (accel_t_gyro.reg.t_h, accel_t_gyro.reg.t_l);
45
SWAP (accel_t_gyro.reg.x_gyro_h, accel_t_gyro.reg.x_gyro_l);
46
SWAP (accel_t_gyro.reg.y_gyro_h, accel_t_gyro.reg.y_gyro_l);
47
SWAP (accel_t_gyro.reg.z_gyro_h, accel_t_gyro.reg.z_gyro_l);
48
49
// Gyroscope readings
50
gyro_x = accel_t_gyro.value.x_accel; //
51
gyro_y = accel_t_gyro.value.y_accel;
52
gyro_z = accel_t_gyro.value.z_accel;
53
54
// Accelerometer readings
55
accel_x = accel_t_gyro.value.x_gyro;
56
accel_y = accel_t_gyro.value.y_gyro;
57
accel_z = accel_t_gyro.value.z_gyro;
58
59 }
60
61
62 // --------------------------------------------------------
63 // MPU6050_read
64 //
65 // This is a common function to read multiple bytes
66 // from an I2C device.
67 //
68 // It uses the boolean parameter for Wire.endTransMission()
69 // to be able to hold or release the I2C-bus.
70 // This is implemented in Arduino 1.0.1.
71 //
72 // Only this function is used to read.

102 // --------------------------------------------------------
103 // MPU6050_write
104 //
105 // This is a common function to write multiple bytes to an I2C device.
106 //
107 // If only a single register is written ,
108 // use the function MPU_6050_write_reg().
109 //
110 // Parameters:
111 //
start : Start address , use a define for the register
112 //
pData : A pointer to the data to write.
113 //
size
: The number of bytes to write.
114 //
115 // If only a single register is written , a pointer
116 // to the data has to be used, and the size is
117 // a single byte:
118 //
int data = 0;
// the data to write
119 //
MPU6050_write (MPU6050_PWR_MGMT_1 , &c, 1);
120 //
121 int MPU6050_write(int start, const uint8_t *pData, int size)
122 {
123
int n, error;
124
125
Wire.beginTransmission(MPU6050_I2C_ADDRESS);
126
n = Wire.write(start);
// write the start address
127
if (n != 1)
128
return (-20);
129
130
n = Wire.write(pData, size);
// write data bytes
131
if (n != size)
132
return (-21);
133
134
error = Wire.endTransmission(true); // release the I2C-bus
135
if (error != 0)
136
return (error);
137
138
return (0);
// return : no error
139 }
